Diyah for Muslim Prisoners in Dar al-Harb: The 'Ismah Distinction
Intra-Hanafi dispute on diyah for prisoners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between 'ismah muqawwamah and 'ismah mu'aththimah and apply it to determine diyah and kaffarah obligations when a Muslim prisoner kills another in Dar al-Harb.

Core Idea
Captivity strips compensatory sanctity but not sin-imposing sanctity: diyah is lost, kaffarah remains.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about the obligations when one Muslim prisoner kills another in enemy territory. The disagreement hinges on a subtle distinction between two types of sanctity ('ismah): one that mandates financial compensation and one that only imposes sin. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the Hanafi position on the legal status of captives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the dispute
Two Muslim prisoners in Dar al-Harb; one kills the other. The ruling is contested between Abu Hanifa and his two companions.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
No diyah in either intentional or erroneous killing; only kaffarah in erroneous killing.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Diyah is obligatory in both intentional and erroneous killing.
4
Analyze the key concepts: 'ismah muqawwamah and 'ismah mu'aththimah
Abu Hanifa argues that captivity nullifies ihraz (protection), thus losing compensatory sanctity but retaining sin-imposing sanctity.
5
Contrast with the case of musta'min
A Muslim with aman retains both types of sanctity because the contract preserves protection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وفي الأسيرين كفر فقط في الخطأ) : أي لا يجب شيء إلا الكفارة في الخطأ عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما : تجب الدية في العمد والخطأ؛ لأن العصمة لا تبطل بالأسر، كما لا تبطل بالاستئمان. 
وله : أن الأسير صار تبعاً لهم بقهرهم إياه، فيبطل الإحراز، فسقطت العصمة المقومة، وهي ما يوجب المال عند التعرّض، فلم تجب الدية لا في العمد ولا في الخطأ، لكن العصمة المؤثمة، وهي ما يوجب الإثم عند التعرّض باقية، فتجب الكفارة في الخطأ.
(And regarding the two prisoners, only expiation in error): That is, nothing is obligatory except expiation in error according to Abu Hanifa. According to them (the two companions), the blood-money is obligatory in both intentional and erroneous killing because sanctity is not nullified by captivity, just as it is not nullified by entering under aman. 
And his (Abu Hanifa's) argument: The prisoner becomes a follower of them (the disbelievers) due to their overpowering him, so the protection (ihraz) is invalidated, thus compensatory sanctity (al-'ismah al-muqawwamah) is lost, which is what necessitates wealth when violated, so blood-money is not obligatory in either intentional or erroneous killing. But the sin-imposing sanctity (al-'ismah al-mu'aththimah), which necessitates sin when violated, remains, so expiation is obligatory in error.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the ruling when two Muslim prisoners in Dar al-Harb (land of war) kill one another. The issue is whether blood-money (diyah) is obligatory on the killer. According to Abu Hanifa, nothing is obligatory except the expiation (kaffarah) in the case of erroneous killing. For intentional killing, neither qisas nor diyah are due due to lack of protection (man'ah). The two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, hold that diyah is obligatory in both intentional and erroneous killing because sanctity ('ismah) is not nullified by captivity, just as it is not nullified by entering with aman. 

The author explains Abu Hanifa's reasoning: a prisoner becomes a follower of the disbelievers due to subjugation, hence the protection of the Islamic abode (ihraz) is nullified. This results in the loss of 'ismah muqawwamah (compensatory sanctity)—the type of sanctity that entails financial compensation or qisas when violated. Therefore, diyah is not obligatory. However, 'ismah mu'aththimah (sin-imposing sanctity)—that which entails sin when violated—remains because the victim is still a Muslim. Thus, kaffarah is due in error. The text then contrasts this with the case of a musta'min (a Muslim entering Dar al-Harb under aman) where diyah is wajib because 'ismah muqawwamah persists. The key distinction lies in the status of the prisoner: he is under the complete power of the enemy, unlike the musta'min who retains protection through the contract of aman.

Key Terms
العصمة المقومة (al-'ismah al-muqawwamah): Compensatory sanctity: the inviolability that entails financial compensation (diyah) or qisas when violated.
العصمة المؤثمة (al-'ismah al-mu'aththimah): Sin-imposing sanctity: the inviolability that entails sin (ithm) when violated, leading to kaffarah, but not necessarily financial compensation.
الأسر (al-asr): Captivity, being taken prisoner by the enemy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is obligatory when one Muslim prisoner kills another unintentionally in Dar al-Harb?
A) Blood-money (diyah) only
B) Expiation (kaffarah) only
C) Both blood-money and expiation
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on diyah for prisoners hinges on the distinction between compensatory and sin-imposing sanctity, a key tool in fiqh al-jinayat.

The Muslim Captive's Diyah, Kaffarah, and Qisas
Explains the status of a Muslim captive regarding blood money and expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school distinguishes between compensatory and sin immunity for a Muslim captive and apply the ruling to determine the consequences of killing the captive.

Core Idea
A Muslim captive loses compensatory immunity (no diyah/qisas) but retains sin-immunity (kaffarah).

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies a nuanced Hanafi ruling: when a Muslim captive is killed, the legal consequences depend on the type of immunity violated. You will see how Abu Hanifa uses qiyas khafi to derive that diyah and qisas fall away, yet kaffarah remains for accidental killing. The distinction between compensatory immunity (isma muqawwama) and sin-immunity (isma mu'aththima) is central to understanding this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being judged (killing a Muslim captive) and the specific rulings: no diyah for intentional or accidental, no qisas for intentional, and kaffarah wajib for accidental.
2
Locate the dalil: qiyas khafi
The proof is qiyas to a Muslim who converts in enemy land and does not migrate. Both lose full protective immunity due to subsidiary status (taba'iyyah) to the enemy.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (the illa)
Identify the effective cause: the captive's subsidiary status and lack of ihraz (effective Muslim possession). This illa removes compensatory immunity but leaves sin-immunity intact.
4
Distinguish the two types of immunity
Compensatory immunity (muqawwama) requires wealth or qisas; sin-immunity (mu'aththima) only entails sin. The captive retains only the latter.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Using the derived rules, state the consequences for intentional vs. accidental killing of a Muslim captive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله : أن الأسير صارَ تبعاً لهم بقهرهم إياه، فيبطل الإحراز، فسقطت العصمة المقومة ، وهي ما يوجبُ المال عند التَّعرّض ، فلم تجب الدية لا في العمد، ولا في الخطأ، لكنَّ العصمة المؤثّمة، وهي ما يوجب الإثم عند التَّعرُّضِ باقية، فتجب الكفارة في الخطأ.
هنا ولا يُمَكنُ " حربي وأما سقوط القصاص في العمد، فإنّما هو لعدم المنعة لا لبطلان العصمة، كما مرَّ في المسألة السابقة. قوله : ولـه...الخ ؛ توضيحه على ما في «الهداية» وحواشيها : إن المسلم الأسير بالأسر صار تبعاً لهم لكونه مقهوراً في أيديهم ؛ ولهذا يكون مسافراً بسفرهم، ويكون مقيماً بإقامتهم. والمتبوعون وهم أهل الحرب ليسوا بمعصومين فكذا الفروع، فصار كالمسلم الذي أسلم ثمة ولم يهاجر إلينا، بخلاف المستأمن ، فإنّه بالأمان لا يصير تبعاً حتى تبطل عصمته.
قوله : فسقطت العصمة المقومة ؛ ذكر الزَّيْلَعِيُّ وغيره : إن العصمة المقومة : وهو على صيغة اسم الفاعل من التقويم : هي ما يوجب المال أو القصاص عند التعرض.
والعصمة المؤلمة : وهى اسم فاعل من التأثيم : هي ما يوجب الإثم عند التعرض. والأولى : تثبت بالإحراز بالدار كعصمة المال لا بالإسلام عندنا، فإنَّ الذمي مع كفره يتقوم بالإحراز.
والثانية: بكونه آدمياً ؛ لأنه خُلِقَ لإقامة الدين، ولا يتمكن من ذلك إلا بعصمة نفسه بأن لا يتعرض له أحد ولا يباح قتله إلا بعارض.
And his argument: The captive becomes a subsidiary to them by their overpowering him, so ihraz is invalidated, so the compensatory immunity falls, which is that which necessitates wealth upon violation, so diyah is not obligatory neither in intentional nor in accidental, but the sin-immunity, which is that which necessitates sin upon violation, remains, so expiation is obligatory in accidental. Here and 'nor can a harbi [be given permanent residence]'. And as for the falling of qisas in intentional, it is only due to lack of protection, not due to abolition of immunity, as passed in the previous issue. His saying: 'And for him... etc.'; explanation according to what is in al-Hidayah and its commentaries: The Muslim captive by capture becomes their subsidiary because he is overpowered in their hands; hence he is a traveler by their travel, and a resident by their residence. And the principals, who are the people of war, are not immune, so likewise the branches, so he becomes like a Muslim who embraced Islam there but did not migrate to us, contrary to the musta'min, for he does not become a subsidiary by the aman such that his immunity is abolished. His saying: 'So the compensatory immunity fell'; al-Zayla'i and others mentioned: The compensatory immunity, which is on the pattern of an active participle from taqwim, is that which necessitates wealth or qisas upon violation. And the sin-immunity, which is an active participle from ta'thim, is that which necessitates sin upon violation. The former is established by ihraz in the abode, like the immunity of wealth, not by Islam according to us, for the dhimmi despite his disbelief has compensatory immunity due to ihraz. The latter is by virtue of being human, because he was created to establish religion, and that is only possible by immunity of his person such that no one violates him nor is his killing permitted except for a cause.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah commenting on the ruling of a Muslim captive. It begins by presenting Abu Hanifa's argument: when Muslims capture an enemy, the captive becomes subsidiary to them, thus losing 'ihraz' (effective possession by Muslims). Consequently, the 'compensatory immunity' (isma muqawwama) falls, meaning no diyah is due for intentional or accidental killing. However, the 'sin-immunity' (isma mu'aththima) remains, so kaffarah is required for accidental killing. Qisas is not established due to lack of protection (man'a), not loss of immunity. The commentary then elaborates via al-Hidayah: the captive is analogous to a Muslim who converted in enemy territory and did not migrate—both lack full protection. Unlike a musta'min, who does not lose immunity through aman. Finally, it defines the two immunities: compensatory immunity requires money or qisas and is based on ihraz (like wealth), while sin-immunity is inherent to being human, established to enable religious practice.

Key Terms
العصمة المقومة (al-‘isma al-muqawwama): Immunity that requires monetary compensation or qisas when violated.
العصمة المؤتمة (al-‘isma al-mu’aththima): Immunity that requires sin (ithm) when violated, without necessarily entailing compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the consequence of accidentally killing a Muslim captive?
A) Full diyah is obligatory
B) No kaffarah or diyah
C) Kaffarah is obligatory but no diyah
D) Qisas is obligatory

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a Muslim captive loses compensatory immunity (no diyah/qisas) but retains sin-immunity (kaffarah for accidental killing) due to subsidiary status.

Ruling on the Musta'min's Stay and Jizya
Explains conditions for musta'min's stay and jizya imposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the maximum duration of a musta'min's stay in Dar al-Islam and explain the conditions under which jizya is imposed.

Core Idea
A musta'min may stay up to one year without jizya; longer requires jizya, but the imam may set a shorter period.

The Hanafi school balances security and commerce when a harbi (non-Muslim from enemy territory) enters Muslim lands with a pledge of security (aman). This lesson examines the maximum permitted stay and the point at which jizya becomes due, drawing on the principle that permanent residence is forbidden due to espionage risk, while temporary stays are allowed for trade.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
State that a musta'min (harbi with aman) cannot be given permanent residence; only temporary stay is allowed.
Permanent residence would require enslavement or jizya.
2
Understand the rationale
Explain the justification: permanent stay risks espionage and harm to Muslims, while temporary stays facilitate trade and travel.
This is a form of istihsan based on preventing harm and allowing benefit.
3
Determine the maximum period
The maximum stay is one year because that is the period in which jizya becomes due; the imam may set a shorter period as needed.
The one-year limit is the default; the imam can reduce it but not increase it.
4
Apply the ruling
If the musta'min returns before the set period, no jizya. If he stays beyond, he becomes a dhimmi and jizya is imposed from the completion of the period.
The imam's declaration is a condition for dhimmi status; without it, even a long stay does not make him a dhimmi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ولا يمكن ; مجهول من التمكين. قوله : حربي ؛ أي داخل بأمان في دارنا ، ولو دخل دارنا بلا أمان كان وما معه فيها سنة ، وقيل له : إن أقمت هنا سنة أو شهراً ، نضع عليك الجزية، فإن رجع قبل ذلك، وإلا فهو ذمي سنة. قوله : سنة ؛ قال في «الهداية» : الأصلُ أنّ الحربي لا يُمكنُ من إقامة دائمة في دارنا إلا بالاسترقاق أو الجزية ؛ لأنه يصير عيناً لهم : أي جاسوساً وعوناً علينا، فتلتحق المضرة بالمسلمين. ويمكن من الإقامة اليسيرة ؛ لأن في منعها قطع المسيرة : أي الطعام والجلب وسد باب التجارة، ففصلنا بينهما بسنة ؛ لأنها مدة تجب فيها الجزية، فتكون الإقامة لمصلحة الجزية.
قوله : أو شهراً ؛ أشار به إلى أن ذكر السنة فقط في «البداية» وغيرها ذكر اتفاقي، والغرض منه تحديد الأكثر ، فلا يجوز تحديد الأكثر من سنة لما مرّ، وأما التحديد بأقل كشهر وشهرين ونحو ذلك ، فللإمام التحديد به حسبما يراه مصلحة، لكن ينبغي أن لا يُقَدِّرَ مدّة قليلة بدءاً بحيث يلحقه الضرر ، كذا في «الفتح». قوله : فإن رجع ؛ يعني إن رجع الحربي إلى بلاده بعد مقاتلة الإمام، قبل المدة التي ضربها له فلا سبيل عليه ، وإن لم يرجع بل أقام تلك المدة فما فوقها تضرب عليه الجزية.
وقد ذكر العتابي في «شرح الزيادات»، وصاحب «الفتح» وغيرهما : إنَّ قول الإمام له ذلك شرط ; لكونه ذميّاً، فلو أقام سنة أو سنتين قبل القول لا يكون ذمياً. قوله : أي فيها ؛ هذا اللفظ كثيراً ما يؤتى به في جزاء الشرط ، ويزادُ عليه : ونعمت، والمعنى فإن كان كذا فبهذه الخصلة يحصلُ المقصود، ونعمت هذه الخصلة. قوله : فهو ذمي؛ أي من حين تمام المدة المضروبة له، فلا يؤدّي جزية فيما قبله.
His saying: 'and nor can'; passive from tamkin. His saying: 'a harbi'; i.e., one who enters with aman into our abode. And if he entered our abode without aman, he and what is with him are [subject to] one year? And it is said to him: If you stay here a year or a month, we impose jizya on you. If he returns before that, then nothing, otherwise he is a dhimmi one year. His saying: 'a year'; he said in al-Hidayah: The principle is that the harbi cannot be given permanent residence in our abode except by enslavement or jizya, because he becomes an 'ayn for them: i.e., a spy and helper against us, so harm attaches to Muslims. Temporary residence is permitted, because preventing it would cut off travel: i.e., food and bringing goods and closing the door of trade. So we distinguished between them by a year, because it is a period in which jizya becomes due, so the residence is for the benefit of jizya. His saying: 'or a month'; he indicated that mentioning only a year in al-Bidayah and others is incidental, and the purpose is to specify the maximum, so it is not permitted to specify more than a year as passed. As for specifying less, like a month or two, etc., the imam has discretion to specify according to what he sees as beneficial, but he should not set a very short period initially such that harm befalls him, as in al-Fath. His saying: 'if he returns'; he means that if the harbi returns to his land after the imam's battle before the period set for him, then no claim upon him. If he does not return but stays that period or beyond, jizya is imposed upon him. And al-Atabi and the author of al-Fath and others mentioned: The imam's saying that to him is a condition for him being a dhimmi, so if he stayed a year or two without the statement, he is not a dhimmi. His saying: 'i.e., in it'; this phrase is often used as the consequence of the condition, and is added: 'and what an excellent thing', meaning if it is so, then by this trait the purpose is achieved, and what an excellent trait. His saying: 'so he is a dhimmi'; i.e., from the moment the set period is completed, he does not pay jizya for what preceded.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifying the ruling on a musta'min (a harbi with aman). The base text states 'nor can a harbi be given permanent residence.' The commentary begins by explaining the passive verb. It then paraphrases the statement: if a harbi enters without aman, he and his property are subject to the year rule? Actually, the text clarifies that the musta'min may be told: if you stay a year or a month, we will impose jizya; if you return before, no jizya; otherwise you become a dhimmi. Then, quoting al-Hidayah, the principle is given: permanent residence is not allowed because of espionage risk; temporary stays are allowed for trade. The one-year maximum is based on jizya obligation. The imam may set a shorter period as needed, but not too short to cause hardship. If the musta'min returns before the period, no liability; if he stays beyond, jizya is due from the start of the period? Actually, from the completion of the set period. The imam's declaration is necessary for him to become a dhimmi; without it, even if he stays a year, he is not a dhimmi. The commentary ends with stylistic notes on the conditional phrase.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A harbi who enters Dar al-Islam with a pledge of security (aman).
جزية (jizya): A tax imposed on non-Muslims under Muslim rule as condition of protection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a musta'min remains beyond the period set by the imam?
A) He must leave immediately
B) He becomes a dhimmi and pays jizya
C) He is executed
D) He is enslaved

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a musta'min temporary stay up to one year, after which jizya is due, balancing security and trade.

Temporary Stay of a Harbi in Islamic Territory
Commentary on the ruling of allowing a harbi to reside temporarily and conditions for jizya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a harbi's temporary residence in Dar al-Islam, explain the conditions under which jizya becomes wajib, and identify the requirement of the Imam's explicit statement for dhimmi status.

Core Idea
A harbi cannot reside permanently; only temporary stay permitted, with jizya imposed after the Imam's set period.

This lesson examines a key ruling from the Hanafi school on the interaction between a Muslim state and a non-Muslim enemy (harbi) who wishes to enter and reside temporarily. The central issue is balancing the need for trade and travel with the security concern that a harbi could become a spy. The passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies the maximum period of stay and the mechanism by which jizya becomes obligatory, emphasizing the role of the Imam's explicit declaration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is the residence of a harbi in Dar al-Islam. The default ruling is that permanent residence is prohibited because it leads to harm (espionage). The exception is enslavement or jizya.
The underlying illa is preventing harm (darar) to Muslims.
2
Determine the conditions for permissible temporary stay
Temporary stay is allowed for trade or supplies. The duration is set by the Imam, with a maximum of one year. The Imam may set a shorter period but must avoid causing undue hardship.
The one-year limit is derived from the fact that jizya is assessed annually.
3
Locate the dalil for the prohibition of permanent stay
The dalil is a talil (reasoning) from Al-Hidayah: 'The principle is that a harbi cannot be allowed permanent residence except through enslavement or jizya because he becomes a spy for them.' This is based on the harm that would ensue.
This is a qiyas-style argument: preventing harm is a maqsad (objective) of Shari'ah.
4
Analyze the condition for becoming a dhimmi
If the harbi stays beyond the set period, jizya is imposed and he becomes a dhimmi. However, the Imam's explicit statement (e.g., 'If you stay a year, we impose jizya') is a necessary condition. Staying without the condition does not automatically make him a dhimmi.
This nuance is highlighted by Al-Atabi and others.
5
Address the grammatical clarification on 'illa'
The text corrects a common misinterpretation: the word 'illa' in 'لا يُترك أن يرجع' is not the exception particle but a fusion of 'an' and 'la' meaning 'that not'. This avoids confusion about the meaning.
Proper understanding of Arabic grammar is essential for accurate fiqh interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجهاد باب استيلاء الكفار وباب المستأمن
سنة ، وقيل له : إن أقمت هنا سنة أو شهراً ، نضع عليك الجزية، فإن رجع قبل ذلك، وإلا فهو ذمي سنة ، وقيل له : إن أقمت هنا سنة ) أو شهراً ، نضع عليك الجزية ، فإن رجع قبل ذلك) : جزاء الشرط محذوف : أي فيها ، أو نحوه، وإلا فهو ذمى [١]قوله : سنة ؛ قال في «الهداية»(۱) : الأصلُ أنّ الحربي لا يُمكنُ من إقامة دائمة في دارنا إلا بالاسترقاق أو الجزية ؛ لأنه يصير عيناً لهم : أي جاسوساً وعوناً علينا، فتلتحق المضرة بالمسلمين. ويمكن من الإقامة اليسيرة ؛ لأن في منعها قطع المسيرة : أي الطعام والجلب وسد باب التجارة، ففصلنا بينهما بسنة ؛ لأنها مدة تجب فيها الجزية، فتكون الإقامة لمصلحة الجزية.
[٢] قوله : أو شهراً ؛ أشار به إلى أن ذكر السنة فقط في «البداية»(۲) وغيرها ذكر اتفاقي، والغرض منه تحديد الأكثر ، فلا يجوز تحديد الأكثر من سنة لما مرّ، وأما التحديد بأقل كشهر وشهرين ونحو ذلك ، فللإمام التحديد به حسبما يراه مصلحة، لكن ينبغي أن لا يُقَدِّرَ مدّة قليلة بدءاً بحيث يلحقه الضرر ، كذا في «الفتح» (۳) [٣] قوله : فإن رجع ؛ يعني إن رجع الحربي إلى بلاده بعد مقاتلة الإمام، قبل المدة التي ضربها له فلا سبيل عليه ، وإن لم يرجع بل أقام تلك المدة فما فوقها تضرب عليه الجزية.
وقد ذكر العتابي في «شرح الزيادات»، وصاحب «الفتح» وغيرهما : إنَّ قول الإمام له ذلك شرط ؛ لكونه ذميّاً، فلو أقام سنة أو سنتين قبل القول لا يكون ذمياً. [٤] قوله : أي فيها ؛ هذا اللفظ كثيراً ما يؤتى به في جزاء الشرط ، ويزادُ عليه : ونعمت، والمعنى فإن كان كذا فبهذه الخصلة يحصلُ المقصود، ونعمت هذه الخصلة. [٥] قوله : فهو ذمي؛ أي من حين تمام المدة المضروبة له، فلا يؤدّي جزية فيما قبله.
(۱) «الهداية (٢) : ١٥٤). (۲) «بداية المبتدي»(ص۱۰۲).
(۳) «فتح القدير» (٥ : ٢٧١).
لا يُتْرَكُ أن يرجع ، كما لو اشترى أرضاً فوضع عليه لا يُتْرَكُ أن يرجع) : أي إن لم يرجع قبل المدة المضروبة ، فهو ذمي. واعلم أنَّ مَن لا مساس له" بالعربية يتوهم " أن : إلا ؛ للاستثناء، ولم يعلم أنه كلمة : أن مع لا ، أُدغم إحداهما في الأخرى".
كما لو اشترى أرضاً فوضع عليه)
[١] قوله : لا يُترك ؛ بصيغة المجهول ؛ أي لا يتركه الإمام أن يرجع إلى بلاده، فإنّ في العود ضرراً بأهل الإسلام بعوده حرباً علينا، وبتوالده في دار الحرب، وبانقطاع الجزية.
والمراد بالرجوع الذي يمنع منه الرجوع على وجه اللحاق بهم، وأما مجرد الذهاب إلى دار الحرب للتجارة وغيرها، فلا يمنع عنه الذمي بشرط أن يكون مأموناً. (٢) قوله : مَن لا مساس له ؛ أي لا إدراك له لقواعد فنّ العربية، وهو يطلق على الصرف والنحو وغيرهما.
[٣] قوله : يتوهم ؛ هذا التوهم كما يشعرُ بأن صاحبه ممن لا يدري مسائل النحو المتداولة فضلاً عن المسائل المشكلة، يشعر بأنه ممن لا فهم ولا دراية له أيضاً، فإنّه لو كان «إلا» هاهنا للاستثناء، فأين المستثنى منه ؟ وما معنى الاستثناء؟
[٤]قوله : أدغم إحداهما في الأخرى ؛ بتبديل النون لاماً، وإدغام اللام في اللام، أجاز هذا لشدّة الاتصال بين الكلمتين، فصارا بمنزلة كلمة واحدة كقوله : إلا تَنصُرُوهُ ؛ أي النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فَقَدْ نَصَرَهُ اللهُ إِذْ أَخْرَجَهُ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا ثَانِيَ اثْنَيْنِ إِذْ هُمَا فِي الْغَارِ ﴾ (۱) ؛ أي إن لا تنصروه فلا بأس به عليه؛ إذ قد نصره الله الله في موضع لم يكن له هناك ناصر ، فلا يضعفه في غيره من المواضع. [0] قوله : فوضع عليه ؛ المراد بالوضع إلزامه به وأخذه منه عند حلول وقته، وهو بمباشرة السبب، وهو زراعتها أو تعطيلها مع التمكن منها إذا كانت في ملكه أو زراعتها بالإجارة، وهي في ملك غيره إذا كان خرج مقاسمة، فإنّه يؤخذ منه لا من المالك ، فيصير به ذميّاً.
(1) التوبة : من الآية ٤٠ .
The Book of Jihad, Chapter on the Overpowering of Disbelievers and the Chapter on the Musta'min. [Matn:] "One year, and it is said to him: If you stay here for a year or a month, we impose the jizya upon you. If he returns before that, then [no jizya], otherwise he is a dhimmi." [Commentary:] His saying "one year": It is stated in al-Hidayah: The principle is that a harbi cannot be allowed to reside permanently in our land except through enslavement or jizya, because he becomes a spy for them, i.e., a spy and supporter against us, causing harm to Muslims. But he can be allowed a short stay, because preventing it would cut off travel, i.e., food supplies and trade, so we differentiate between the two by one year, because it is a period in which jizya is obligatory, so the stay is for the benefit of jizya. His saying "or a month": This indicates that the mention of only one year in al-Bidayah and others is incidental, and the purpose is to set the maximum, so it is not permitted to set more than one year as previously stated. As for setting less, like a month, two months, etc., the Imam has the discretion to set it according to what he sees as beneficial, but he should not set a very short period initially that would cause hardship to [the harbi], as stated in al-Fath. His saying "if he returns": It means if the harbi returns to his homeland after the Imam's campaign, before the time specified for him, there is no claim against him. But if he does not return and instead stays that period and beyond, jizya is imposed upon him. Al-Atabi in Sharh al-Ziyadat, the author of al-Fath, and others have mentioned: That the Imam's statement to him is a condition for him being a dhimmi. So if he stays a year or two before the statement, he does not become a dhimmi. His saying "i.e., in it": This phrase is often used for the result of the conditional, and it may be added "and excellent". The meaning: if it is such, then this quality achieves the purpose, and excellent is this quality. His saying "so he is a dhimmi": i.e., from the completion of the imposed period, so he does not pay jizya for the time before that. (1) al-Hidayah (2:154). (2) Bidayat al-Mubtadi (p.102). (3) Fath al-Qadir (5:271). "He is not left to return, just as if he bought land and [land tax] was placed upon him, he is not left to return." Meaning: if he does not return before the imposed period, he is a dhimmi. Know that one who has no grasp of Arabic thinks that "إلا" is for exception, not realizing that it is the word "أن" with "لا" merged together. "As if he bought land and [land tax] was placed upon him." His saying "he is not left": in the passive voice; i.e., the Imam does not leave him to return to his homeland, because in returning there is harm to the people of Islam, as he would become a combatant against us, and by his progeny in Dar al-Harb, and by the cessation of jizya. What is meant by "return" that is prevented is returning with the intention of joining them. As for merely going to Dar al-Harb for trade etc., the dhimmi is not prevented from that, provided he is trustworthy. His saying "one who has no grasp": i.e., no understanding of the rules of the Arabic language, and it applies to morphology and syntax etc. His saying "thinks": This mistaken notion indicates that its holder is one who does not know common grammatical issues, let alone problematic ones. It shows that he has no understanding or knowledge either. If "إلا" were for exception here, then where is the thing excluded? And what is the meaning of exception? His saying "merged one into the other": by changing the noon into a lam and assimilating the lam into the lam. This is allowed due to the strong connection between the two words, so they become like one word, as in His saying (Quran 9:40): "إلا تَنصُرُوهُ" i.e., if you do not help him, then indeed Allah helped him when those who disbelieved drove him out, etc. Meaning: if you do not help him, there is no harm to him, for Allah helped him in a place where he had no helper, so it will not weaken him in other situations. His saying "and [land tax] was placed upon him": What is meant by placement is obligating him to it and taking it from him when it is due. This is by directly working the land, or neglecting it while able to work it if it is in his ownership, or leasing it if it is owned by someone else and he takes a share, then the tax is taken from him, not from the owner, and he becomes a dhimmi by that. (1) Surah al-Tawbah: part of verse 40.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a section from Al-Hidayah discussing the rules for a harbi (non-Muslim enemy combatant) who wishes to reside in Islamic lands. The main ruling is that a harbi cannot be given permanent residence except through becoming a slave (riqq) or a dhimmi (with jizya), to prevent espionage and harm to Muslims. However, temporary residence for trade or supply is permitted. The maximum period for such temporary residence is one year, as that is the minimum period for which jizya is due. The Imam can set a shorter period based on public interest, but should not make it so short as to cause hardship. If the harbi stays beyond that period, jizya is imposed, but he does not become a dhimmi until the Imam explicitly states the condition. The commentary then clarifies a grammatical point: the word "إلا" in the phrase "لا يُتْرَكُ أن يرجع" is actually "أن لا" merged, meaning "that not", not the exception particle. This is important for correct understanding. Finally, it explains that a dhimmi is not prevented from traveling to enemy territory for trade if he is trustworthy.

Key Terms
حربي (harbi): A non-Muslim enemy combatant who resides in Dar al-Harb and is in a state of war with Muslims.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim resident of Dar al-Islam who has a covenant (dhimma) with the Muslim state, paying jizya in exchange for protection.
جزية (jizya): A tax imposed on able-bodied adult male dhimmis in exchange for protection and exemption from military service.
عين (ayn): Here meaning 'spy' or 'informant' (عين عليهم), an agent for the enemy.
إلا (illa): In this passage, it is not the exception particle but the fusion of 'أن' and 'لا' meaning 'that not'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the maximum period a harbi can be allowed to stay in Dar al-Islam for temporary purposes, and what is required for him to become a dhimmi?
A) One year; he automatically becomes dhimmi after one year.
B) One month; he must pay jizya immediately.
C) One year; the Imam must state the condition that jizya will be imposed.
D) There is no limit; he can stay indefinitely.

Key Takeaway
A harbi's temporary stay in Islamic lands is permissible up to one year; jizya becomes due if he stays beyond the set period with the Imam's explicit condition, but not automatically.

Ruling on Musta'min Buying Kharaj Land and Prohibition of Return
Commentary on a ruling preventing a musta'min from returning to dar al-harb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a musta'min who buys kharaj land is prevented from returning to dar al-harb, explain the harm that justifies this prevention, and identify the linguistic contraction of 'an la' into 'illa' in the text.

Core Idea
A musta'min who settles and pays kharaj becomes a dhimmi and is barred from returning to dar al-harb.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a ruling from the matn: a musta'min (non-Muslim visitor) who purchases land and has kharaj imposed on him becomes a dhimmi and is not allowed to return to enemy territory. The commentator also resolves a common grammatical misunderstanding about the word 'illa' in the text, showing that it is not an exception particle but a contraction of 'an la' (if not).

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its key elements
Find the core ruling: a musta'min who buys land and has kharaj imposed is not left to return to dar al-harb. Note the conditions: buying land, imposition of kharaj, and failure to return before a stipulated period.
The ruling is stated in the passive voice: 'He is not left to return.'
2
Understand the rationale
The commentator gives three harms: the musta'min would return as a warrior against Muslims, his procreation in dar al-harb increases enemy strength, and jizya revenue is lost. This justifies the prevention.
The prevention applies to permanent return, not temporary travel for trade if trustworthy.
3
Analyze the linguistic point
The phrase 'إلا أن يرجع' is often misread as an exception. The commentator explains that 'إلا' is actually a contraction of 'أن لا' (if not), assimilated for ease of pronunciation. Verify by checking that if it were an exception, there would be no excluded item.
The Quranic verse 9:40 is cited as a parallel: 'إلا تنصروه' (if you do not help him).
4
Apply the distinction between permanent return and temporary travel
The commentary specifies that only permanent return (in the manner of joining them) is prohibited. Temporary travel for trade or other purposes is allowed for a dhimmi (and by extension for a musta'min who has not settled) provided he is trustworthy.
This nuance prevents an overly broad application of the ruling.

Source Text

[0] قوله : فوضع عليه ؛ المراد بالوضع إلزامه به وأخذه منه عند حلول وقته، وهو بمباشرة السبب، وهو زراعتها أو تعطيلها مع التمكن منها إذا كانت في ملكه أو زراعتها بالإجارة، وهي في ملك غيره إذا كان خرج مقاسمة، فإنّه يؤخذ منه لا من المالك ، فيصير به ذميّاً.

He is not left to return, as if he bought land and [kharaj] was imposed upon him. (He is not left to return): that is, if he does not return before the stipulated period, then he is a dhimmi. And know that one who has no touch with Arabic imagines that 'illa is for exception, and does not know that it is the word 'an with la, one of them assimilated into the other. As if he bought land and it was imposed upon him. [1] His saying: 'He is not left' in the passive voice; that is, the imam does not leave him to return to his country, for in the return there is harm to the people of Islam by his returning as a warrior against us, and by his procreation in dar al-harb, and by the cessation of jizya. And what is meant by the return which is prohibited is the return in the manner of joining them; as for mere going to dar al-harb for trade and other things, it is not prohibited for the dhimmi provided he is trustworthy. [2] His saying: 'one who has no touch'; that is, no grasp of the rules of Arabic philology, and it is applied to morphology, syntax, and others. [3] His saying: 'imagines'; this imagining indicates that its possessor is one who does not know the common syntactic issues, let alone problematic ones; it indicates that he has no understanding or knowledge as well, for if 'illa here were for exception, where is the excluded item? And what would be the meaning of the exception? [4] His saying: 'assimilated one into the other'; by changing the nun into a lam, and assimilating the lam into the lam; this is allowed due to the strong connection between the two words, so they become like one word, as in His saying: 'If you do not help him (i.e., the Prophet), then Allah has helped him when those who disbelieved expelled him, the second of two when they were in the cave' (Quran 9:40); that is, if you do not help him, then no matter, for Allah has helped him in a place where he had no helper, so He will not weaken him in other places. [0] His saying: 'and it was imposed upon him'; what is meant by 'imposition' is making it obligatory upon him and taking it from him at the time of its due, and it is by directly undertaking the cause, which is cultivating it or leaving it uncultivated while being able to cultivate it if it is in his ownership, or cultivating it by lease if it is in someone else's ownership if it is a muqasama tax, for it is taken from him and not from the owner, and thereby he becomes a dhimmi.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn statement. The original ruling states that a musta'min (a non-Muslim given safe passage) who buys land and has kharaj imposed upon him cannot return to dar al-harb (enemy territory). The commentator clarifies the wording: the phrase 'la yutraku an yarji'a' means the imam prevents him from returning permanently if he settles and pays kharaj. The reason is that such return would harm Muslims by strengthening the enemy and breaking the jizya contract. However, temporary travel for trade is permitted if he is trustworthy. A key grammatical point is that the word 'illa' in the text is not the exception particle but a contraction of 'in la' (if not), as proven by elision. The commentator gives an example from Quran 9:40. Finally, the imposition (wad') of kharaj is clarified: it becomes due when the land is cultivated or left uncultivated with ability to cultivate, or when leased for muqasama (sharecropping). The person becomes a dhimmi only when kharaj is actually taken from him.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim from dar al-harb who is given aman (safe passage) to enter Muslim lands temporarily.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim permanently residing in Muslim lands under protection, liable for jizya.
خراج (kharaj): A land tax imposed on agricultural land, distinguished from ushr (tithe) which is on Muslim-owned land.
أن لا (an la): A compound word meaning 'if not', contracted to 'illa' in this text.
إلا (illa): In this passage, it is not the exception particle but a contraction of 'an' and 'la' (if not).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A musta'min buys kharaj land and stays beyond the stipulated period. What is his legal status and what is he prevented from doing?
A) He remains a musta'min and may return anytime.
B) He becomes a dhimmi and is prevented from returning to dar al-harb permanently.
C) He becomes a dhimmi but can still return if he pays extra tax.
D) He must be expelled immediately.

Key Takeaway
A musta'min who settles and pays kharaj becomes a dhimmi and is permanently prohibited from returning to dar al-harb, and the word 'illa' in the text is a contraction of 'an la' meaning 'if not'.

When a Musta'min Becomes a Dhimmi by Buying Kharaj Land
Intra-Hanafi dispute on dhimma through kharaj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a musta'min becomes a dhimmi through kharaj land purchase and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding kharaj muqasama.

Core Idea
A musta'min becomes a dhimmi only when kharaj is imposed, not by purchase alone.

The issue of a musta'min buying land in dar al-Islam raises questions about his legal status. Does the mere act of purchasing land obligate him to kharaj and thus make him a dhimmi? The Hanafi school distinguishes between purchase and actual imposition. This lesson examines the relied-upon position and the internal disagreement over who bears kharaj muqasama.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal effect
The act is a musta'min buying kharaj land. The legal effect is a change of status from musta'min to dhimmi.
The ruling concerns the conditions for this status change.
2
State the general rule
According to the relied-upon position, the musta'min becomes a dhimmi only when kharaj is actually imposed on him, not by the act of purchase alone.
The text states: 'he does not become a dhimmi merely by purchase; because he may buy it for trade.'
3
Distinguish the two types of kharaj
Kharaj mawazzaf is a fixed annual sum on the landowner; kharaj muqasama is a proportional share of produce, taken from the tenant according to the Sahibayn, but from the landowner according to Abu Hanifa.
This distinction affects who becomes a dhimmi when kharaj is imposed.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that all kharaj is on the landowner, so only the landowner becomes dhimmi. The Sahibayn differentiate: for muqasama, the tenant bears it and becomes dhimmi.
The thamarah al-khilaf: if the musta'min is a tenant, his status depends on which view is followed.
5
Note the minority view and the mufta bihi
Some shaykhs (e.g., al-Karkhi) held that mere purchase makes one a dhimmi, but the mufta bihi from al-Hidayah requires imposition.
Practical consequence: under the dominant view, the musta'min can return to dar al-harb until kharaj is imposed.

بخلاف ما إذا كان على المالك بأن كان خراجاً موظفاً ؛ أي دراهم معلومة، فإنّه على مالك الأرض، فلا يصير به المستأجر ذميّاً، أما خراج المقاسمة، وهو ما يكون جزاء الخارج كنصفه أو ثلثه فإنّه يؤخذ من المستأجر، لكن هذا على قولهما. أما على قوله : فإنَّ الخراج مطلقاً على المالك، فلا يصير ذميّاً بوضع الخراج إلا مالك الأرض، ومثله الخلاف في العشر. كذا في «الفتح»، و«شرح السير الكبير». Book of Jihad, Chapter on Subjugation of Disbelievers and Chapter on the Musta'min. (It says:) 'Its kharaj, and upon him is jizya for one year from the time of imposition of kharaj.' (Commentary: 'Its kharaj'): That is, if the musta'min buys kharaj land and kharaj is imposed upon him, he becomes a dhimmi; because if he undertakes it, he undertakes residence in our land. And he does not become a dhimmi merely by purchase, because perhaps he buys it for trade. (And upon him is jizya for one year from the time of imposition of kharaj.) This is different from if the kharaj is upon the owner, i.e., if it is a fixed kharaj (mawazzaf), meaning known dirhams, then it is upon the landowner, so the lessee does not become a dhimmi by it. As for muqasama kharaj, which is a portion of the produce like half or one-third, it is taken from the tenant. But this is according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). As for his (Abu Hanifa's) saying: kharaj absolutely is upon the owner, so no one becomes a dhimmi by imposition of kharaj except the landowner. Similarly there is a difference in 'ushr. This is in al-Fath and Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir. [1] His saying: 'kharajuha' with fath of the kha', and it is what the imam imposes on land, and its detail will come in the chapter on impositions, and we have passed some of it in the chapter on zakat of produce from the Book of Zakat. [2] His saying: 'kharaj land': meaning land on which kharaj is obligatory, for lands include those on which 'ushr is obligatory and those on which kharaj is obligatory. But we have taught you in the Book of Zakat that the disbeliever is only obliged to pay kharaj on his land, not 'ushr, even if he buys 'ushr land. So it would have been appropriate for the commentator to say just 'land', but perhaps he derived this restriction from the possessive construction in the author's words: 'its kharaj', but this derivation is weak. [3] His saying: 'he undertakes residence': al-Hidayah says: 'When kharaj of land becomes obligatory upon him, then after that jizya becomes obligatory upon him for the future year, because he becomes a dhimmi by the obligation of kharaj, so the period is considered at the time of its obligation upon him.' [4] His saying: 'merely by purchase': al-Binayah says: 'Al-Karkhi explicitly stated this in his Mukhtasar, and among the shaykhs there are those who said he becomes a dhimmi merely by purchase, as mentioned by Qadi Khan.'
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the musta'min's situation when he buys kharaj land. The main ruling is that the musta'min becomes a dhimmi only when kharaj is actually imposed on him (by the imam), not merely by the act of buying the land, because purchase could be for trade. Once he becomes a dhimmi through the obligation of kharaj, he must also pay jizya for a full year starting from the time kharaj was imposed. The passage then distinguishes between two types of kharaj: mawazzaf (fixed amount) and muqasama (proportional share of produce). In the case of mawazzaf, the kharaj remains on the landowner, so a tenant (musta'jir) does not become a dhimmi. For muqasama, the kharaj is taken from the tenant, but this is according to the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani). According to Abu Hanifa, all kharaj is on the landowner, so only the landowner becomes a dhimmi. This intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is noted. The passage also criticizes the commentator's use of 'kharaj land' as a restriction, arguing it is unnecessary since all land bought by a musta'min is subject to kharaj anyway. Finally, there is a mention that some earlier Hanafi scholars (like al-Karkhi) held that mere purchase of kharaj land makes one a dhimmi, but the relied-upon position (as per the Hidayah) is that imposition is required.

Key Terms
خراج موظف (kharaj mawazzaf): A fixed annual land tax (in dirhams) imposed by the imam, regardless of produce.
خراج مقاسمة (kharaj muqasama): A proportional tax on the produce of the land, e.g., half or one-third.
جزية (jizya): A poll tax imposed on dhimmis as a sign of protection and subjecthood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), when does a musta'min who buys kharaj land become a dhimmi?
A) Immediately upon purchasing the land.
B) Only when kharaj is imposed upon him by the imam.
C) After one year of residence.
D) When he starts farming the land.

Key Takeaway
A musta'min becomes a dhimmi only when kharaj is actually imposed on him, and the type of kharaj determines who bears the liability and thus who changes status.

Musta'min and Dhimmi Status through Kharaj Imposition
Intra-Hanafi dispute on dhimmi status via kharaj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a musta'min becomes a dhimmi according to the Hanafi school, and explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding kharaj muqasamah.

Core Idea
Imposition of kharaj, not mere purchase, makes a musta'min a dhimmi; but only if he bears the tax.

This passage examines the precise legal trigger for a musta'min (non-Muslim with safe passage) to become a dhimmi (permanent protected subject) through engagement with kharaj land. The key is whether he actually bears the kharaj, which depends on the type of kharaj and whether he is the owner or tenant. The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on this point, which this lesson will dissect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key actors and land types
The musta'min buys or cultivates land subject to kharaj. Distinguish between the owner (malik) and the tenant/lessee (musta'jir), and between kharaj muwazzaf (fixed annual) and kharaj muqasamah (proportional share of produce).
The passage also mentions 'ushr as analogous.
2
State the general rule on becoming dhimmi
The musta'min does NOT become a dhimmi by mere purchase; he only becomes a dhimmi when kharaj is imposed on him. Then he owes jizya for the coming year.
The rationale: acceptance of kharaj implies acceptance of permanent residence (iltizam al-muqam).
3
Distinguish between kharaj muwazzaf and muqasamah
Muwazzaf falls on the landowner; muqasamah is taken from the cultivator (musta'jir) according to the Sahibayn, but from the owner according to Abu Hanifa.
This distinction drives the ikhtilaf.
4
Map the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa: all kharaj on owner → only owner becomes dhimmi. Sahibayn: muqasamah on cultivator → cultivator becomes dhimmi. The same ikhtilaf applies to 'ushr.
The passage references Fath al-Qadir and Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir as sources.
5
Determine the operative ruling (mufta bihi)
Although the passage does not explicitly give tarjih, later Hanafi works follow the Sahibayn: a musta'min who is a tenant and pays kharaj muqasamah becomes a dhimmi.
The commentator notes a weak alternative view (becoming dhimmi by mere purchase) but it is not relied upon.

The book of jihad, chapter on the overpowering of the disbelievers and chapter on the musta'min: its kharaj, and upon him is the jizya of one year from the time of imposition of kharaj. (Its kharaj): That is, if the musta'min buys kharaj land and kharaj is imposed on him, he becomes a dhimmi because if he accepts it, he accepts residence in our land, and he does not become a dhimmi by mere purchase because he might buy it for trade. (And upon him is jizya of one year from the time of imposition of kharaj). Contrary to when it is upon the owner, being fixed kharaj, i.e., known dirhams, then it is upon the owner of the land, so the musta'jir (lessee) does not become a dhimmi by it. As for kharaj al-muqasamah, which is a portion of the produce like half or a third, it is taken from the musta'jir, but this is on their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) view. As for his (Abu Hanifa's) view, kharaj absolutely is upon the owner, so no one becomes a dhimmi by the imposition of kharaj except the owner of the land, and similarly the difference in 'ushr. Thus in al-Fath and Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir. [1] His words: 'its kharaj' with fath of kha', which is what the imam imposes on land, and its detail will come, if Allah wills, in 'Chapter of Taxes', and we have passed a portion of it in 'Chapter of Zakat on Crops' from the 'Book of Zakat'. (2) His words: 'kharaj land' meaning land in which kharaj is obligatory, for lands include those in which 'ushr is obligatory and those in which kharaj is obligatory, but we have informed you in the 'Book of Zakat' that the disbeliever is only obligated in his land for kharaj, not 'ushr, even if he buys 'ushri land. So the commentator should have said 'land' only, and perhaps he deduced this condition from the idafa in the author's words 'its kharaj', but it is a weak deduction. [3] His words: 'he accepted residence'; in al-Hidayah: 'When the kharaj of the land becomes incumbent on him, then after that the jizya for a future year becomes incumbent on him, because he becomes a dhimmi by the obligation of kharaj, so the period is considered at the time of its obligation on him.' [4] His words: 'by mere purchase'; in al-Binayah: 'This is stated by al-Karkhi in his Mukhtasar, and some of the mashayikh said he becomes a dhimmi by mere purchase, mentioned by Qadi Khan.' (1) Fath al-Qadir (6:24). (2) Ending from al-Hidayah (6:23). (3) Ending from al-Binayah (5:779).
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a critical refinement on the commentary (sharh) of al-Wiqayah regarding the status of a musta'min (a non-Muslim granted safe passage) who purchases land subject to kharaj. The author explains that the musta'min becomes a dhimmi (a protected non-Muslim subject) only when kharaj is actually imposed on him, not merely by purchasing the land, because the purchase might be for trade. Upon imposition of kharaj, he also owes jizya for one year starting from the time of imposition. The text then distinguishes between two types of kharaj: (1) kharaj muwazzaf (fixed annual tax on land) which falls on the owner, so a tenant (musta'jir) does not become a dhimmi; (2) kharaj muqasamah (proportional tax on produce) which, according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), is taken from the tenant. However, Abu Hanifa holds that all kharaj falls on the owner regardless, so only the landowner becomes a dhimmi. The same ikhtilaf applies to 'ushr (tithe land). The commentator then critiques the phrase 'kharaj land' as used by the original author, arguing that it is too narrow since the rule applies to all land, not just kharaj land. The passage also clarifies that once the musta'min becomes a dhimmi through kharaj obligation, jizya is due for the next year. This is a technically dense passage requiring careful parsing of intra-Hanafi positions.

Key Terms
خراج (kharāj): A tax imposed by the imam on agricultural land, either fixed (muwazzaf) or proportional (muqasamah).
ذمي (dhimmī): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state who has a contract of protection (dhimma) and pays jizya.
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim who enters dar al-Islam with a temporary safe passage (aman) for trade or other purposes.
خراج موظف (kharāj muwazzaf): Fixed annual tax on land, paid in dirhams.
خراج مقاسمة (kharāj muqāsama): Proportional tax on produce, e.g., half or one-third.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does a musta'min become a dhimmi upon engaging with kharaj land?
A) Immediately upon purchasing the land
B) When kharaj is imposed on him and he bears it
C) When he cultivates the land for one season
D) Never, because musta'min cannot become dhimmis

Key Takeaway
A musta'min becomes a dhimmi only when kharaj is imposed on him and he bears it; for fixed kharaj that means being the owner, for proportional kharaj the Sahibayn extend it to the tenant.

Marriage and Dhimma Status: The Rule of Taba'iyya
Ruling on change of dhimma status via marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the change of dhimma status for a musta'mina marrying a dhimmi and explain the opposite case for a harbi male using the principle of taba'iyya.

Core Idea
A wife's residence follows her husband; thus a musta'mina becomes a dhimmi by marrying a dhimmi, but a harbi does not by marrying a dhimmiya.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi fiqh of jihad and marriage: when a non-Muslim woman who has entered dar al-Islam with aman (musta'mina) marries a dhimmi, her status changes to dhimmi. The rationale is the legal dependence of the wife on the husband in matters of residence. Conversely, a male harbi does not become a dhimmi by marrying a dhimmiya because marriage does not require him to permanently reside. Understanding this distinction clarifies the application of the principle of taba'iyya.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage discusses two cases: (1) a harbiya (female harbi) with aman marries a dhimmi; (2) a harbi (male) with aman marries a dhimmiya. Identify the difference in outcomes.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
In the first case, she becomes a dhimmi. In the second, he does not. State these rulings clearly.
3
Identify the underlying principle
The principle is taba'iyya (dependence): the wife's residence is tied to the husband's. She cannot leave his home, so marrying a dhimmi implies acceptance of permanent residence in dar al-Islam.
4
Explain the opposite case
A man's marriage does not prove he accepts permanent residence; he can divorce and return to dar al-harb. Hence no change in his status.
5
Apply the extension
If both spouses enter dar al-Islam and the husband later becomes a Muslim or a dhimmi, the wife also becomes a dhimmi by dependence. This is cited from al-Bahr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو نَكَحَتْ حربية ذميًا هاهنا، وفي عكسه لا : أي إن نكح الحربي ذمية لا يصير الزوج ذميًا إذ يمكن أن يطلق، فيرجع بخلاف الأول حيث صارت تبعاً للزوج. [١]قوله : أو نكحت ; يعني دخلت حربية دار الإسلام بأمان فتزوجت ذميا ، فتصير بالتزوّج ذميّة ؛ لأن الزوجة تابعة في المقام للزوج، وعليه منع الخروج من بيته فلما التزمت ذلك التزمت المقام. بخلاف ما إذا دخل الحربي دار الإسلام بأمان، فتزوج ذميّة، فإنّه لا يصير بمجرد التزوّج ذميّا ؛ لأن تزوّج الرجل ليس بدليل على التزام المقام ؛ إذ له أن يستمتع بها مدة مقامه، ثمّ يطلق ويرجع إلى داره. [٢] قوله : هاهنا ; وكذا لو دخل الزوجان دارنا ثمّ أسلم الزوج هاهنا أو صار ذمياً، فإنها تصير ذميّة. كذا في «البحر».
Or if a harbiya (female harbi) marries a dhimmi here, and in the opposite case not: That is, if a harbi (male) marries a dhimmiya, the husband does not become a dhimmi because he can divorce and return, contrary to the first case where she becomes a follower of the husband. [1] His words: 'or if she marries' – meaning a harbiya entered dar al-Islam with aman and married a dhimmi, so she becomes a dhimmi by marriage because the wife is dependent on the husband in residence, and he cannot leave his house; so when she accepted that, she accepted residence. Contrary to if a harbi entered dar al-Islam with aman and married a dhimmiya, he does not become a dhimmi by mere marriage because a man's marriage is not evidence of accepting residence; he can enjoy her during his stay, then divorce and return to his land. [2] His words: 'here' – likewise if both spouses enter our land and then the husband becomes Muslim here or becomes a dhimmi, she becomes a dhimmi. Thus in al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage addresses the effect of marriage between a non-Muslim (harbi/musta'min) and a dhimmi on the status of dhimma. The rule is that if a harbiya (female non-Muslim who entered with aman) marries a dhimmi man, she becomes a dhimmi herself because the wife is legally dependent on her husband in matters of residence; she must live where he lives and cannot leave his home. By marrying a dhimmi, she implicitly accepts residence in dar al-Islam, thus becomes a dhimmi. Conversely, if a harbi (male non-Muslim with aman) marries a dhimmiya, he does not become a dhimmi merely by the marriage, because a man is not bound to follow his wife's residence; he can divorce her and return to dar al-harb. Marriage for a man is not sufficient proof of intention to permanently reside. The commentator adds that if both spouses enter dar al-Islam and the husband later becomes Muslim or a dhimmi, the wife becomes a dhimmi as well, as she is a dependent.

Key Terms
حربية (ḥarbiyya): A female non-Muslim who is from dar al-harb (land of war) and has no protection; here specifically one who enters dar al-Islam with aman.
تبعية (taba'iyya): The legal principle of dependence; the wife is considered a dependent of her husband in matters of residence and status.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under the protection of the Islamic state, with a contract of dhimma.
مستأمنة (musta'mina): A female non-Muslim who has been granted temporary safe passage (aman) to enter dar al-Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a harbi male who marries a dhimmiya not become a dhimmi?
A) Because he cannot divorce her
B) Because marriage is not evidence of accepting residence for a man
C) Because he must become Muslim first
D) Because the dhimmiya loses her status

Key Takeaway
A musta'mina becomes a dhimmi by marrying a dhimmi due to marital dependency (taba'iyya), while a harbi does not because he retains the ability to leave.

Rulings on the Musta'min's Return to Dar al-Harb
Rulings on blood forfeiture and debts when a musta'min returns to dar al-harb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two consequences of a musta'min's return to dar al-harb (blood becomes halal, debts forfeited) and identify the conditions that trigger each consequence.

Core Idea
Returning to dar al-harb nullifies aman: his blood becomes halal, and if killed, his debts vanish but his property does not become booty.

This lesson examines the legal consequences when a non-Muslim who has been granted temporary safe passage (aman) voluntarily returns to enemy territory. The classical Hanafi texts establish that by returning, he revokes his own protection, making his blood permissible. Moreover, any debts owed to him by Muslims or dhimmis are forfeited if he is captured and killed. However, his tangible property in dar al-Islam is not automatically considered booty (fay'). Understanding these rules is essential for correctly applying the law of aman in contexts of war and peace.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal status change upon return
Recognize that the musta'min's aman is based on a covenant; returning to dar al-harb breaches that covenant, making his blood permissible (halal) immediately.
This applies even if he returns to a different part of dar al-harb, not necessarily his original home.
2
Distinguish between the two consequences
Note that blood becoming halal is an immediate effect of return, whereas debt forfeiture requires two additional events: capture (or conquest of his group) and killing.
3
Analyze the fate of property
Understand that debts are intangible claims (rights), not physical assets. Thus they do not become fay' (booty) but simply cease to exist upon the creditor's death as an enemy.
Tangible property of the musta'min in dar al-Islam remains in a state of uncertainty (not automatically booty) — he might still become Muslim and reclaim it.
4
Connect to the principle of aman and its breach
Relate this to the general rule that aman is a temporary permission; voluntary return to dar al-harb is a clear indicator of intent to rejoin the enemy, thus nullifying the protection.

If the musta'min returns to his land, his blood becomes permissible. Then if he is captured, or they are overcome, and he is killed, any debt he had against a protected person (ma'sum) falls away – i.e., a Muslim or dhimmi. [3] His words: 'his blood becomes permissible' – meaning he becomes liable to be killed like the rest of the harbi disbelievers. As long as he is in dar al-Islam, his blood is not permissible due to the covenant of aman. When the covenant is removed by his return to dar al-harb, even if not to his own specific land, his blood becomes permissible. [4] His words: 'if he is captured' – meaning the Muslims capture him without having overpowered his abode. [0] His words: 'or they are overcome' – in the passive voice; it is said: 'zahara 'alayhi' means he conquered him; thus the Muslims conquered the disbelievers, took that returning musta'min, and killed him. [6] His words: 'and he is killed' – connected to both capture and conquest, and he points out that his property before killing is not fay', but rather in a state of uncertainty; perhaps he will surrender and take it. [7] His words: 'falls away' – meaning after his death, every debt he had in dar al-Islam against a Muslim or dhimmi is forfeited. It does not become fay' because it is not his property; the debt is owned by the debtor, and the creditor has the right to claim it for collection; what is similar is the 'ayn, not the debt.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage explains the consequences when a musta'min (non-Muslim with temporary aman) voluntarily returns to dar al-harb. By returning, he nullifies his aman, and his blood becomes permissible (halal) to kill, just like any other harbi (enemy combatant). The commentator clarifies that this applies even if he returns to a different part of dar al-harb. However, if he is captured or the Muslims conquer his group and he is killed, his debts that were owed to him in dar al-Islam (i.e., debts owed by Muslims or dhimmis) are forfeited. The reason is that these debts are not physical property; they are rights of claim. Upon his death as a harbi, those rights disappear. However, his other property (if any) that is in dar al-Islam before his killing is not automatically fay' (booty) because it is in a state of uncertainty; he might still become Muslim and reclaim it. The commentary emphasizes that the debt is not a tangible thing to be seized as booty; it is a claim that dies with him as an enemy.

Key Terms
حل دمه (ḥalla damuhu): His blood becomes permissible to shed; i.e., killing him no longer constitutes a crime or sin.
فىء (fay'): Booty taken from enemies without active warfare (as opposed to ghanimah taken in battle).
دين (dayn): A debt (claim) owed to the musta'min by a Muslim or dhimmi.

Key Takeaway
Upon a musta'min's return to dar al-harb, his blood becomes halal and his debts are forfeited if killed; tangible property is not automatically fay'.

Fate of a Musta'min's Property After Return to Dar al-Harb
Commentary on musta'min's return consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the fate of a musta'min's debts and deposits upon his death in dar al-harb, distinguishing between death with and without Muslim victory, and explain the legal reasoning (ta'lil) that property follows the person.

Core Idea
A musta'min's property follows his person: if he becomes spoil, his property does; if not, his heirs inherit.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah addresses the legal status of a musta'min once he returns to dar al-harb, thereby nullifying his aman. The key issue is the fate of his outstanding debts and deposits in dar al-Islam. The ruling hinges on a critical distinction: whether the musta'min is killed after a Muslim victory (ghalaba) or dies naturally or is killed before such victory. This distinction, explained through ta'lil, illustrates a fundamental Hanafi principle that the status of property is tied to the status of its owner.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios of the musta'min's death
The passage distinguishes two scenarios: (1) the musta'min is killed after the Muslims achieve victory (ghalaba) over his people, or (2) he dies a natural death or is killed without such victory.
Ghalaba means the Muslims overwhelmingly defeat his community, capturing his abode.
2
Determine the fate of debts and deposits in each scenario
In scenario 1 (with ghalaba): debts owed to him by Muslims or dhimmis are cancelled (sukut), and deposits held for him become fay' (spoils). In scenario 2 (without ghalaba): both debts and deposits go to his heirs.
The debt falls; it does not become fay' because debt is not a specific property but a right of collection.
3
Understand the 'illa (legal cause) linking person and property
In scenario 1, the musta'min's person itself becomes spoil (fay') because he was captured or killed after the enemy's defeat. Since his person is spoil, his property follows as a consequence. In scenario 2, his person was not made spoil, so his property remains his and passes to his heirs.
The 'illa is extracted by tanqih al-manat: the fact of the person being made spoil is the operative cause.
4
Distinguish between debts and deposits
Debts are not considered property in specie (mal) but merely a right to claim. Therefore, they are not taken as fay' but simply cease to exist. Deposits, however, are physical property conceptually in the musta'min's hand through the depositary, so they become fay' when he becomes spoil.
The depositary's hand is like his hand, so the deposit is constructively possessed by him.
5
Apply the subsidiary rulings on marriage and residence
A harbiyya who enters dar al-Islam and marries a dhimmi becomes a dhimmiyya because the wife follows the husband in residence. But a harbi who marries a dhimmiyya does not become a dhimmi, as he can divorce and return. This shows that residence is the basis for personal status.
This principle complements the main ruling by clarifying who is under aman.

Or if a harbiyya woman marries a dhimmi here, and in the opposite case not. Then if the musta'min returns to his abode, his blood becomes permissible. Then if he is captured, or they are defeated, so he is killed, a debt that was owed to him by a protected person falls, and a deposit held for him is made into spoils. And if he dies or is killed without defeat having been inflicted on them, then both go to his heirs. [1] He said: 'Or if she marries' – meaning a harbiyya woman entered Dar al-Islam with aman and married a dhimmi, so she becomes a dhimmiyya by marriage, because the wife is a follower (tabi') in residence to the husband, and upon him is the prohibition of leaving his house, so when she undertook that, she undertook residence. Contrary to if a harbi man entered Dar al-Islam with aman and married a dhimmiyya, then he does not become a dhimmi merely by marriage, because a man's marriage is not evidence of undertaking residence, for he can enjoy her during his stay, then divorce and return to his abode. [2] He said: 'here' – likewise if the two spouses entered our abode then the husband accepted Islam or became a dhimmi here, then she becomes a dhimmiyya. As in al-Bahr. [3] He said: 'his blood becomes permissible' – meaning he deserves to be killed like the rest of the harbi disbelievers. [4] He said: 'then if he is captured' – meaning the people of Islam capture him without having defeated his abode. [5] He said: 'or defeated' – in the passive voice; one says: I defeated him if I overcame him, and he was defeated means he became overcome; meaning the Muslims overcame the disbelievers, so they took that musta'min who had returned and killed him. [6] He said: 'so he is killed' – linked to both capture and defeat, and he indicated by this that his property before the kill is not spoil, rather it is in a state of uncertainty; perhaps he will accept Islam and take it. [7] He said: 'falls' – meaning after his killing, every debt that he had in Dar al-Islam upon a Muslim or a dhimmi falls. And it does not become fay', because it is not his property, for the debt is the property of the debtor, and the owner has the right to demand it for collection – its like, not its specific. Then 'and a deposit held for him becomes fay'' – meaning every deposit that he had with a protected person in our abode becomes fay'. [8] He said: 'and becomes fay'' – meaning every property that he has with a Muslim or dhimmi as a deposit, it is fay', and likewise his property with his partner and his mudarib and what is in his house of his property in our abode. And the deposit and the like become fay' because it is in his hand tacitly, for the hand of the depositary is like his hand, so it becomes fay' in followership of himself. As for what a Muslim has usurped from him in our abode or a protected person has taken from him as rent for a specific rental, it also falls due to the prior hand upon it, like the debt, as in al-Bahr. [9] He said: 'both' – meaning his debt and his deposit, and likewise all his property that is in our abode. [10] He said: 'remains in his property' – because his person did not become spoil, so likewise his property, contrary to the first case where his person became spoil, so likewise his property.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on several rulings concerning a musta'min (a person from dar al-harb granted temporary safe conduct, aman). The author explains each line of the base text (matn) from Al-Hidayah. The first ruling deals with a harbiyya (a woman from dar al-harb) who enters Dar al-Islam with aman and marries a dhimmi. By marrying, she becomes a dhimmiyya herself because the wife follows the husband in residence. Conversely, if a harbi man marries a dhimmiyya while in Dar al-Islam on aman, he does not become a dhimmi simply by marriage, as he can divorce and return to his land. The second ruling: if a musta'min returns to dar al-harb, his covenant of aman ends, and his blood becomes permissible (i.e., Muslims may kill him) like any other harbi. The third set of rulings concerns the fate of the musta'min's debts and deposits if he is killed. If he is captured or the Muslims defeat his people and he is killed, any debt owed to him by a Muslim or dhimmi in Dar al-Islam is cancelled (the creditor loses the right to claim). His deposits (wadi'a) held by Muslims or dhimmis become fay' (spoils of war) for the Muslim treasury. However, if he dies naturally or is killed without the Muslims having achieved victory (ghalaba), then both his debts and deposits go to his heirs, as his person was not taken as spoil. The commentary clarifies that deposits become fay' as they are conceptually in his hand through the depositary, while debts are not property in specie, only a right to collect, so they are cancelled but not taken as booty. The author also notes that property usurped from him or rent due from him is treated like debts.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A person from dar al-harb who enters dar al-Islam with a temporary safe conduct (aman) for a specific purpose.
فيء (fay'): Spoils taken from the enemy without active fighting, or property that becomes state property after the enemy's defeat or flight.
أمان (aman): A pledge of safety granted to a non-Muslim enemy combatant, allowing temporary entry into dar al-Islam without harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The debt becomes fay' (state property).
B) The debt is cancelled and the Muslim owes nothing.
C) The debt goes to the musta'min's heirs in dar al-harb.
D) The debt is transferred to the Muslim treasury.

Key Takeaway
A musta'min's property is tied to his person: if his person becomes spoil, so does his property; otherwise, it goes to his heirs.
