Fay' of a Harbi's Property After Aman and Conquest
Explanation of fay' classification for a harbi's assets and dependents after aman and conquest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on the status of a harbi's property and dependents after aman and conquest, distinguishing between protected and unprotected items.

Core Idea
A harbi's property in Muslim hands becomes fay' upon conquest, but family members follow different rules based on dependency.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses the complex scenario of a harbi who enters Muslim territory with aman, then the Muslims conquer his original territory. The ruling distinguishes between his own status and that of his family and property, based on the principle of taba'iyya and the concept of ghalaba (overwhelming). We will examine the key distinctions and the proof from hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the harbi's scenario
Understand the two-stage situation: the harbi enters with aman, then the Muslims conquer his homeland. His acts (conversion, death) before or after conquest affect the ruling.
Key variables: timing of conversion/death relative to conquest.
2
Distinguish property by location and possessor
Property held in Muslim territory by a protected person (Muslim or dhimmi) is considered constructively in the harbi's hand; property held by harbis is unprotected.
Deposits with Muslims/dhimmis become fay' upon conquest; deposits with harbis are unprotected.
3
Apply dependency rules for family
Small children follow their father in Islam if the abode is unified; adult children and wife do not. Their status as protected or fay' depends on this.
Unification of abode: if father converted in Dar al-Harb, children follow only if under his custody.
4
Analyze the hadith objection and Hanafi response
The hadith 'They protected their blood and property' is overridden by the fact of overwhelming force (ghalaba) — the law builds on the practical situation.
This shows the principle: al-ghalaba tu'aththiru fi al-ahkam.
5
Derive final ruling for each category
Combine the above steps to state: property with protected persons becomes fay'; harbi's own person becomes fay' if killed after conquest; small children of convert are protected; wife and adult children are fay'.
Exceptions apply if harbi dies naturally before conquest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أفيء وديعة له عنده، وإن مات أو قتل بلا غلبة عليهم فهما لورثته. حربي هنا، وله ثمة عرس وأولاد ووديعة. (وأفيء وديعة له عنده): أي صار في كل وديعة له عند معصوم في دارنا. (وإن مات أو قتل بلا غلبة عليهم فهما لورثته): أي دين كان له على معصوم، أو وديعة له عنده؛ وذلك لأن الأمان باق في ماله، فيرد عليه إن كان حياً، وعلى ورثته إن مات أو قتل بلا غلبة، لكن لو قتل بعدما ظهرنا عليهم صار ماله غنيمة بتبعيته. (حربي هنا، وله ثمة عرس وأولاد ووديعة). قوله: وأفيء؛ يعني كل مال له عند مسلم أو ذمي وديعة، فهو فيء، وكذا ماله عند شريكه ومضاربه وما في بيته من ماله في دارنا، وإنما صارت الوديعة ونحوها فيئاً؛ لأنها في يده تقديراً، فإن يد المودع كيده فتصير فيئاً تبعاً لنفسه. وأما ما غصب منه مسلم في دارنا أو أخذ معصوم منه أجرة عين أجرها، فهو أيضاً ساقط لسبق اليد عليه، كما في الدين، كذا في "البحر". قوله: فهما؛ أي دينه ووديعته، وكذا جميع أمواله التي في دارنا. قوله: باق في ماله؛ لأن نفسه لم تصر مغنومة، فكذا ماله بخلاف الصورة الأولى، فإن نفسه صارت مغنومة، فكذا ماله. قوله: حربي... الخ؛ قال في "البداية": إذا دخل الحربي دارنا بأمان، وله امرأة في دار الحرب وأولاد صغار وكبار ومال أودع بعضه ذمياً وبعضه حربياً وبعضه مسلماً فأسلم هاهنا، ثم ظهر على الدار فذلك كله فيء. وقال في "الهداية": أما المرأة وأولاده الكبار؛ فظاهر؛ لأنهم حربيون وليسوا بأتباع، وكذلك ما في بطنها لو كانت حاملاً، وأما أولاده الصغار؛ فلأن الصغير إنما يصير مسلماً تبعاً لإسلام أبيه إذا كان في يده وتحت ولايته، ومع تباين الدارين لا يتحقق. مع معصوم وغيره، فأسلم هنا، ثم ظهر عليهم فكله فيء. وإن أسلم ثمة فجاء مسلم، ووديعة مع معصوم له، وغيره فيء. ومن أسلم فطفله فظهر عليهم حريمة[؟] وله ورثة هنالك، فقتله مسلم، فلا شيء عليه إلا كفارة الخطأ. مع معصوم وغيره، فأسلم هنا، ثم ظهر عليهم فكله فيء. أما العرس والأولاد الكبار؛ فلعدم التبعية، وأما غير ذلك؛ فلأنه ليست في يده، فإسلامه لا يوجب عصمته. (وإن أسلم ثمة فجاء فظهر عليهم، فطفله حر مسلم، ووديعة مع معصوم له، وغيره فيء). فقوله: ووديعة مبتدأ، ومع معصوم صفته، وله خبره، أي لحربي أسلم. (ومن أسلم ثمة وله ورثة هنالك، فقتله مسلم، فلا شيء عليه إلا كفارة الخطأ) ذلك، وكذا أمواله لا تصير محرزة بإحرازه نفسه؛ لاختلاف الدارين، فبقي الكل فيئاً غنيمة. انتهى. وقال في "البناية": فإن قلت: قوله: "عصموا دماءهم وأموالهم" يخالفه. قلت: هذا باعتبار الغلبة: يعني المال الذي في يده أو ما هو في معناه للعرف؛ لأن من دأب الشرع بناء الحكم على الغلبة. انتهى. قوله: مع معصوم وغيره؛ مرتبط بوديعة: أي وديعة عند مسلم أو ذمي أو حربي. قوله: فلعدم التبعية؛ فإن الأولاد البالغين لا يتبعون آباءهم في الإسلام، ولا يصيرون مسلمين بإسلام أحد الأبوين، والزوجة لا تتبع الزوج إسلاما. قوله: فطفله حر مسلم؛ فلا يتعرض له؛ لأن الأولاد الصغار يتبعون أباهم في الإسلام عند اتحاد الدار، فلما أسلم في دار الحرب تبعوه وصاروا معصومين بخلاف الأولاد الكبار والزوجة، فإنهم ليسوا بمعصومين، فيكون كل منهم فيئاً، وكذا ماله الذي عند حربي؛ لأن يد الحربي ليست يداً محترمة، بخلاف وديعته عند مسلم أو ذمي.
And the deposit belonging to him that he has becomes fay', and if he dies or is killed without (the Muslims) overpowering them, then both (the debt and deposit) are for his heirs. A harbi here, and he has there a wife and children and a deposit. (And the deposit belonging to him that he has becomes fay'): Meaning every deposit he has with a protected person (ma'sum) in our abode becomes fay'. (And if he dies or is killed without overpowering them, then both are for his heirs): Meaning a debt he had against a protected person, or a deposit he had with him; that is because the aman (safe conduct) remains regarding his property, so it is returned to him if he is alive, and to his heirs if he dies or is killed without overpowering, but if he is killed after we overcome them, his property becomes booty by virtue of his being a dependant. (A harbi here, and he has there a wife and children and a deposit). His statement: 'And becomes fay'' means every property he has with a Muslim or a dhimmi as a deposit becomes fay', and likewise his property with his partner and his mudarib and whatever is in his house of his property in our abode; the deposit and the like become fay' because it is (considered) in his hand virtually, for the hand of the depositary is like his hand, so it becomes fay' following himself. As for what a Muslim seized from him in our abode or a protected person took from him as rent for a specific object he rented, that too is forfeited due to the prior possession of it, like a debt, as in 'al-Bahr'. His statement: 'both' means his debt and his deposit, and likewise all his properties that are in our abode. His statement: 'remains in his property' because his own person did not become booty, so his property (also remains protected) unlike the first case, where his person became booty, so his property (also becomes booty). His statement: 'A harbi... etc.'; said in 'al-Bidaya': If a harbi enters our abode with aman, and he has a wife in Dar al-Harb and small and adult children, and property some of which he deposited with a dhimmi, some with a harbi, and some with a Muslim, then he becomes Muslim here, then (the Muslims) overcome the abode, then all that becomes fay'. And said in 'al-Hidaya': As for the wife and his adult children, it is obvious; because they are harbis and not dependents, and likewise what is in her belly if she were pregnant. As for his small children, because a minor only becomes Muslim by following his father's Islam if he is in his custody and under his guardianship, but with the separation of the two abodes it is not realized. With a protected person and others, then he becomes Muslim here, then they overcome them, then all of it is fay'. And if he becomes Muslim there (in Dar al-Harb) and then comes as a Muslim, then a deposit with a protected person is for him, and other than that is fay'. And whoever becomes Muslim and his child (is with him?) and then they overcome them, (his property?) and he has heirs there, then a Muslim kills him, then nothing is upon him except the expiation for accidental killing. With a protected person and others, then he becomes Muslim here, then they overcome them, then all of it is fay'. As for the wife and adult children, because of lack of dependency; and as for other than that, because it is not in his hand, so his becoming Muslim does not cause its inviolability. (And if he becomes Muslim there and comes, then they overcome them, then his child is a free Muslim, and a deposit with a protected person is for him, and other than that is fay'). So his statement: 'and a deposit' is the subject, 'with a protected person' is its adjective, and 'for him' is its predicate, i.e., for a harbi who became Muslim. (And whoever becomes Muslim there and has heirs there, then a Muslim kills him, then nothing is upon him except expiation for accidental killing). That, and likewise his property does not become protected by his own protection because of the difference of the two abodes, so all remains fay' as booty. End. And said in 'al-Binaya': If you say: His statement 'They protected their blood and their property' contradicts it. I say: This is with regard to overwhelming: meaning the property that is in his hand or what is in its meaning customarily, because it is the way of the Law to base the ruling on overwhelming. End. His statement: 'with a protected person and others' is connected to 'deposit': meaning a deposit with a Muslim or a dhimmi or a harbi. His statement: 'because of lack of dependency', for adult children do not follow their fathers in Islam, nor do they become Muslims by the Islam of one of the parents, and the wife does not follow the husband in Islam. His statement: 'so his child is a free Muslim'; so he is not to be touched, because small children follow their father in Islam when the abode is unified, and when he became Muslim in Dar al-Harb they followed him and became inviolable, unlike adult children and the wife, for they are not inviolable, so each of them is fay'. And likewise his property which is with a harbi, because the hand of a harbi is not a respected hand, unlike his deposit with a Muslim or a dhimmi.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah discusses the ruling on the property and family of a harbi (enemy combatant) who enters Muslim territory with aman (safe conduct) and then the Muslims conquer the territory. The key distinction is between when the harbi is alive and when he is dead or killed, and between property that is in the hands of Muslims/dhimmis versus in the hands of harbis. The core ruling: any property of the harbi that is in the hands of a protected person (Muslim or dhimmi) becomes fay' (booty) for the Muslim state upon conquest, because it is considered constructively in his hand. However, if the harbi dies or is killed without the Muslims having overpowered him, the aman remains effective on his property, so it goes to his heirs. But if he is killed after the Muslims have established dominance, his property becomes booty. Regarding his family: his wife and adult children do not follow him in Islam if he converts because they are independent; they remain harbis and thus become booty. His small children, however, follow him in Islam if the abode is unified; but if he converted in Dar al-Harb and then came to Muslim territory, they follow him and are protected. A deposit (wadi'a) of the harbi with a Muslim or dhimmi remains protected even if the harbi dies naturally, but becomes fay' if he is killed during conquest. The passage also addresses an objection based on the hadith 'they protected their blood and their property' (meaning those who embrace Islam or have a pact), and resolves it by stating that this protection applies when there is no overwhelming conquest; the law often bases rulings on the practical situation of dominance.

Key Terms
فيء (fay'): Booty taken from disbelievers without active fighting, i.e., property that falls to the Muslim state upon conquest without a formal battle.
وديعة (wadi'a): Deposit; property entrusted to another for safekeeping.
معصوم (ma'sum): Protected person; one whose life and property are inviolable under Islamic law, including Muslims and dhimmis.
أمان (aman): Safe conduct granted to a harbi for a limited time, rendering his person and property temporarily protected.
تبعية (taba'iyya): Dependency; the legal principle that a person follows another in legal status, e.g., a child follows a parent in religion and domicile.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position stated in the passage, what is the status of the small children of a harbi who enters Muslim territory with aman and then converts to Islam in Dar al-Harb, after the Muslims conquer that territory?
A) They become fay' (booty) because they are harbis.
B) They are considered free Muslims and protected, because they follow their father in Islam.
C) They are enslaved only if they are above the age of puberty.
D) Their status depends on whether they were born before or after the father's conversion.

Key Takeaway
The protection of a harbi's property by aman is overridden by the fact of conquest, but specific exceptions exist for deposits with protected persons and small children who follow their father in Islam.

Property and Children of a Convert in Dar al-Harb upon Conquest
Commentary on rulings regarding convert's property and children in conquest scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two scenarios of a convert's conversion location and apply the rules of hand and dependency to determine what is fay' and what is protected.

Core Idea
Protection extends only to property in a protected hand and to young children who follow their father in Islam.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah examines the legal status of a convert's family and property when Muslim forces conquer a territory. The ruling hinges on whether the convert accepted Islam inside or outside the conquered territory, and on whose hands hold the property. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying the laws of war booty (fay').

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The ruling distinguishes between a Muslim who converts in dar al-Islam (huna) and then the Muslims conquer, and one who converts in dar al-harb (thamma) and then comes to dar al-Islam, followed by conquest.
The phrase 'أسلم هنا' vs 'أسلم ثمة' marks the two scenarios.
2
List the categories of property and persons involved
The passage mentions toddler children, adult children, wife, property in the convert's own hand, property as deposit with a Muslim/dhimmi, and property with a harbi.
Each category has a different ruling.
3
Apply the principle of protection (ismah) based on hand and dependency
The toddler follows the father in Islam because of dependency and unity of abode, so becomes ma'sum. The wife and adult children do not follow, so they are not protected. Property in the convert's own hand or with a harbi is not in a protected hand, so it is fay'.
The hand of a harbi is not respected; the hand of a Muslim or dhimmi is protected.
4
Derive the ruling for each category
For scenario 2: toddler is free Muslim; deposit with protected person remains protected; everything else is fay'. For scenario 1: all property and family become fay'.
The second scenario provides more nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع معصوم وغيره، فأسلم هنا، ثم ظهر عليهم فكله فيء، وإن أسلم ثمة فجاء فظهر عليهم، فطفله حر مسلم، ووديعة مع معصوم له، وغيره فيء. أما العرس والأولاد الكبار فلعدم التبعية، وأما غير ذلك فلأنه ليست في يده، فإسلامه لا يوجب عصمته. [1] قوله: مع معصوم وغيره؛ مرتبط بوديعة: أي وديعة عند مسلم أو ذمي أو حربي. [2] قوله: فلعدم التبعية؛ فإن الأولاد البالغين لا يتبعون أباءهم في الإسلام، ولا يصيرون مسلمين بإسلام أحد الأبوين، والزوجة لا تتبع الزوج إسلاما. [3] قوله: فطفله حر مسلم؛ فلا يتعرّض له؛ لأن الأولاد الصغار يتبعون أباهم في الإسلام عند اتحاد الدار، فلما أسلم في دار الحرب تبعوه وصاروا معصومين بخلاف الأولاد الكبار والزوجة، فإنهم ليسوا بمعصومين، فيكون كل منهم فيئاً، وكذا ماله الذي عند حربي؛ لأن يد الحربي ليست يداً محترمة، بخلاف وديعته عند مسلم أو ذمي.
With a protected person and other than him, so he accepted Islam here, then they gained victory over them, so all of it is fay'. And if he accepted Islam there and came, then they gained victory over them, his toddler is a free Muslim, and a deposit with a protected person is his, and other than that is fay'. As for the wife and adult children, due to lack of dependency. And as for other than that, because it is not in his hand, his acceptance of Islam does not entail protection for it. [1] His statement: 'with a protected person and other than him' is connected to deposit: meaning a deposit with a Muslim, dhimmi, or harbi. [2] His statement: 'due to lack of dependency'; because adult children do not follow their fathers in Islam, and they do not become Muslims by the conversion of one parent, and the wife does not follow the husband in Islam. [3] His statement: 'his toddler is a free Muslim'; so he is not touched; because young children follow their father in Islam when the abode is unified, so when he converted in the abode of war, they followed him and became protected, unlike adult children and the wife, for they are not protected, so each of them is fay', and likewise his property that is with a harbi; because the hand of a harbi is not a protected hand, unlike his deposit with a Muslim or dhimmi.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from the matn regarding the effects of a Muslim's conversion in dar al-harb when Muslims subsequently conquer. The ruling distinguishes between two scenarios: (1) a Muslim who converts while in dar al-Islam (here) and then the Muslims conquer, then all his property in that land is fay' (spoils) because his conversion does not grant protection to property that is not in his hand. (2) A Muslim who converts in dar al-harb (thamma) and then comes to dar al-Islam, and then the Muslims conquer, then his toddler child becomes a free Muslim and is protected, his deposit (wadi'ah) that he left with a protected person (Muslim or dhimmi) remains his protected property, but other property in the hands of harbis is fay'. The commentary explains the reasoning: the wife and adult children do not follow the father in conversion (they are not dependent on him in religion), so they remain non-Muslims and become fay'. Young children, however, follow their father in Islam as long as they are in the same abode. The property with a harbi is not in a protected hand, so it is fay', but the deposit with a protected person is respected because that hand is protected. This shows the Hanafi principle that both conversion and the status of the abode (dar) and the hand (yad) affect legal protection.

Key Terms
فيء (fay'): Spoils taken from non-Muslims without fighting, or property that becomes war booty upon conquest.
معصوم (ma'sum): Protected person or property whose inviolability is guaranteed by Islamic law.
وديعة (wadi'ah): A deposit left for safekeeping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man converts to Islam in dar al-harb and later Muslims conquer that land. Which of the following becomes fay'?
A) His toddler child
B) His deposit with a Muslim
C) His adult child
D) His property in his own hand

Key Takeaway
The inviolability of a convert's property and children in conquered territory depends on whether they are in a protected hand or dependent on him in faith.

Killing a Muslim Convert in Dar al-Harb: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Hanafi ruling on qisas/diyah for killing a Muslim convert in dar al-harb with Shafi'i opposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school does not require qisas or diyah for killing a Muslim convert in dar al-Harb, and outline the Shafi'i counterargument and the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
Full blood protection requires both Islam and abode – the convert in dar al-harb lacks the latter.

When a Muslim converts to Islam while living in enemy territory (dar al-harb) and is later killed by another Muslim, a critical legal question arises: does the killer owe qisas, diyah, or only kaffarah? The Hanafi school gives a nuanced answer based on the Quran and a distinction between two types of inviolability. This lesson unpacks that ruling and the Shafi'i objection, showing why the convert's location matters as much as his faith.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Begin by clearly stating the Hanafi ruling for both intentional and mistaken killing of a Muslim convert in dar al-harb who has Muslim heirs.
The ruling is: no qisas, diyah, or kaffarah for intentional killing; only kaffarah for mistaken killing.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Understand that al-Shafi'i holds the opposite: the convert's blood is fully protected by Islam, so intentional killing requires qisas.
The Shafi'i proof: the blood of a believer is ma'sum (protected) by virtue of iman.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof from Quran 4:92
Read the verse carefully: it mentions three scenarios. Notice that in the second scenario (believer from hostile people), diyah is omitted. This is the key textual evidence for the Hanafi position.
The omission of diyah where expected (by analogy with the other scenarios) indicates that full compensatory protection is absent.
4
Understand the ismah distinction
Learn the Hanafi differentiation between ismah mu'limah (protection from suffering) and ismah muqawwamah (protection with financial value). The convert in dar al-harb has only the former.
Compensatory protection requires both Islam and the abode of Islam (dar al-Islam).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Summarize the practical difference: in Hanafi, the killer walks free (no qisas); in Shafi'i, the killer is executed.
This has real-world implications for cases involving Muslims in non-Muslim countries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن أسلم ثمة وله ورثة هنالك، فقتله مسلم، فلا شيء عليه إلا كفارة الخطأ. [1] قوله: مسلمون؛ قيّد به؛ لأنه لو لم يكن وارث مسلم، بل كافر، فعدم وجوب القصاص والدية ظاهر، وكذا لو لم يكن له وارث. [2] قوله: فلا يجب شيء؛ أي لا القصاص ولا الدية ولا الكفارة، أما عدم الكفارة؛ فلأنه لا كفارة في القتل العمد عندنا مطلقاً؛ لأن نص الكفارة وهو الآية التي في سورة النساء مقيد بالخطأ، وتفصيله في موضعه. وأما عدم وجوب الدية والقصاص؛ فلعدم العصمة المقومة وإن وجدت المؤلمة، فإن المؤلمة بالآدمية، والمقومة وإن كان ثبوتها بالإسلام لكن يعتبر معه الدار أيضاً، فمن أسلم ولم يهاجر إلينا فهو من أهل دارهم حكماً، وهذا هو الوجه في عدم وجوب الدية في الخطأ. ويشهد له قوله: (وَمَن قَتَلَ مُؤْمِنًا خَطَأً فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُّؤْمِنَةٍ وَدِيَةٌ مُسَلَّمَةٌ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ إِلَّا أَن يَصَدَّقُوا فَإِن كَانَ مِن قَوْمٍ عَدُوٍّ لَّكُمْ وَهُوَ مُؤْمِنٌ فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُّؤْمِنَةٍ وَإِن كَانَ مِن قَوْمٍ بَيْنَكُمْ وَبَيْنَهُم مِّيثَاقٌ فَدِيَةٌ مُسَلَّمَةٌ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ وَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُّؤْمِنَةٍ فَمَن لَّمْ يَجِدْ فَصِيَامُ شَهْرَيْنِ مُتَتَابِعَيْنِ تَوْبَةً مِّنَ اللَّهِ) حيث ذكر الله له الدية في الصورة الأولى والثالثة مع الكفارة، واكتفى في الصورة الثانية على الكفارة، فلو كانت الدية واجبة فيها أيضاً لذكرها كما ذكرها في غيرها. [3] قوله: يجب القصاص ... الخ؛ عللوه بأن القاتل أراق دماً معصوماً؛ لوجود المؤلمة والمقومة. وعند الشافعي يجب القصاص في العمد لأنه لا يكون فيئاً؛ لأنه في يد محترمة.
And whoever converts there and has heirs there, and a Muslim kills him, then nothing is due upon him except kaffarah for error. [1] His statement: 'Muslim heirs' is restricted; because if he did not have a Muslim heir, but a disbeliever, then the non-obligation of qisas and diyah is apparent, and likewise if he had no heir. [2] His statement: 'nothing is due' meaning no qisas, no diyah, and no kaffarah. As for the non-obligation of kaffarah, because there is no kaffarah in intentional killing absolutely in our school; because the text of kaffarah which is the verse in Surat an-Nisa' is restricted to error, and its detail is in its place. And as for the non-obligation of diyah and qisas, due to the lack of compensatory protection (ismah muqawwamah) even though suffering protection (ismah mu'limah) exists. For suffering protection is due to humanity, and compensatory protection, although its establishment is by Islam, but the abode is also considered with it. So whoever converts and does not migrate to us is from the people of their abode in effect. And this is the reason for the non-obligation of diyah in error. And it is attested by His statement: (And whoever kills a believer by mistake then the freeing of a believing slave and blood money paid to his family unless they give charity; but if he is from a people hostile to you and he is a believer, then freeing a believing slave; and if he is from a people between whom and you is a treaty, then blood money paid to his family and freeing a believing slave.) where Allah mentioned for him diyah in the first and third forms along with kaffarah, and sufficed in the second form with kaffarah. So if diyah were obligatory there as well, He would have mentioned it as He mentioned it in the others. [3] His statement: 'qisas is required... etc.'; they reasoned that the killer spilled protected blood, due to the existence of suffering and compensatory protection. And according to al-Shafi'i, qisas is required in intentional killing because it is not fay', because it is in a protected hand.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi position on killing a Muslim who converted in dar al-harb and has Muslim heirs there, and contrasts it with the Shafi'i view. The Hanafi ruling distinguishes between intentional and mistaken killing. In intentional killing, no qisas, diyah, or kaffarah is due. In mistaken killing, only kaffarah is due. The reasoning is that while the person has the protection (ismah) of suffering (mu'limah) by virtue of humanity, the compensatory protection (ismah muqawwamah) that gives entitlement to diyah or qisas requires not only Islam but also the abode (dar). Since the convert remains in dar al-harb and has not migrated to dar al-Islam, he is considered from the people of that abode effectively, and thus his blood is not protected in the compensatory sense. The Quranic verse (4:92) supports this: for a believer among hostile people (second scenario), Allah only prescribed kaffarah and did not prescribe diyah, implying that the blood of such a believer does not warrant diyah. Al-Shafi'i objects, arguing that the blood is protected because the person is a believer and therefore qisas applies. The Hanafi response is that full protection requires both Islam and abode; the verse indicates that abode matters. This is a classic example of the Hanafi usul principle of considering both text and context. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the Hanafi view.

Key Terms
عصمة مؤلمة (ismah mu'limah): Protection from suffering; the inviolability of a person that prohibits causing harm but does not entail financial compensation.
عصمة مقومة (ismah muqawwamah): Protection that entails financial compensation (diyah) or qisas if violated.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory under non-Muslim rule where Islamic law is not dominant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a Muslim intentionally kills another Muslim who converted to Islam in dar al-harb and has Muslim heirs there, what is due?
A) Qisas
B) Diyah
C) Kaffarah
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that full blood protection (ismah muqawwamah) requires both Islam and the abode of Islam; a convert who remains in dar al-harb does not have full protection.

Intentional Killing of a Muslim in Dar al-Harb: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Commentary on ruling of killing Muslims in dar al-harb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on intentional killing of a Muslim in Dar al-Harb, explain the Hanafi distinction between deterrent and compensatory inviolability, and compare it with the Shafi'i position using Quran 4:92 as evidence.

Core Idea
A Muslim in Dar al-Harb lacks compensatory inviolability; thus no qisas or diyah, only kaffarah for accidental killing.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a critical dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i. The question: if a Muslim kills another Muslim in enemy territory (Dar al-Harb), does the killer owe qisas or diyah? The Hanafi answer rests on a subtle distinction between two types of inviolability: deterrent (based on humanity) and compensatory (based on Islam plus residence). Let's examine the evidence from the Quran and the reasoning behind the Hanafi view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
What does the Hanafi school say about intentional killing of a Muslim in Dar al-Harb? Identify what is and is not obligatory.
The text states: 'If the killing is intentional, nothing is obligatory.'
2
State the Shafi'i ruling
How does al-Shafi'i differ? What does he require and why?
Al-Shafi'i requires qisas because the blood is inviolable due to Islam.
3
Explain the Hanafi distinction
What are the two types of inviolability? How do they differ?
al-isma al-mu'alima (deterrent) and al-isma al-muqawwima (compensatory).
4
Analyze the Quranic evidence
How does Quran 4:92 support the Hanafi position?
The verse omits diyah for a believer from an enemy tribe, indicating no financial liability.
5
Determine the practical consequence
What is the thamarah al-khilaf? How does this difference affect actual rulings?
A Hanafi judge gives no qisas or diyah; a Shafi'i judge gives qisas.

كتاب الجهاد / باب استيلاء الكفار وباب المستأمن
[1] أي له ورثة مسلمون في دار الحرب ، فإن كان القتل عمداً ، فلا يجب شيء وإن كان خطأ لا يجب إلا الكفارة وعند الشَّافِعِي ) ب القصاص في العمد فإنه لا يكون فيئاً ؛ لأنه في يد محترمة. [1] قوله : مسلمون ؛ قيَّد به ؛ لأنه لو لم يكن وارث مسلم، بل كافر، فعدمُ وجوب القصاص والدية ظاهر، وكذا لو لم يكن له وارث.
[2] قوله : فلا يجب شيء ؛ أي لا القصاص ولا الدية ولا الكفارة، أما عدم الكفارة ؛ فلأنه لا كفارة في القتل العمد عندنا مطلقاً؛ لأن نص الكفارة وهو الآية التي في سورة النساء مقيد بالخطأ، وتفصيله في موضعه.
وأما عدم وجوب الدية والقصاص ؛ فلعدم العصمة المقومة وإن وجدت المؤلمة، فإنَّ المؤلمة بالآدمية، والمقومة وإن كان ثبوتها بالإسلام لكن يعتبر معه الدار أيضاً، فمن أسلم ولم يهاجر إلينا فهو من أهل دارهم حكماً ، وهذا هو الوجه في عدم وجوب الدية في الخطأ.
ويشهد له قوله : ( وَمَن قَتَلَ مُؤْمِنًا خَطَا فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُؤْمِنَةٍ وَدِيَةٌ مُسَلَّمَةٌ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ إِلا أَن يَصَدَقُوا فَإِن كَانَ مِن قَوْمٍ عَدُوّ لَكُمْ وَهُوَ مُؤْمِنٌ فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُّؤْمِنَةٍ وَإِن كَانَ مِن قَوْمٍ بَيْنَكُمْ وَبَيْنَهُم مِيثَقٌ فَدِيَةٌ مُسَلَّمَةٌ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ وَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُّؤْمِنَةٌ فَمَن لَّمْ يَجِدْ فَصِيَامُ شَهْرَيْنِ مُتَتَابِعَيْنِ تَوْبَةً مِّنَ اللَّهِ ) (٢) حيث ذكر الله له الدية في الصورة الأولى والثالثة مع الكفارة، واكتفى في الصورة الثانية على الكفارة، فلو كانت الديّة واجبة فيها أيضا لذكرها كما ذكرها في غيرها، وفي حواشي «الهداية»(۳) ، و«الهداية»(4) في هذا المقام تفصيل أغناني عن ذكره ما ذكرته لكفايته.
[3]قوله : يجب القصاص ... الخ ؛ عللوه بأن القاتل أراق دماً معصوماً ؛ لوجود العاصم، وهو الإسلام، فيجب القصاص في العمدِ والدية في الخطأ، ونحن نقول : هذا القدر لا يكفي إلا لوجود العصمة المؤلمة، ومبنى وجوب القصاص والدية هو وجود العصمة المقومة.
[1] قوله : وأخذ الإمام...الخ ؛ يعني إذا قتل مسلم مسلماً لا ولي له قتلاً خطأً ، فديته يأخذها الإمام، ويجعلُها في بيت المال، فإن الإمامَ وَلِيُّ مَن لا وليَّ له ، ولم يذكر وجوب الكفارة ؛ لظهوره من كتاب الجنايات»، وإنما ذكر هذه المسألة هاهنا مع كونها من «كتاب الجنايات» أو من كتاب الديات بتبعيَّة ذكر حكم المستأمن، وهو المقصود في هذا الباب.
[2] قوله : من عاقلة قاتله ؛ العاقلةُ . هم أهل الديوان والعصبة، وتفصيله في «كتاب الديات».
[3]قوله : أو أخذ الدِّية ؛ أي صُلْحاً برضاء القاتل، فإن موجب العمـد هـو القصاص، ووجوب الدية فيه يكون بالصلح ، وإنما لم يجز للإمام العفو مع جواز العفو للأولياء نظراً لحق العامة، فإنّ ولايته نظرية ، ولا نظر في إسقاط حقهم بلا عوض، كذا في «الفتح»(۱).
(۱) «فتح القدير»(٥ : ٢٧٧).
حجامي
Book of Jihad / Chapter on the Seizure of Disbelievers and Chapter on the Musta'min. [1] i.e., he has Muslim heirs in Dar al-Harb. If the killing is intentional, nothing is obligatory. If it is erroneous, only kaffarah is obligatory. And according to al-Shafi'i, qisas is obligatory in intentional killing because the blood is inviolable since it is in a respected hand. [1] His saying: 'Muslims' – he restricted it because if there is no Muslim heir, but a disbeliever, then the non-obligation of qisas and diyah is apparent, likewise if there is no heir at all. [2] His saying: 'nothing is obligatory' – i.e., neither qisas nor diyah nor kaffarah. As for the absence of kaffarah, it is because there is no kaffarah in intentional killing according to us absolutely; since the text of kaffarah, which is the verse in Surah al-Nisa, is restricted to error, and its detail is in its place. As for the non-obligation of diyah and qisas, it is due to the lack of compensatory inviolability (al-isma al-muqawwima) even though the deterrent inviolability (al-mu'alima) exists. For the deterrent inviolability is due to humanity, and the compensatory inviolability, although established by Islam, also requires consideration of the abode. So whoever embraces Islam but does not emigrate to us is considered one of the people of their abode by legal fiction, and this is the reason for the non-obligation of diyah in error. This is supported by His saying: 'And whoever kills a believer by mistake, then the freeing of a believing slave and a diyah paid to his family unless they give charity. If he is from a people who are your enemies and he is a believer, then the freeing of a believing slave. If he is from a people between whom and you is a treaty, then a diyah paid to his family and the freeing of a believing slave. And whoever cannot find [the means] then fasting for two consecutive months as repentance from Allah.' (al-Nisa 92) Where Allah mentioned diyah in the first and third situations along with kaffarah, and sufficed with kaffarah in the second situation. If diyah were obligatory there too, He would have mentioned it as He mentioned it in others. And in the margins of al-Hidayah and al-Hidayah itself regarding this point there is detail that I have sufficed with what I mentioned, making it sufficient. [3] His saying: 'qisas is obligatory... etc.'; they argued that the killer shed inviolable blood due to existence of the protector (al-asim), which is Islam, so qisas is obligatory in intentional killing and diyah in error. We say: this amount is insufficient except for the existence of deterrent inviolability, and the basis for obligation of qisas and diyah is the existence of compensatory inviolability. [1] His saying: 'And the Imam takes... etc.'; meaning if a Muslim kills a Muslim who has no heir by mistake, then the diyah is taken by the Imam and placed in the treasury, for the Imam is the guardian of one who has no guardian. He did not mention the obligation of kaffarah because it is clear from the Book of Jinayat. He mentioned this issue here, though it is from the Book of Jinayat or the Book of Diyat, as a consequence of mentioning the ruling of the musta'min, which is the intended purpose in this chapter. [2] His saying: 'from the aqila of the killer'; the aqila are the people of the register and the asaba, and its detail is in the Book of Diyat. [3] His saying: 'or takes the diyah'; i.e., by mutual settlement with the consent of the killer, since the legal consequence of intentional killing is qisas, and obligation of diyah therein occurs by settlement. The Imam is not permitted to pardon, whereas the heirs are permitted, due to consideration of the public right. For his authority is fiduciary, and there is no consideration in dropping their right without compensation. Thus in al-Fath. (1) Fath al-Qadir (5:277). Hujjami.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqaya, addresses two main rulings: the legal status of a Muslim killed in Dar al-Harb, and the Imam's authority regarding diyah (blood money). The passage begins with a dispute between the Hanafi school and al-Shafi'i. According to the Hanafis, if a Muslim is killed in Dar al-Harb by another Muslim, neither qisas nor diyah is obligatory for intentional killing; only kaffarah (expiation) is obligatory if the killing was accidental. Al-Shafi'i, however, requires qisas for intentional killing because the victim's blood is inviolable (mahrum) due to Islam. The Hanafi response distinguishes between two types of inviolability: al-isma al-mu'alima (deterrent inviolability, based on humanity) and al-isma al-muqawwima (compensatory inviolability, which requires both Islam and residence in Dar al-Islam). A Muslim in Dar al-Harb lacks the latter, so no qisas or diyah is due. The Quranic verse (4:92) supports this: for a believer from an enemy tribe, only kaffarah is mentioned, not diyah. The second ruling concerns the Imam's role: if a Muslim with no heir, or a musta'min (non-Muslim given safe conduct) who embraced Islam, is killed accidentally, the Imam takes the diyah from the killer's aqila and places it in the public treasury. For intentional killing, the Imam has the option to enforce qisas or accept diyah through settlement, but he cannot pardon without compensation, as he acts as trustee for the public.

Key Terms
العصمة المؤلمة (al-ʿiṣmah al-muʾlimah): Deterrent inviolability based on humanity, which prohibits killing and may entail punishment (like ta'zir) but does not entitle to financial compensation or qisas.
العصمة المقومة (al-ʿiṣmah al-muqawwimah): Compensatory inviolability that arises from Islam combined with residence in Dar al-Islam, entitling to diyah or qisas if violated.
المستأمن (al-mustaʾmin): A non-Muslim from Dar al-Harb who enters Dar al-Islam with safe conduct (aman) temporarily.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a Muslim intentionally kills another Muslim in Dar al-Harb?
A) Qisas is obligatory.
B) Diyah is obligatory.
C) Nothing is obligatory.
D) Kaffarah is obligatory.

Key Takeaway
Intentional killing of a Muslim in Dar al-Harb incurs no financial or retaliatory liability under Hanafi law due to the absence of compensatory inviolability.

The Imam's Authority over Diyah for a Victim without Heirs
Comment on Imam's authority in diyah for heirless victim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Imam's role in collecting diyah for a Muslim killed accidentally without heirs, and state why the Imam cannot pardon the killer without compensation.

Core Idea
The Imam is the guardian of the heirless, but he cannot forgive the public right to diyah without compensation.

The second part of this passage addresses a procedural rule: if a Muslim is killed accidentally and has no Muslim heirs (or a musta'min who embraced Islam dies heirless), the Imam takes the diyah from the killer's aqila and deposits it in the public treasury. The Imam acts as guardian (wali) for those without guardians, but his authority is fiduciary—he cannot drop the right to diyah without receiving compensation. This rule underscores the public interest in blood money.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Under what circumstances does the Imam take the diyah?
When a Muslim kills another Muslim accidentally and the victim has no Muslim heir.
2
State what the Imam does
What does the Imam do with the diyah?
He takes it from the aqila of the killer and places it in the bayt al-mal (treasury).
3
Explain the rationale
Why is the Imam the recipient?
The Imam is the guardian of one who has no guardian (wali man la wali lahu).
4
Analyze the limitation on the Imam
Why can't the Imam pardon the killer?
Because the killer owes the diyah to the public treasury; the Imam's authority is fiduciary (wilaya nazariyya) and cannot drop a public right without compensation.
5
Understand the difference from intentional killing
For intentional killing, the Imam may accept diyah by settlement but cannot pardon.
The default for intentional killing is qisas; diyah only comes through sulh (settlement). The Imam may accept diyah but not forgive.

(al-Nisa 92) Where Allah mentioned diyah in the first and third situations along with kaffarah, and sufficed with kaffarah in the second situation. If diyah were obligatory there too, He would have mentioned it as He mentioned it in others. And in the margins of al-Hidayah and al-Hidayah itself regarding this point there is detail that I have sufficed with what I mentioned, making it sufficient. [3] His saying: 'qisas is obligatory... etc.'; they argued that the killer shed inviolable blood due to existence of the protector (al-asim), which is Islam, so qisas is obligatory in intentional killing and diyah in error. We say: this amount is insufficient except for the existence of deterrent inviolability, and the basis for obligation of qisas and diyah is the existence of compensatory inviolability. [1] His saying: 'And the Imam takes... etc.'; meaning if a Muslim kills a Muslim who has no heir by mistake, then the diyah is taken by the Imam and placed in the treasury, for the Imam is the guardian of one who has no guardian. He did not mention the obligation of kaffarah because it is clear from the Book of Jinayat. He mentioned this issue here, though it is from the Book of Jinayat or the Book of Diyat, as a consequence of mentioning the ruling of the musta'min, which is the intended purpose in this chapter. [2] His saying: 'from the aqila of the killer'; the aqila are the people of the register and the asaba, and its detail is in the Book of Diyat. [3] His saying: 'or takes the diyah'; i.e., by mutual settlement with the consent of the killer, since the legal consequence of intentional killing is qisas, and obligation of diyah therein occurs by settlement. The Imam is not permitted to pardon, whereas the heirs are permitted, due to consideration of the public right. For his authority is fiduciary, and there is no consideration in dropping their right without compensation. Thus in al-Fath. (1) Fath al-Qadir (5:277). Hujjami.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqaya, addresses two main rulings: the legal status of a Muslim killed in Dar al-Harb, and the Imam's authority regarding diyah (blood money). The passage begins with a dispute between the Hanafi school and al-Shafi'i. According to the Hanafis, if a Muslim is killed in Dar al-Harb by another Muslim, neither qisas nor diyah is obligatory for intentional killing; only kaffarah (expiation) is obligatory if the killing was accidental. Al-Shafi'i, however, requires qisas for intentional killing because the victim's blood is inviolable (mahrum) due to Islam. The Hanafi response distinguishes between two types of inviolability: al-isma al-mu'alima (deterrent inviolability, based on humanity) and al-isma al-muqawwima (compensatory inviolability, which requires both Islam and residence in Dar al-Islam). A Muslim in Dar al-Harb lacks the latter, so no qisas or diyah is due. The Quranic verse (4:92) supports this: for a believer from an enemy tribe, only kaffarah is mentioned, not diyah. The second ruling concerns the Imam's role: if a Muslim with no heir, or a musta'min (non-Muslim given safe conduct) who embraced Islam, is killed accidentally, the Imam takes the diyah from the killer's aqila and places it in the public treasury. For intentional killing, the Imam has the option to enforce qisas or accept diyah through settlement, but he cannot pardon without compensation, as he acts as trustee for the public.

Key Terms
العاقلة (al-ʿāqilah): The group responsible for paying diyah (blood money) on behalf of a killer; traditionally the clan or registered soldiers.
المستأمن (al-mustaʾmin): A non-Muslim from Dar al-Harb who enters Dar al-Islam with safe conduct (aman) temporarily.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the Imam not permitted to pardon the killer who owes diyah for an accidental killing of an heirless Muslim?
A) Because the Imam has no authority in diyah cases.
B) Because the diyah is a public right and his authority is fiduciary.
C) Because the killer must always pay qisas.
D) Because the victim's relatives would object.

Key Takeaway
The Imam must collect diyah for a Muslim killed accidentally without heirs and cannot pardon the killer without compensation, as the diyah is a public right.

Classification of Lands into 'Ushri and Kharaji
Defines 'ushri and kharaji lands with rationale based on Prophetic practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'ushri and kharaji lands according to the Hanafi school and explain the rationale for the classification using the dalil of Prophetic practice.

Core Idea
Arab lands are 'ushri because the Prophet and caliphs never imposed kharaj on them—the silence of custom proves the rule.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah lays down the foundational Hanafi rule for classifying lands into 'ushri (subject to tithe) and kharaji (subject to land tax). The classification hinges on whether the land ever came into Muslim hands and whether it was distributed among the Muslim army. Most importantly, it reveals a crucial principle: that kharaj is not initially imposed on a Muslim. Let us unpack the legal reasoning and the evidence that supports it.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
Identify the three categories of land that are 'ushri: the land of the Arabs, lands whose inhabitants became Muslim without conquest, and lands conquered by force and distributed among the Muslim army (including Basra).
The rule is given concisely at the beginning.
2
Identify the evidence (dalil)
The proof is the absence of transmitted evidence (lam yathbut) that the Prophet ﷺ or the caliphs ever took kharaj from Arab lands. If they had, the community's custom would have transmitted it.
This is a dalil based on what is not transmitted—a 'negative evidence' argument.
3
Understand the rationale (wajh)
The rationale is that from the Arabs only Islam or the sword is accepted; they do not pay jizyah on their persons. Since kharaj on land is analogous to jizyah on persons, it is also not imposed on Arab Muslims.
Qiyas between jizyah and kharaj.
4
Apply the principle to a new case
If conquered land is distributed among Muslims (even non-conquering Muslims), it remains 'ushri. But if distributed among non-Muslims, it becomes kharaji. This flows from the principle that kharaj is not initially imposed on a Muslim.
The commentary provides this branching rule.

Chapter of Taxes: The land of the Arabs, and what its people became Muslim upon, or was conquered by force and distributed among our army, and Basra is 'ushri. [1] His statement: 'Chapter of Taxes' is a plural of 'function', meaning what is imposed and fixed upon the persons of the disbelievers, and it is called jizyah, and what is imposed upon lands, which is 'ushr and kharaj; and its discussion has already appeared in the discussion of 'ushr from the Book of Zakat. [2] His statement: 'The land of the Arabs...etc.' The purpose of it is to mention what incurs 'ushr and what incurs kharaj as a standard, so that their branches may be derived. [3] His statement: 'among our army' is a precaution against when it is distributed among a disbelieving people other than its inhabitants, for then it becomes kharaji; and if it is distributed among Muslims other than the conquering army, it becomes 'ushri. The principle regarding this is that kharaj is not initially imposed upon a Muslim; the tax on his land is only 'ushr. Rather, kharaj is specific to disbelievers, and 'ushr is initially imposed upon the Muslim because of the meaning of worship in it. [4] His statement: 'and Basra' - with fatḥ, ḍamm, and kasr of the single bā', the most eloquent and famous being the first - it is the city built by 'Utba ibn Ghazwān during the caliphate of 'Umar ibn al-Khaṭṭāb in the year seventeen; people settled it in the year eighteen, and it is part of the Sawād of Iraq, as in the Book of Lineages by Abū Sa'd al-Sam'ānī. [0] His statement: ''ushri' - as for the land of Arabs being 'ushri, meaning what is taken from its lands is 'ushr rather than kharaj, it is because it is not established that the Prophet ﷺ and his caliphs took kharaj from it. If the Prophet ﷺ or any of his companions had done so, custom would have required its transmission, even if through a weak chain. The rationale is: from the Arabs only Islam or death is accepted; there is no jizyah on their heads, so there is no kharaj on their lands, for kharaj on land is like kharaj on the head.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage opens the section on 'imposts' (al-waẓā’if), covering the head-tax (jizyah) on non-Muslims and the land-taxes of 'ushr (tithe) and kharāj (land tax). The author provides a concise rule: Arab lands—including lands whose inhabitants became Muslim without conquest, lands conquered by force and later distributed among the Muslim army, and specifically the city of Baṣrah—are 'ushrī, meaning the only tax on their produce is the 'ushr (tithe, a type of zakāh). The commentary clarifies that this classification is not arbitrary: it is based on the historical fact that the Prophet ﷺ and the Rāshidūn caliphs never imposed kharāj on the original Arab lands. If they had, the custom of the community (tawātur ‘ādatī) would have transmitted it even through weak chains. The rationale (wajh) is that the Arabs were only given the choice of Islam or the sword; they do not pay jizyah on their persons, so analogically, they do not pay kharāj on their lands—since land tax is akin to head tax. Conversely, when conquered lands are distributed among the conquering army (Muslims), they also become 'ushrī because the initial imposition of kharāj on a Muslim is not permitted; kharāj is reserved for non-Muslim landholders. However, if the same land is distributed among non-Muslims (other than its original inhabitants), it becomes kharājī. The passage thus establishes a foundational legal principle (aṣl) for distinguishing 'ushrī and kharājī lands across the Islamic empire.

Key Terms
عشر ('ushr): The tithe imposed on produce of 'ushrī lands, similar to zakāh on agricultural output.
خراج (kharāj): Land tax imposed on kharājī lands, typically held by non-Muslims or conquered without distribution.
وظائف (waẓā’if): Imposts or taxes, including jizyah on persons and kharāj/'ushr on lands.
عنوةً ('anwatan): By force, referring to conquest without peace treaty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafī school, why are Arab lands considered 'ushrī and not kharājī?
A) Because the Prophet explicitly declared them 'ushrī in a hadith
B) Because neither the Prophet nor the caliphs ever collected kharāj from them, and the custom would have transmitted it
C) Because the Arabs paid a higher jizyah in lieu of kharāj
D) Because all lands conquered by force are automatically 'ushrī

Key Takeaway
Arab lands and conquered lands distributed to Muslims are 'ushri because kharaj cannot be imposed on a Muslim; the historical practice of the Prophet and caliphs confirms this.

Defining Arab Land and Sawad al-Iraq for Kharaj Classification
Defining boundaries of Arab land and Sawad al-Iraq for tax classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the boundaries of Arab land and Sawad al-Iraq and explain why they are subject to different tax rates ('ushr vs kharaj) in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Arab land is 'ushr land; all other conquered lands are kharaj land.

This lesson clarifies a key distinction in Hanafi fiscal law: lands of the Arabian Peninsula are treated differently from other conquered territories. The passage, a commentary on al-Wiqayah, defines precise geographical boundaries for 'Arab land' and 'Sawad al-Iraq,' connecting these regions to their respective tax obligations. Understanding these definitions is essential for correctly applying kharaj and 'ushr rulings in historical and contemporary contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling on Conquered Land
The passage begins by restating the rule: land conquered by peace (sulh) is kharaj land. Recognize that kharaj is wajib, not 'ushr, and that this is based on the practice of Caliphs 'Umar and 'Uthman.
This rule applies to all conquered lands outside the Arabian Peninsula.
2
Define the Boundaries of Arab Land
The commentary provides a detailed geographical definition of 'Arab land' (the Arabian Peninsula), dividing it into five regions: Tihamah, Najd, Hijaz, 'Arid, and Yemen. Note the specific boundaries from al-'Udhayb to the farthest Hajar.
These boundaries determine which land is exempt from kharaj and subject to 'ushr.
3
Define Sawad al-Iraq
Sawad al-Iraq is defined as the cultivated land of Arab Iraq, bounded by al-'Udhayb and 'Aqabat Hulwan. Understand that 'Iraq means flat land, and the attribution 'Arab Iraq' distinguishes it from 'Ajam Iraq.
This region is kharaj land, not 'ushr land.
4
Connect Boundaries to Tax Classification
Combine the definitions: Arab land (the Arabian Peninsula) is 'ushr land; all other conquered lands, including Sawad al-Iraq, are kharaj land. Appreciate that the method of conquest (peace or force) does not change the classification for these regions—the Arabian Peninsula is uniquely treated as 'ushr.
This distinction is a specific Hanafi ruling.

The land of the Arabs: what is between al-'Udhayb and the farthest Hajar, in Yemen at Mahra, with them, not by force. [1] His saying: "Or if they made peace;" meaning the disbelievers made peace and the land was opened by peace. [2] His saying: "kharaj land;" so upon its lands is kharaj, not 'ushr, and this is the transmitted (athar) from 'Umar and his deputies, and 'Uthman and his workers, as narrated by Abu Yusuf in his book al-Kharaj. [3] His saying: "Land of the Arabs;" He said in Mukhtasar Taqwim al-Buldan as quoted in Radd al-Muhtar: "The Arabian Peninsula has five divisions: Tihamah, Najd, Hijaz, 'Arid, and Yemen. Tihamah: the southern region of Hijaz. Najd: the region between Hijaz and Iraq. Hijaz: a mountain coming from Yemen until it connects to Sham, containing Madinah and 'Amman. 'Arid: al-Yamamah to Bahrain; it was called Hijaz because it separated between Najd and al-Yamamah." Al-Waqidi said: "Hijaz is from Madinah to Tabuk, and from Madinah to the road of Kufa, and beyond that until approaching Basra is Najd, and from Madinah to the road of Makkah until reaching Habt al-'Arj is also Hijaz, and beyond that to Makkah and Jeddah is Tihamah, and what is between Iraq and Wajrah and 'Umrah of Ta'if is Najd, and what is beyond Dajrah to the sea is Tihamah, and what is between Tihamah and Najd is Hijaz." End. [4] His saying: "between al-'Udhayb;" al-Nawawi said in Tahdhib al-Asma' wa al-Lughat: it is with damm on the 'ayn and fatha on the dhal: a station for the Iraqi pilgrims near Kufa. Al-Hazimi said: it is the boundary of Sawad, and al-'Udhayb is also a place in Basra, and al-'Udhayb is water in the land of Kalb. [0] His saying: "to the farthest Hajar;" - with two fatha - meaning rock: i.e. to the furthest part of Yemen, and it is - with two fatha - a well-known region of the lands of Aden, and it is the passage of pilgrims who go from India to Makkah, so they assume ihram from Yalamlam: which is the miqat of the people of Yemen and whoever passes it, as detailed in the Book of Hajj. [1] His saying: "to the boundary of Sham;" this indicates its latitudinal boundary, and what preceded from it is its longitudinal boundary. He said in al-Kifayah: "As for latitudinally, it is between Bayrin, al-Dahna', and Rimal 'Alij to the outskirts of Sham: i.e. its villages to which the Eastern swords are attributed." [2] His saying: "Sawad of Arab Iraq;" Iraq - with kasr - is the name of a well-known region, containing many lands like Basra, Kufa, Baghdad, etc., named for the flatness of its land and its being free of mountains and valleys, for Iraq means flatness, and it has other aspects mentioned in Tahdhib al-Nawawi. Its attribution to Arabs is to distinguish from 'Ajam Iraq, which is from the west: Azerbaijan, from the south: part of Arab Iraq and Khuzestan, from the east: the desert of Khorasan and Fars, from the north: the lands of Daylam, as in Taqwim al-Buldan. [3] His saying: "to 'Aqabat Hulwan;" it is - with damm on the important ha' - the name of a town named after its builder Hulwan ibn 'Imran ibn Quda'ah. Yusuf Chalabi known as Akhi Chalabi mentioned in his glosses known as Dakhirat al-'Uqba: "Hulwan is the name of his town, and it is written on it: to it is attributed Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani among the mujtahids." End. And it is a grave mistake from him as I clarified in the Introduction, and in al-Ta'liqat al-Saniyya 'ala al-Fawa'id al-Bahiyya, and in Tadhkirat al-Rashid bi Radd Tabsirat al-Naqid, and in my prohibitions on my treatise.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on al-Wiqayah, defining the boundaries of Arab land and Sawad al-Iraq for the purpose of tax classification (kharaj vs. 'ushr). The author first explains that land conquered by peace treaty (sulh) is kharaj land, meaning its produce is subject to kharaj tax, not 'ushr. This is established from the practice of Caliphs 'Umar and 'Uthman as recorded by Abu Yusuf. Then he defines 'Arab land' (ard al-'arab) as the Arabian Peninsula, dividing it into five regions: Tihamah, Najd, Hijaz, 'Arid, and Yemen, with detailed boundaries based on geographical sources. The boundary point al-'Udhayb is identified as a station near Kufa. The farthest Hajar refers to the region of Aden in Yemen. Finally, he defines Sawad al-'Iraq (the cultivated land of Iraq) as the area between al-'Udhayb and 'Aqabat Hulwan, explaining that 'Iraq means flat land, and the attribution to Arabs distinguishes it from 'Ajam Iraq. The passage also includes a critical note correcting a mistake about Hulwan and the attribution of Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani. This is all contextual for understanding which lands are subject to which tax in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
أرض العرب (ard al-'arab): The Arabian Peninsula, defined by specific boundaries from al-'Udhayb to the farthest Hajar in Yemen.
سواد عراق العرب (sawad 'iraq al-'arab): The cultivated land of Arab Iraq, bounded by al-'Udhayb to 'Aqabat Hulwan.
خراجية (kharajiyyah): Land on which kharaj tax is imposed, as opposed to 'ushr land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, land conquered by peace treaty (sulh) is classified as what type of land?
A) 'Ushr land
B) Kharaj land
C) Both 'ushr and kharaj
D) Tax-free land

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school preserves the original tax classification of lands based on the method of conquest and region, with the Arabian Peninsula uniquely exempt from kharaj.

Boundaries of the Sawad of Iraq
Definition and correction of Sawad boundaries

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the boundaries of the Sawad of Arab Iraq and identify the correct southern limit.

Core Idea
The Sawad is the heartland of Arab Iraq, defined by specific markers that distinguish it from Persian Iraq.

This passage defines the Sawad of Arab Iraq, its width and length limits, and clarifies a common error about the attribution of the scholar Shams al-A'imma. Understanding these boundaries is crucial for later discussions on kharaj and land taxation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the region
Sawad is the fertile agricultural land of Iraq. Its name implies levelness, free from mountains and valleys.
See the etymological explanation in the passage.
2
Locate the width limit
The width limit is from al-Udhaib to 'Aqabat Hulwan, and also to the border of Syria as mentioned.
The passage indicates width is defined by al-Birin, al-Dahna', and Raml 'Alij to the outskirts of Syria.
3
Locate the length limit
The length limit is from al-Tha'labiyya or more correctly from al-'Alth, a village east of the Tigris.
The passage corrects al-Tha'labiyya as being too far south.
4
Correct the attribution error
Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani is not from the town of Hulwan; his nisbah refers to selling halwa (sweets).
This is a biographical correction important for avoiding confusion.
5
Distinguish Arab Iraq from Persian Iraq
Arab Iraq is bounded by specific territories; Persian Iraq has different limits (Azerbaijan, Khuzistan, etc.).
The addition 'of the Arabs' is to differentiate from Persian Iraq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجهاد / باب الوظائف
إلى حد الشام.
وسواد عراق العرب : ما بين العُذيب إلى عقبة حلوان من الهند إلى مكة، فيحرمون من يلملم : وهو ميقات أهل اليمن ومن مرّ عليه، على ما مر تفصيله في «كتاب الحج».
[ قوله : إلى حدّ الشّام؛ هذا إشارة إلى حده عرضاً، وما سبق منه إلى حده طولاً. قال في «الكفاية» : أما عرضاً فهو ما بين بيرين والدهناء ورمل عالج إلى مشارف الشّام : أي قراها التي تنسب إليها السيوف المشرقية.
قوله : وسواد عراق العرب؛ العراق - بالكسر – اسم لإقليم معروف ، مشتمل على بلاد كثيرة ؛ كالبصرة والكوفة وبغداد وغيرها، سُمِّيَ به لاستواء أرضه وخلوها عن جبال وأودية، فإنّ العراق بمعنى الاستواء، وله وجوه أخر مذكورة في «تهذيب النووي». وإضافته إلى العرب للاحتراز عن عراق العجم، وهو من الغرب : أذربيجان، ومن الجنوب : شيء من عراق العرب وخورستان ومن الشرق : مفازة خراسان وفارس، ومن الشمال : بلاد الديلم، كذا في «تقويم البلدان».
قوله : إلى عقبة حلوان؛ هو - بضم الحاء المهملة - : اسم لبلد سُمِّي باسم بانيه حلوان بن عمران بن قضاعة. ذكر يوسف جلبي المعروف بأخي جلبي له في حواشيه المعروفة المسمّاة بـ«ذخيرة العقبى» : «إنّ حلوان اسم بلدته، وكتب عليه منهيّة : ينسب إليه شمس الأئمة الحلواني من المجتهدين». انتهى. وهو خطأ فاحش منه كما أوضحته في «المقدّمة»، وفي «التعليقات السنية على الفوائد البهية»، وفي تذكرة الراشد برد تبصرة الناقد، وفي منهياتي على رسالتي ومكات أحيي يعتبر بقربه، وخراج وضعه عمر الله على السواد «مقدمة الهداية»، وفي مقدمة شرحي الكبير المسمّى بـ «السعاية في كشف ما في شرح الوقاية»، فإنّ شمس الأئمة ليس من بلدة حلوان، ونسبته إلى بيع الحلواء.
قوله : ومن التغلبية؛ هذا بيان لحد السواد طولاً ، وهو - بفتح الثاء المثلثة ، وسكون العين المهملة، بعدها لام مفتوحة، بعدها باء موحدة ، ثم ياء تحتانية مشدّدة - : اسم موضع.
قوله : ويقال من العلث؛ - بفتح العين المهملة، وسكون اللام ، آخره ثاء مثلثة - : قرية شرقي دجلة، وهذا القول هو الصحيح، وإن ذكره الشارح الله بصيغة : قيل. وصاحب «الهداية» بلفظ : يقال. والقول الأوّل غلط ؛ فإنَّ التَّعْلبية منزل من منازل البادية بعد العذيب بكثير ، كذا في «المغرب» و«غاية البيان» و«البناية»، وغيرها.
قوله : عبادان؛ - بفتح العين المهملة، وتشديد الباء الموحدة – : اسم حصن صغير بشط بحر فارس، وهو عن البصرة بمرحلة ونصف، كذا في «تقويم البلدان».
قوله : وموات؛ - بفتح الميم – مبتدأ موصوف بما بعده : أي أحيي بصيغة المجهول من الإحياء وخبره قوله : يعتبر بقربه. قال أبو يوسف له في كتاب «الخراج»: «سألت يا أمير المؤمنين عن الأرضين التي افتتحت عنوة أو صولح عليها أهلها، وفي بعض قراها أرض كثيرة لا يرى عليها أثر زرع، ولا بناء لأحد فيها ؟
The book of Jihad / Section on Functions
Up to the border of Syria.
And the Sawad of Arab Iraq: what is between al-Udhaib to 'Aqabat Hulwan from India to Mecca, so they enter into ihram from Yalamlam: which is the miqat of the people of Yemen and whoever passes by it, as detailed in the Book of Hajj.
[His saying: Up to the border of Syria; this indicates its width limit, and what preceded from it to its length limit. He said in al-Kifayah: As for width, it is between Birin and al-Dahna' and Raml 'Alij to the outskirts of Syria: i.e., its villages to which the Eastern swords are attributed.
His saying: And the Sawad of Arab Iraq; al-Iraq (with kasra) is a name for a known region, including many cities like Basra, Kufa, Baghdad, etc. It was named thus because of the levelness of its land and its freedom from mountains and valleys, for Iraq means levelness. It has other aspects mentioned in Tahdhib al-Nawawi. Its addition to Arabs is to distinguish it from Persian Iraq, which is from the west: Azerbaijan, from the south: part of Arab Iraq and Khuzistan, from the east: the desert of Khorasan and Fars, from the north: the land of Daylam, as in Taqwim al-Buldan.
His saying: To 'Aqabat Hulwan; it (with damm on the ha) is a name of a town named after its founder Hulwan ibn 'Imran ibn Quda'ah. Yusuf Chalabi known as Akhi Chalabi mentioned in his marginalia known as Dhakhirat al-'Uqba: 'Hulwan is the name of his town, and it is written upon it as a warning: Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani, one of the mujtahids, is attributed to it.' End. It is a gross error from him as I clarified in al-Muqaddimah, and in al-Ta'liqat al-Sunniyya 'ala al-Fawa'id al-Bahiyya, and in Tadhkirat al-Rashid bi Radd Tabasirat al-Naqid, and in my marginalia on my treatise and dead land revived is considered by its proximity, and the kharaj that 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) imposed on the Sawad, Muqaddimat al-Hidaya, and in the introduction to my large commentary called al-Sa'aya fi Kashf ma fi Sharh al-Wiqaya, for Shams al-A'imma is not from the town of Hulwan; his attribution is to selling halwa.
His saying: And from al-Tha'labiyya; this is an explanation of the length limit of the Sawad, and it (with fatha on the tha, sukun on the 'ayn, fatha on the lam, then ba with one dot, then shadda on the ya) is a place name.
His saying: And it is said from al-'Alth; (with fatha on the 'ayn, sukun on the lam, final tha) is a village east of the Tigris. This saying is correct, though the commentator (Allah's mercy) mentioned it with the form of 'qil'. And the author of al-Hidaya with the wording 'yuqal'. The first saying is wrong, for al-Tha'labiyya is a desert station far beyond al-Udhaib, as in al-Mughrib, Ghayat al-Bayan, al-Binaya, and others.
His saying: 'Abbadan; (with fatha on the 'ayn, shadda on the ba) is a name of a small fortress on the shore of the Persian sea, about a stage and a half from Basra, as in Taqwim al-Buldan.
His saying: And dead land; (with fatha on the mim) is a mubtada' described by what follows: meaning revived (passive) from revival, and its khabar is 'is considered by its proximity'. Abu Yusuf said in Kitab al-Kharaj: 'I asked, O Commander of the Faithful, about the lands that were conquered by force or reconciled with their people, and in some of their villages there is much land with no trace of cultivation or construction upon it?
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the boundaries of the Sawad of Iraq and a ruling concerning dead land (موات). The author first defines the Sawad as the region between al-Udhaib and 'Aqabat Hulwan, clarifying that this is the width limit, while the length limit is given later. The term 'Iraq' is explained as meaning level land, free of mountains, and the addition 'of the Arabs' distinguishes it from Persian Iraq. The author corrects a biographical error regarding Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani, asserting that he is not from the town of Hulwan but rather attributed to selling sweets (halwa). The focus then shifts to dead land (موات), stating that when such land is revived, proximity to existing land is taken into account. The author quotes Abu Yusuf's question to 'Umar about uncultivated land in conquered territories, indicating that the ruling on dead land is based on the practice of the early caliphs. The passage confirms that the correct boundary of the Sawad to the south is al-'Alth, not al-Tha'labiyya, and that 'Abbadan is a fortress in the south. The ruling on dead land is a key principle in Hanafi fiqh regarding land ownership and taxation.

Key Terms
سواد (sawād): The fertile agricultural land of Iraq, specifically the region of Arab Iraq bounded by specific markers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) al-Tha'labiyya
B) al-'Alth
C) 'Abbadan
D) al-'Udhaib

Key Takeaway
The Sawad of Arab Iraq is bounded by al-Udhaib, 'Aqabat Hulwan, the Syrian border, and al-'Alth, with 'Abbadan as the southernmost point.

Ruling on Reviving Dead Land (Mawat)
Definition and ruling on dead land revival

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on reviving dead land and the condition of proximity.

Core Idea
Reviving dead land grants ownership, with proximity to existing land as a key condition.

This passage defines dead land (موات) and states that its revival is considered with proximity. The ruling is based on Abu Yusuf's inquiry to 'Umar about uncultivated lands. Understanding this ruling is fundamental to Hanafi land ownership.

Guided Path
1
Define Mawat
Mawat is dead land: uncultivated, unowned land not attached to any settlement or already-owned land.
The term is derived from the word for death, indicating it is not alive with cultivation.
2
Identify the ruling
Reviving mawat gives ownership to the reviver, but proximity to existing cultivated land is a consideration.
The passage says 'يعتبر بقربه' (it is considered by its proximity).
3
Trace the basis
The ruling originates from the practice of 'Umar as reported by Abu Yusuf in Kitab al-Kharaj.
Abu Yusuf asked 'Umar about uncultivated land in conquered territories.
4
Note the Hanafi position
Hanafis do not require the imam's permission for revival; proximity to one's own land is sufficient.
The passage implies that if the land is near someone's existing land, they have priority.
5
Contrast with other schools
The Shafi'i school requires the imam's explicit permission to revive mawat.
This ikhtilaf affects the ownership process.

Ruling on Reviving Dead Land and its Tax Classification
Commentary on al-Hidayah on revival of dead land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani on ownership of revived dead land and explain the basis for each view, and apply the criteria for determining whether the land is 'ushri or kharaji.

Core Idea
Reviving dead land gives ownership, but Hanafi imams differ on whether the Imam's permission is required.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah examines the revival of dead land, a key issue in Islamic land law. The central dispute is whether the reviver automatically owns the land or requires the Imam's approval. Additionally, the text explains how the tax status ('ushr or kharaj) of revived land is determined, with Abu Yusuf relying on proximity and Muhammad on the water source.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of dead land
Understand the definition of mawat: land without cultivation, building, inheritance, or public use. This is the fundamental concept that triggers the rulings.
The text defines mawat explicitly: no trace of building or cultivation, not a courtyard, cemetery, or pasture.
2
State the basic ownership positions
All Hanafi imams agree that reviving dead land establishes ownership, but Abu Hanifa conditions it on Imam's permission while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad do not.
Abu Hanifa's view is precautionary; Abu Yusuf cites the hadith directly.
3
Analyze the proofs
Examine the hadith 'Whoever revives dead land, it is his' narrated by Abu Yusuf. Note that Abu Hanifa's concern for public interest (maslaha) leads him to require Imam's permission.
The hadith is ahad (zanni) but is used as primary evidence by Abu Yusuf.
4
Determine the tax classification criteria
Compare Abu Yusuf's proximity rule (land takes status of adjacent area) with Muhammad's water-source rule (if irrigated by new wells/rivers/rain it is 'ushri; if by Persian canals it is kharaji).
The text notes that if land is neither near 'ushri nor kharaji, proximity is the default; Muhammad's method is an alternative.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The text favors Abu Yusuf's position on ownership (no Imam's permission needed) as the relied-upon view, because the hadith is clear and there is no harm to others.
For tax, both views are transmitted; the passage does not explicitly prefer one over the other.

[١] قوله : صاع من بر أو شعير؛ يعني الإمام مخير في أخذه أحدهما، وفي «الكافي»: يأخذه مما يزرع في تلك الأرض، والمراد بالدرهم : هو ما يكون معتبراً في وزن سبعة، وقد مرّ تفصيله مع تعيين مقدار الصاع في «كتاب الزكاة».
And dead land revived is considered by its proximity, and the kharaj which 'Umar placed on the Sawad from al-Tha'labiyya, and it is said from al-'Alath to 'Abbadan (1). (And dead land revived is considered by its proximity, and the kharaj placed by 'Umar on the Sawad — Muqaddimat al-Hidaya (2), and in the introduction of my large commentary called 'al-Sa'aya fi Kashf ma fi Sharh al-Wiqaya' (3), for Shams al-A'imma is not from the town of Hulwan, and his attribution is to selling halwa. 1] His saying: 'and from al-Tha'labiyya': this is an explanation of the length border of Sawad, and it is – with fatha of the tha, sukun of the 'ayn, after it a lam with fatha, after it a ba with tashdid, then a ya with tashdid – a name of a place. [2] His saying: 'and it is said from al-'Alath': – with fatha of the 'ayn, sukun of the lam, its end is a tha – a village east of the Tigris, and this saying is correct, even though the commentator mentioned it in the form of 'qila'. And the author of al-Hidaya (4) with the wording 'yuqalu'. And the first saying is mistaken; for al-Tha'labiyya is a station of the desert after al-'Udhayb by a great distance, as in al-Mughrib (5) and Ghayat al-Bayan and al-Binaya (6) and others. [3] His saying: 'Abbadan: – with fatha of the 'ayn and tashdid of the ba – a name of a small fortress on the shore of the sea of Fars, and it is from Basra one and a half stages, as in Taqwim al-Buldan. [4] His saying: 'and dead land (mawat)': – with fatha of the mim – a mubtada modified by what follows it: that is, revived (uhyi) in the passive form from ihya, and its khabar is his saying: 'is considered by its proximity'. Abu Yusuf said in his book al-Kharaj: 'I asked, O Commander of the Faithful, about the lands which were conquered by force or whose people made peace, and in some of their villages there is abundant land on which no trace of cultivation is seen, nor any building of anyone in it? For every jarib that water reaches, a sa' of wheat or barley and a dirham. For every jarib that water reaches, a sa' of wheat or barley and a dirham. That. Then if there is in these lands no trace of building nor cultivation, and there is no courtyard of the village people, nor a place of their cemetery, nor a place of their firewood, nor a place of pasture of their animals and sheep, and it is not owned by anyone, and not in anyone's hand, then it is dead land (mawat). So whoever revives something from it, it is his. And you may give a grant to whomever you like, and you may lease it and work in it for improvement. And everyone who revives dead land, it is his.' And Abu Hanifa used to say: 'Everyone who revives dead land, it is his if the Imam permits it. And whoever revives dead land without the Imam's permission, it is not his, and the Imam has the right to remove it from his hand and place in it what he sees.' End. (1) Then Abu Yusuf narrated with his multiple chains from the Prophet that he said: 'Whoever revives dead land, it is his.' (2) And he established that if there is no harm to anyone in the dead land, then it is owned by the one who revived it by the permission of the Messenger, without need for the Imam's permission. Then if the dead land is among the 'ushri lands which have been mentioned, like the land of Arabs and others, then upon its owner is the 'ushr. And if it is among the kharaji lands, then upon its owner is the kharaj. And if it is neither of these, then the consideration is proximity. So if it is near to 'ushri land, then it is 'ushri; and if it is near to kharaji land, then it is kharaji. And according to Muhammad, the consideration in such a case is the irrigation water. So if he revives it by a well he dug, or a spring he extracted, or the water of the Tigris and Euphrates and large rivers that no one owns, then it is 'ushri. Similarly if he revives it by rainwater. And if he revives it by water from rivers that the Persians dug, then it is kharaji. And its detail has already passed in the Book of Zakat. [1] His saying: 'a sa' of wheat or barley'; meaning the Imam has a choice to take either. And in al-Kafi: he takes it from what is cultivated in that land. And the meaning of the dirham is that which is noteworthy in weight of seven. And its detail with the determination of the amount of the sa' has already passed in the Book of Zakat.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute and commentary on the Hidayah regarding the revival of dead land (ihya' al-mawat) and its tax implications. The text begins with geographical clarifications of the Sawad region, defining its borders from al-Tha'labiyya to 'Abbadan, correcting earlier misconceptions. The core legal point is the ruling on mawat: land with no trace of cultivation, building, or ownership. Abu Yusuf, citing his book al-Kharaj, relates that he consulted the caliph about such lands. The caliph's response (attributed to 'Umar or Abu Bakr) is that whoever revives such land owns it, and the caliph may grant or lease it. However, Abu Hanifa conditioned ownership on the Imam's permission, without which the reviver does not own it and the Imam may take it back. Abu Yusuf then cites the hadith 'Whoever revives dead land, it is his' to argue that the Prophet's permission suffices and no Imam's permission is needed, provided no harm is caused to others. The tax on the revived land depends on its classification: if it is near 'ushri lands (like Arab lands), it pays 'ushr; if near kharaji lands, it pays kharaj; if neither, proximity determines. Muhammad al-Shaybani differs, stating that the criterion is the water source: if irrigated by new wells, springs, or major rivers (like Tigris/Euphrates) or rainwater, it is 'ushri; if by canals dug by non-Arabs, it is kharaji. The passage concludes with a note on the tax amount (one sa' of wheat/barley plus a dirham per jarib) and references the Book of Zakat for details.

Key Terms
موات (mawāt): Dead land: land with no trace of cultivation, building, or ownership, not used by nearby inhabitants.
إحياء (iḥyā’): Revival of dead land by making it cultivable or building on it.
خراج (kharāj): Land tax imposed on conquered lands (like Sawad) paid per area.
عشر ('ushr): Tithe: one-tenth of produce, paid from 'ushri lands (e.g., Arab lands).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the revival of dead land?
A) Abu Hanifa requires Imam's permission; Abu Yusuf does not.
B) Abu Hanifa says it is haram; Abu Yusuf says it is wajib.
C) Abu Hanifa says tax is 'ushr; Abu Yusuf says it is kharaj.
D) Abu Hanifa says the reviver must pay the Imam; Abu Yusuf says no payment.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that reviving dead land establishes ownership, but the three founding imams differ on the condition of Imam's permission and on the tax classification criteria.
