Jizyah Exemptions: Minors, Women, Slaves, Disabled, and Poor
Listing jizyah exemptions with proofs and disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on jizyah exemption by identifying the exempt categories and explaining the rationales based on qiyas and the practice of Uthman ibn Hunayf.

Core Idea
Jizyah is an exchange for military support; those incapable of fighting or earning are exempt.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary enumerates those exempt from jizyah, grounded in the principle that jizyah is a substitute for killing and for providing military support. It lists children, women, slaves, the blind, the chronically ill, and the poor who cannot earn, providing reasons for each. The author also addresses an intra-Hanafi difference with Abu Yusuf and a cross-madhhab dispute with the Shafi'i school, using qiyas (analogy with kharaj) and historical practice as evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exempt categories
List each category mentioned: child, woman, slave (including mukatab, mudabbar, umm walad), blind, chronically ill (zamin, including paralyzed and very old), and poor who cannot earn.
2
Understand the rationale (ta'lil) for each
For children, women, and disabled: they are not killed or expected to fight. For slaves: they cannot provide military support. For the poor who cannot earn: they lack capacity to pay, analogous to barren land not bearing kharaj.
3
Examine the qiyas dalil
The Hanafi school uses qiyas: just as kharaj is not imposed on land without capacity, jizyah (kharaj al-ra's) is not imposed on a person without earning ability.
The dalil is drawn from the practice of Uthman ibn Hunayf, who placed jizyah only on those who work (mu'tamil).
4
Analyze the ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf holds jizyah on a poor person if he has wealth, even if he cannot earn. The Shafi'i school imposes jizyah on every free adult male non-Muslim except women, children, slaves, and the insane, including the poor who can earn. Note the Hanafi response: the hadith cited by Shafi'i is interpreted as applying to peace treaties, and it excludes children and the poor.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon Hanafi position exempts the poor who cannot earn at all, following Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, supported by the practice of Uthman ibn Hunayf. Al-Hidayah favors this majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصبي، وامراة، ومملوك، وأعمى، وزمن، وفقير لا يكسب. قوله: وصبي...الخ: أما عدم وجوب الجزية على الصبي وكذا المجنون، وعلى المرأة؛ فلأنها وجبت بدلاً عن القتل في حق المأخوذ منه، وعن النصرة في حقنا، وهما لا يقتلان، ولا يقاتلان لعدم الأهلية، وعدم وجوبها على مملوك وإن كان مكاتباً أو مدبراً أو ابن أم ولد؛ فلأن العبيد لا يقدرون على النصرة فلا يجب عليهم بدلها، فإن الجزية بدل عن النصرة في حقنا. فإن قلت: فهلاً يجب على الموالي أداء جزيتهم. قلت: للزوم وجوب الجزية عليهم مرتين. وأما عدم وجوبها على الأعمى والزمن وكذا المفلوج والشيخ الكبير؛ فلأنهم لا يقتلون ولا يقاتلون. كذا في الهداية وحواشيها. قوله: زمن؛ يقال: زمن يزمن زمانة، فهو زمن: نقص بعض أعضائه أو تعطلت قواه، فيدخل فيه المفلوج والشيخ العاجز. قوله: تجب؛ لأنه يقتل في الجملة إذا كان ذا رأي في الحرب. وعند أبي يوسف تجب إذا كان له مال. قوله: وفقير لا يكسب؛ أي لا يقدر على الكسب والعمل. والوجه فيه: إن خراج الأرض لا يوظف على أرض لا طاقة لها، فكذا خراج الرأس. ويشهد له: إن عثمان بن حنيف حين وجهه عمر بن الخطاب إلى سواد العراق إنما وضع الجزية على المعتمل، ولم يثبت أنه وضعها على غير المعتمل. وأما حديث: 'وضع الجزية على كل حالم وحالمة'، وهو الذي استند بإطلاقه الشافعي، فقد مر أنه محمول على الصلح بدليل ذكر الحالمة فيه على أنه كما خص منه الصبي خص منه الفقير.
And [jizyah is not on] a child, a woman, a slave, a blind person, a chronically ill person, and a poor person who cannot earn. His statement: 'and a child... etc': As for the non-obligation of jizyah on a child and likewise a madman, and on a woman; because jizyah became obligatory as an alternative to killing for the one taken from, and as an alternative to support for us, and they are not killed and do not fight due to lack of capacity. And the non-obligation on a slave, even if he is a mukatab, mudabbar, or umm walad; because slaves are unable to support, so no alternative is due on them; indeed jizyah is an alternative for support in our view. If you say: Why is it not obligatory on the masters to pay their jizyah? I say: That would necessitate the obligation of jizyah on them twice. As for the non-obligation on the blind, the chronically ill, and likewise the paralyzed and the very old; because they are not killed and do not fight. Thus in al-Hidayah and its supercommentaries. His statement: 'chronically ill'; it is said: zamina yazmanu zamanatan, so he is zamin: deficiency of some limbs or loss of his abilities, so includes the paralyzed and the helpless old. His statement: 'he gives'; because he is killed in general if he has an opinion in war. According to Abu Yusuf, it is obligatory if he has wealth. His statement: 'and a poor person who cannot earn'; i.e., he cannot earn and work. The reasoning: Just as kharaj is not imposed on land that has no capacity, similarly the head tax. Evidence for this: When Umar ibn al-Khattab sent Uthman ibn Hunayf to the Sawad of Iraq, he only placed jizyah on the one who works, and it is not established that he placed it on the one who does not work. As for the hadith: 'Jizyah is placed on every adult male and female' which is the basis for al-Shafi'i's absolute application, it has already been mentioned that it is interpreted as referring to peace treaties, evidenced by the mention of females, and just as it excludes children, it excludes the poor.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage details categories exempt from jizyah, based on the principle that jizyah is a substitute for killing (for the dhimmi) and for providing military support (for the state). The author lists: children, women, slaves (even if mukatab, mudabbar, or umm walad), the blind, the chronically ill (zamin, including paralyzed and very old), and the poor who cannot earn. The reason for each is either lack of eligibility to fight (women, children, disabled) or inability to support the state (slaves, poor). A potential objection about masters paying for slaves is refuted: it would double the obligation. The commentary then addresses a dispute: According to Abu Yusuf, jizyah is obligatory on a poor person if he has wealth (even if he cannot earn). The Shafi'i view that jizyah is on every adult is countered by citing the practice of Uthman ibn Hunayf in Iraq, who placed jizyah only on those who could work, not on the incapacitated. A supporting hadith is interpreted as applying to peace treaties, not general obligation.

Key Terms
زمن (zamin): A person with a chronic illness or disability preventing work or fighting; includes paralysis and old age.
خراج الرأس (kharaj al-ra's): Another term for jizyah (poll tax), analogized to kharaj (land tax).
معتمل (mu'tamil): One who works or is capable of work; jizyah is placed on such a person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Being a slave
C) Being over 60 years old and healthy

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, jizyah is not a universal tax; it is conditioned on the ability to fight or earn, exempting children, women, slaves, the disabled, and the poor who cannot earn.

Exemptions from Jizya: Who is Not Required to Pay
Explaining the categories exempt from jizya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six categories exempt from jizya in the Hanafi school and explain the reasoning and intra/inter-madhhab disputes.

Core Idea
Jizya is not imposed on those who cannot fight or earn — incapacity removes liability.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains which individuals are exempt from jizya in Hanafi law. The exemptions are based on the principle that jizya substitutes for military support, and those lacking capacity to fight are not required to pay. We will examine the six exempt categories, the underlying legal reasoning, and the disagreements with Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exempt categories
List the six groups explicitly mentioned: minors, women, slaves, blind, chronically ill (zaman), and poor who cannot earn. Note that 'zaman' includes paralyzed and helpless elderly.
2
Understand the rationale for each exemption
For each category, explain why jizya is not wajib. Common theme: jizya is a substitute for killing (protection) and support (military service). Minors, women, and slaves lack capacity to fight; blind and chronically ill are similarly incapable. The poor cannot earn, so levying jizya is like taxing barren land.
The passage uses the phrase 'لا يقتلون ولا يقاتلون' for the incapacitated.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Yusuf holds that jizya is obligatory on a poor person if he has wealth. This limits the exemption to those who are both poor and unable to earn.
The mufta bihi is the unconditioned exemption, but some later scholars consider Abu Yusuf's view.
4
Analyze the inter-madhhab dispute
Al-Shafi'i relies on the general hadith 'jizya on every adult male and female' to argue that even a poor person must pay. The Hanafi response: the hadith is restricted to a peace treaty context, as evidenced by mentioning women (who are otherwise exempt).
The Hanafis also use istihsan: analogizing to land tax not imposed on barren land.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi position is strengthened by the practice of Uthman ibn Hunayf, who only imposed jizya on those who work. This is a strong precedent from the Companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصبي، وامراة، ومملوك، وأعمى، وزمن، وفقير لا يكسب. وعند أبي يوسف له تجب إذا كان له مال، وعند الشَّافِعِي له تجب. قوله: وصبي...الخ؛ أما عدم وجوب الجزية على الصبي وكذا المجنون، وعلى المرأة؛ فلأنها وجبت بدلاً عن القتل في حق المأخوذ منه، وعن النصرة في حقنا، وهما لا يقتلان، ولا يقاتلان لعدم الأهلية. وعدم وجوبها على مملوك وإن كان مكاتباً أو مدبراً أو ابن أم ولد؛ فلأن العبيد لا يقدرون على النصرة فلا يجب عليهم بدلها، فإن الجزية بدل عن النصرة في حقنا. فإن قلت: فهلاً يجب على الموالي أداء جزيتهم. قلت: للزوم وجوب الجزية عليهم مرتين. وأما عدم وجوبها على الأعمى والزمن وكذا المفلوج والشيخ الكبير؛ فلأنهم لا يقتلون ولا يقاتلون. كذا في «الهداية» وحواشيها. قوله: زمن؛ يقال: زمن يزمن زمانة، فهو زمن: نقص بعض أعضائه أو تعطلت قواه، فيدخل فيه المفلوج والشيخ العاجز. قوله: تجب؛ لأنه يقتل في الجملة إذا كان ذا رأي في الحرب. قوله: وفقير لا يكسب؛ أي لا يقدر على الكسب والعمل. والوجه فيه: إن خراج الأرض لا يوظف على أرض لا طاقة لها، فكذا خراج الرأس. ويشهد له: إن عثمان بن حنيف رضي الله عنه حين وجهه عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه إلى سواد العراق إنما وضع الجزية على المعتمل، ولم يثبت أنه وضعها على غير المعتمل. وأما حديث: «وضع الجزية على كل حالم وحالمة»، وهو الذي استند بإطلاقه الشافعي رحمه الله، فقد مر أنه محمول على الصلح بدليل ذكر الحالمة فيه على أنه كما خص منه الأعمى ونحوه منه يخص الفقير الغير المعتمل.
A minor, a woman, a slave, a blind person, a chronically ill person, and a poor person who cannot earn. According to Abu Yusuf, jizya is obligatory on him if he has wealth. According to al-Shafi'i, it is obligatory on him. His saying: 'A minor... etc' — as for the non-obligation of jizya on the minor, similarly the insane, and on the woman — because it became obligatory as a substitute for killing on the part of the one taken from, and for support on our part, and they (minors and women) are not killed and do not fight due to lack of capacity. And its non-obligation on a slave, even if he is a mukatab, mudabbar, or umm walad — because slaves are unable to provide support, so its substitute is not obligatory on them, for jizya is a substitute for support on our part. If you say: why is it not obligatory on the masters to pay their jizya? I say: because it would necessitate the obligation of jizya on them twice. As for its non-obligation on the blind and the chronically ill, similarly the paralyzed and the very old — because they are not killed and do not fight. Thus in al-Hidayah and its glosses. His saying: 'chronically ill' — it is said: zamina yazmanu zamanatan, so he is zaman: a defect in some of his limbs or his faculties are incapacitated, so it includes the paralyzed and the helpless elderly. His saying: 'obligatory' — because he is killed in general if he has an opinion in war. His saying: 'a poor person who cannot earn' — i.e., he cannot gain and work. The reasoning for it: if land tax is not imposed on land that has no capacity, then similarly head tax. And this is witnessed by the fact that Uthman ibn Hunayf, may Allah be pleased with him, when Umar ibn al-Khattab, may Allah be pleased with him, sent him to the Sawad of Iraq, only imposed jizya on those who work, and it is not established that he imposed it on those who do not work. As for the hadith: 'Jizya is placed on every adult male and adult female,' which al-Shafi'i relied upon in its general application, it has been mentioned that it is interpreted as referring to a peace treaty (sulh) by the evidence of mentioning the adult female in it, and just as the blind and similar are excluded from it, so the poor person who does not work is excluded.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school regarding exemptions from jizya. The author lists six categories of individuals who are not required to pay jizya: minors, women, slaves, the blind, the chronically ill, and the poor who cannot earn. The reasoning is that jizya is a substitute for protection and military support; these individuals lack the capacity to fight or provide support. The author also notes two points of disagreement: (1) Abu Yusuf holds that if a poor person has wealth, jizya is obligatory on him. (2) Imam al-Shafi'i holds that jizya is obligatory on a poor person who cannot earn, relying on the general hadith 'jizya on every adult male and female.' The Hanafi response is that this hadith refers to a specific peace treaty and is not general; moreover, the poor who cannot work are analogous to the blind and chronically ill who are explicitly exempt. The practice of Uthman ibn Hunayf under Caliph Umar further supports the Hanafi position, as jizya was only imposed on those capable of work.

Key Terms
جزية (jizyah): A poll tax imposed on free non-Muslim males under Muslim rule in exchange for protection and exemption from military service.
حالم (haalim): An adult who has reached puberty; literally 'one who dreams' (i.e., has wet dreams).
خراج الرأس (kharāj al-ra's): Another name for jizya, meaning 'head tax'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is NOT exempt from jizya?
A) A blind man
B) A poor person who cannot earn
C) A rich merchant
D) A woman

Key Takeaway
Jizya exemptions in Hanafi law are based on the principle that jizya replaces military service, so those incapable of fighting or earning are exempt.

Jizya Falls Due to Death or Islam
Ruling with proof: jizya falls due to death or Islam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that jizya falls due to death or Islam, cite the hadith proof, and explain the response to the Shafi'i analogy.

Core Idea
Jizya is a punishment for disbelief; when the cause ends, the punishment ends.

This passage clarifies a key distinction between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding jizya. While Shafi'is view jizya as a debt akin to rent, the Hanafi school relies on a hadith and rational argument to show it is cancelled by death or Islam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage states that jizya falls (tasqut) due to death and Islam. This is the Hanafi position.
2
State the Shafi'i opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that jizya remains a debt after conversion or death, because it is compensation for protection received.
Their dalil is qiyas to rent (ijarah).
3
Examine the Hanafi dalil
The Hanafi proof is the hadith 'There is no jizya upon a Muslim' (narrated by Abu Dawud and others), which by its generality negates any jizya obligation on a Muslim, including obligations accrued before conversion.
Sufyan al-Thawri's interpretation confirms this meaning.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response counters the Shafi'i analogy by stating that jizya is a punishment for disbelief, not a rent. Punishment ceases when the cause ceases (Islam or death).
The purpose of worldly punishment is to repel evil, which is already achieved by death/Islam.
5
Determine the practical difference
The thamarah al-khilaf: Shafi'is would collect jizya from the convert's estate; Hanafis would not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتسقط بالموتِ والإسلام، وتتداخل. قوله: فيهما؛ فإن من عليه جزية إذا أسلم أو مات قبل أدائها لا تسقط عنه عنده؛ لأنها وجبت بدلاً عن حقن الدم أو عن السكنى، وقد وصل إليه المعوض، فلا يسقط عنه العوض، بل يؤخذ عنه وإن أسلم، ومن ماله إذا مات؛ كما إذا استأجر ذمي داراً ثم أسلم أو مات لا تسقط عنه أجرة الدار لوصول المعوض، وهو منافع الدار إليه. ولنا: حديث: «ليس على مسلم جزية»، أخرجه أبو داود، وسئل سفيان الثوري عن معناه فقال: يعني إذا أسلم فلا جزية عليه، وبهذا اللفظ رواه الطبراني مرفوعاً، فهذا بعمومه يوجب سقوط ما استحق عليه قبل الإسلام، بل هو المراد منه بخصوصه، فإنه محل الفائدة؛ إذ عدم الجزية على المسلم ابتداء من ضروريات الدين. والسر فيه: إن الجزية عقوبة على الكفر، والعقوبة على الكفر تسقط بالإسلام، ولا تقام بعد الموت؛ كيف لا؟ فإن شرع العقوبة في الدنيا ليس إلا لدفع الشر، وقد اندفع بالموت والإسلام فلا يبقى وجوبها عليه. كذا في «فتح القدير» و«الهداية». قوله: وتتداخل؛ يعني من لم تؤخذ منه الجزية في سنة حتى جاءت سنة أخرى لم تؤخذ منه إلا جزية واحدة عنده، خلافاً لهما، وعلى هذا الخلاف خراج الأرض. وقيل: لا تداخل فيه اتفاقاً؛ لأن خراج الأرض في حالة البقاء لا يلتفت فيه إلى جهة العقوبة؛ ولهذا يجب على المسلم إذا اشترى أرضاً خراجية فجاز أن لا يتداخل.
And it (jizya) falls due to death and Islam, and it does not accumulate. His saying: 'in them both' — for whoever is subject to jizya, if he becomes Muslim or dies before paying it, it does not fall away according to him (al-Shafi'i), because it became obligatory as a substitute for protection of blood or residence, and the compensated matter has been received by him, so the substitute does not fall away, rather it is taken from him even if he becomes Muslim, and from his wealth if he dies; just as if a dhimmi rents a house then becomes Muslim or dies, the rent of the house does not fall away because the compensated matter, which is the benefits of the house, has been received. And our proof: the hadith: 'There is no jizya upon a Muslim', narrated by Abu Dawud, and Sufyan al-Thawri was asked about its meaning and said: 'It means when he becomes Muslim, there is no jizya upon him.' And with this wording al-Tabarani narrated it as a marfu' hadith. So this, by its generality, necessitates the dropping of what was due upon him before Islam; indeed it is specifically intended for this, because it is the point of benefit; since the absence of jizya on a Muslim from the beginning is from the necessities of the religion. The secret behind it is that jizya is a punishment for disbelief, and punishment for disbelief falls away with Islam, and it is not carried out after death; how could it be? For the legislation of punishment in this world is only to repel evil, and it has been repelled by death and Islam, so its obligation upon him does not remain. Thus in Fath al-Qadir and al-Hidayah. His saying: 'and it does not accumulate' — meaning whoever does not have jizya taken from him in one year until another year comes, only one jizya is taken from him according to him (Abu Hanifa), contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And upon this disagreement is the land tax (kharaj al-ard). And it is said: there is no accumulation in it by agreement; because land tax, in its continuing state, does not regard the aspect of punishment; and therefore it is obligatory on a Muslim if he buys a kharaji land, and thus it may not accumulate.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage contains two related rulings: (1) Jizya is cancelled by death or conversion to Islam. The author contrasts the Hanafi position with that of Imam al-Shafi'i. Shafi'i argues by analogy with rent: just as rent remains due if the tenant dies or converts, jizya remains a debt. The Hanafi response relies on the explicit hadith 'There is no jizya upon a Muslim,' which indicates that any prior obligation falls. The rational basis is that jizya is a punishment for disbelief, and punishment ends when the cause ends (Islam or death). (2) Jizya does not accumulate from year to year (tadakhul). This is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that only one year's jizya is due even if multiple years pass unpaid, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that it accumulates as a debt. The author states that kharaj (land tax) is analogous: if it is not paid for multiple years, it does not accumulate; this is agreed upon for kharaj because it is not a punishment.

Key Terms
سقوط (suqūt): The falling away or cancellation of a legal obligation.
جزية (jizya): The poll tax imposed on non-Muslims (dhimmis) under Islamic rule.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is the primary evidence the Hanafi school uses to prove that jizya falls due to conversion to Islam?
A) The analogy of jizya with rent
B) The hadith 'There is no jizya upon a Muslim'
C) The consensus of the Companions
D) The principle of istihsan

Key Takeaway
Jizya is cancelled by death or conversion to Islam because it is a punishment for disbelief that ends when its cause ends.

Jizya Does Not Accumulate Over Years
Intra-Hanafi dispute on accumulation of jizya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that jizya does not accumulate (tadakhul) over years and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Jizya does not stack — only one year's jizya is due even if multiple years pass unpaid.

The second part of the passage addresses whether unpaid jizya accumulates as a debt over multiple years. There is an intra-Hanafi disagreement: Abu Hanifa holds it does not, while his two disciples consider it a debt that accumulates. The author also notes the analogous ruling for kharaj (land tax).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The passage states that if jizya is not taken in one year until another year comes, only one jizya is taken according to Abu Hanifa. This is the ruling of non-accumulation (tadakhul).
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagree.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that jizya does not accumulate; only one year's jizya is due even if multiple years pass without payment.
Rationale: jizya is a punishment, and punishments are not cumulative.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that jizya accumulates as a debt over the years.
They view jizya as a financial obligation akin to other debts.
4
Understand the rationale for non-accumulation
The text notes that jizya is a punishment, and punishment is not designed to accumulate. In contrast, kharaj (land tax) is not a punishment, so theoretically it might accumulate, but the text says there is agreement that kharaj also does not accumulate.
5
Apply the same dispute to kharaj
The author states that the same khilaf applies to kharaj, but some say there is agreement that kharaj does not accumulate because it is not a punishment.
Kharaj is obligatory on Muslim buyers of kharaji land, so it cannot be punishment-based.

Falling of Jizya with Death or Islam: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Commentary on matn with Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on jizya falling upon death or Islam, explain the Shafi'i counter-argument based on qiyas, and evaluate the Hanafi proof using hadith and the rationale of punishment.

Core Idea
Jizya falls upon death or Islam because it is a punishment for kufr, not a rental fee.

This passage addresses a classic inter-madhhab dispute: does a dhimmi's unpaid jizya remain due after he dies or converts to Islam? The Hanafi school, relying on a direct hadith and the characterization of jizya as a worldly punishment for disbelief, holds that it drops entirely. Imam al-Shafi'i, however, analogizes jizya to rent for protection, arguing that the obligation persists because the benefit was already received. We will unpack both positions and their proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school rules that jizya falls (tasqut) upon the death or Islam of the dhimmi; no payment is due even if it had accrued before the event.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani as recorded in al-Hidayah and Fath al-Qadir.
2
Understand the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues by qiyas: jizya is a substitute (badal) for protection of blood and residence, so like rent, it remains due after the compensated benefit is received.
A common analogy: if a dhimmi rents a house then converts, the rent for past occupancy is still owed.
3
Examine the Hanafi dalil
The Hanafi proof is the hadith: 'There is no jizya upon a Muslim' (Abu Dawood, Tabarani), interpreted by Sufyan al-Thawri to mean that jizya drops upon becoming Muslim. Its generality covers both past and future obligations.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear (qati al-dalala) on the issue.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rationale
Jizya is fundamentally a punishment (uquba) for kufr. Punishment in this world is intended to repel evil; when the cause (kufr) ends through death or Islam, the evil is repelled and the punishment ceases.
This rational argument (ma'qul) reinforces the hadith and distinguishes jizya from compensatory payments.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
According to Hanafis, any unpaid jizya is waived; the state cannot collect it from the convert or the deceased's estate. Shafi'is require collection even after conversion or from the estate.
The difference has real fiscal implications for the Islamic treasury (bayt al-mal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتسقط بالموتِ والإسلام)، خلافاً للشَّافِعِي له فيهما . (وتتداخل)

قوله : فيهما ؛ فإن من عليه جزية إذا أسلم أو مات قبل أدائها لا تسقط عنه عنده ؛ لأنها وجبت بدلاً عن حقن الدم أو عن السكني ، وقد وصل إليه المعوض، فلا يسقط عنه العوض، بل يؤخذ عنه وإن أسلم، ومن ماله إذا مات ؛ كما إذا استأجر ذمي داراً ثمّ أسلم أو مات لا تسقط عنه أجرة الدّار لوصول المعوض، وهو منافع الدار إليه.

ولنا : حديث : (ليس على مسلم جزية) ، أخرجه أبو داود، وسئل سفيان الثوري عن معناه فقال : يعني إذا أسلم فلا جزية عليه(۳)، وبهذا اللفظ رواه الطبراني مرفوعاً ، فهذا بعمومه يوجب سقوط ما استحق عليه قبل الإسلام، بل هو المراد منه بخصوصه، فإنّه محلّ الفائدة ؛ إذ عدم الجزية على المسلم ابتداء من ضروريات الدين.

والسر فيه : إنّ الجزية عقوبة على الكفر، والعقوبة على الكفر تسقط بالإسلام، ولا تقام بعد الموت ؛ كيف لا ؟ فإنّ شرع العقوبة في الدنيا ليس إلا لدفع الشر، وقد اندفع بالموت والإسلام فلا يبقى وجوبها عليه. كذا في فتح القدير»، و«الهداية»).
And it falls with death and Islam, contrary to al-Shafi'i, for him in both. (And they merge). He said: 'in both'; because if a dhimmi becomes Muslim or dies before paying jizya, it does not fall according to him (al-Shafi'i); because it was obligated as a substitute for the protection of blood or residence, and the compensated thing has reached him, so the compensation does not fall, rather it is taken from him even if he becomes Muslim, and from his property if he dies; just as if a dhimmi rented a house then became Muslim or died, the rent for the house does not fall due to the receipt of the compensated thing, which is the benefits of the house. And our proof: the hadith: 'There is no jizya upon a Muslim,' narrated by Abu Dawood, and Sufyan al-Thawri was asked about its meaning and said: 'Meaning, if he becomes Muslim, then there is no jizya upon him,' and with this wording al-Tabarani narrated it as marfu'. This, by its generality, necessitates the fall of what was owed before Islam; indeed it is intended specifically, for that is the place of benefit; since the absence of jizya upon a Muslim initially is from the necessities of the religion. And the secret in it: jizya is a punishment for disbelief, and the punishment for disbelief falls with Islam, and is not carried out after death; how could it not? For the legislation of punishment in this world is only to repel evil, and evil has been repelled by death and Islam, so its obligation does not remain. Thus in Fath al-Qadir and al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and al-Shafi'i regarding whether jizya falls (becomes unowed) upon the death or Islam of a dhimmi before payment. The Hanafi position, supported by the hadith 'There is no jizya upon a Muslim' and the rationale that jizya is a punishment for kufr, holds that the obligation drops entirely. Al-Shafi'i argues by analogy to rent: jizya is a substitute (badal) for the protection of blood and residence, so once the benefit is received, the consideration remains due. The Hanafi response distinguishes jizya as a punitive measure, which ceases when the cause (kufr) ends. The author follows the order of the matn, first quoting the ruling, then explaining the disagreement, then presenting the Hanafi proof and reasoning.

Key Terms
جزية (jizya): A poll tax imposed on non-Muslim subjects (dhimmis) in an Islamic state, in exchange for protection and exemption from military service.
عقوبة (uquba): A legal punishment; here, the author describes jizya as a punitive measure for disbelief in this world.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the Hanafi school, if a dhimmi becomes Muslim before paying the annual jizya, what happens to the jizya obligation?
A) It must still be paid because it was owed for past protection.
B) It falls (is waived) because jizya is a punishment for kufr that ends with Islam.
C) It is converted into a charitable donation.
D) It is deferred until the next year.

Key Takeaway
Jizya falls upon death or Islam because it is a punishment for kufr, not a compensation for protection.

Tadakhul al-Jizya: Do Multiple Years Merge?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tadakhul of jizya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's position on tadakhul of jizya, identify the Sahibayn's opposing view, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf in collecting overdue jizya.

Core Idea
Jizya is a punishment, so years merge; Sahibayn say compensation like kharaj—only one payment due.

This lesson explores a key intra-Hanafi dispute over whether unpaid jizya from multiple years combines into a single payment when finally collected. Abu Hanifa held that jizya is a punishment (uquba) and thus merges, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, considered it a compensation (iwad) akin to kharaj, arguing each year's jizya remains due. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi fiqh in real cases of overdue jizya.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Recognize that the passage addresses tadakhul (merging) of jizya obligations across multiple years. The core question: when a dhimmi fails to pay jizya for several years, how many payments are due upon collection?
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and dalil
Abu Hanifa holds that only one jizya is due regardless of the number of missed years. His reasoning: jizya is a punishment (uquba) for persistence in kufr, and punishments merge when multiple instances occur, just as hadd penalties merge.
Key evidence: the dhimmi must bring jizya personally, standing humbly—showing punitive nature.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that jizya does not merge; each year's payment remains due. They analogize jizya to kharaj, which is a compensation (iwad) for benefiting from protected status. Compensations accumulate when possible to collect.
They point to the fact that kharaj is collected year after year without merging.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa counters that jizya is fundamentally different from kharaj: it is a punishment both initially and perpetually, not a compensation. The manner of its collection (personal, humbling) confirms its punitive character. Hence the analogy to kharaj fails.
This is a ta'lil (reasoning) based on the nature (haqiqah) of jizya.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
The practical consequence: if jizya is unpaid for two years, Abu Hanifa takes only one year's payment; Sahibayn take both. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's view, as supported by al-Hidayah and al-Inayah.
This ruling applies when collecting overdue jizya from a dhimmi who has not converted.

قوله : وتتداخل ؛ يعني من لم تؤخذ منه الجزية في سنة حتى جاءت سنة أخرى لم تؤخذ منه إلا جزية واحدة عنده، خلافاً لهما، وعلى هذا الخلاف خراج الأرض. وقيل : لا تداخل فيه اتفاقاً ؛ لأنّ خراج الأرض في حالة البقاء لا يلتفت فيه إلى جهة العقوبة ؛ ولهذا يجب على المسلم إذا اشترى أرضاً خراجية فجاز أن لا يتداخل بالتكرار، هذا عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لهما. بخلاف الجزية فإنّها عقوبة ابتداء وبقاء، كذا في «العناية»).

[۱] قوله : بالتكرار ؛ اختلفت في أنّ التكرر يحصل بمجيء سنة أخرى أو بمضيها، قال في «الهداية ) : الأصح أنّ الوجوب عندنا في ابتداء الحول، وعند الشافعي له في آخره اعتبارا بالزكاة. ولنا : إنّ ما وجب بدلاً عنه لا يتحقق إلا في المستقبل، فتعذر إيجابه بعد مضي الحول فأَوْجَبناها في أوله.

(۲) قوله : خلافا لهما ؛ بناءً على أنّ الخراج يجب عوضاً، والأعواض إذا اجتمعت وأمكن استيفاؤها تستوفى، وقد أمكن هاهنا استيفاؤها بعد توالي السنين، بخلاف ما أسلم فإنه يتعذر حينئذ استيفاؤه ؛ لعدم وجوب الجزية على مسلم. وله : أنّ الجزية وجبت عقوبة على الإصرار على الكفر، ولهذا لا تقبل منه لو بعث بالجزية على يد نائبه في أصح الروايات، بل يكلّف أن يأتي بها بنفسه، فيعطى قائماً، والقابض منه قاعد والعقوبات إذا اجتمعت تداخلت كالحدود، كذا في «الهداية»(۳).
His saying: 'and they merge'; meaning, whoever has not had jizya taken from him in one year until another year comes, only one jizya is taken from him according to him (Abu Hanifa), contrary to those two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And upon this disagreement is the kharaj of land. And it is said: there is no disagreement on its merging; because kharaj of land in the state of perpetuity does not pay attention to the aspect of punishment; therefore it is obligatory upon a Muslim if he buys kharaj land, that it is permissible that it does not merge with repetition. This is according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to those two. Unlike jizya, for it is a punishment initially and perpetually. Thus in al-Inayah. [1] His saying: 'with repetition'; they differed about whether repetition occurs by the arrival of another year or by its passing. He said in al-Hidayah: The correct view is that obligation according to us is at the beginning of the year, and according to al-Shafi'i at its end, by analogy with zakat. And our proof: that what is obligated as a substitute is only realized in the future, so its imposition after the passing of the year is impossible, so we impose it at its beginning. (2) His saying: 'contrary to those two'; based on that kharaj is obligatory as compensation, and compensations when they aggregate and it is possible to collect them, they are collected, and here it is possible to collect them after successive years, unlike one who becomes Muslim, for then collection becomes impossible due to the absence of jizya on a Muslim. And his (Abu Hanifa's) argument: that jizya was obligated as a punishment for persistence in kufr, and therefore it is not accepted from him if he sends jizya through a deputy in the most authentic narrations, rather he is ordered to bring it himself, giving it standing, with the receiver sitting, and punishments when they aggregate merge like the hadd penalties. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage addresses the intra-Hanafi dispute on tadakhul (merging) of jizya payments across multiple years. According to Abu Hanifa, if a dhimmi fails to pay jizya for several years, only one payment is due when finally collected. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani disagree, arguing by analogy to kharaj (land tax) that jizya is a compensation that accumulates. Abu Hanifa counters that jizya is fundamentally a punishment (uquba) for persistence in kufr, and punishments merge when multiple instances occur, just as hadd penalties merge. The text also discusses the timing of obligation: Abu Hanifa holds it begins at the start of the year (unlike Shafi'i who ties it to the end), and explains why. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's.

Key Terms
تداخل (tadakhul): Merging or overlap; here, the rule that multiple obligations of the same type (jizya) combine into one.
خراج (kharaj): Land tax imposed on agricultural land in Islamic territories, considered a compensation (iwad) for use of land.
جزية (jizya): Poll tax imposed on non-Muslim subjects (dhimmis) in an Islamic state, in exchange for protection and exemption from military service.
عقوبة (uquba): Punishment; here referred to as the nature of jizya according to Abu Hanifa.
عوض (iwad): Compensation; here referred to as the nature of kharaj and, for Sahibayn, jizya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a dhimmi fails to pay jizya for two consecutive years, how much is collected when he finally pays?
A) Two years' jizya in full.
B) Only one year's jizya, because punishments merge.
C) A fine equal to one year's jizya.
D) Nothing, as jizya is waived due to delay.

Key Takeaway
Jizya is a punishment; therefore, multiple years' unpaid jizya merge into a single payment, unlike compensations like kharaj.

Prohibition of Establishing New Churches and Moving Them
Ruling that no new churches may be established; moving a church counts as new establishment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that prohibits new churches and apply the rule that moving a church constitutes new establishment, citing the hadith proof and the qiyas extension.

Core Idea
Establishing new churches in Islamic lands is prohibited; moving a church counts as new establishment.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the matn's phrase 'wa la tuhdath' (and do not establish). The commentator clarifies that this is a prohibition against dhimmis building new places of worship. Importantly, moving a church to a new location is included because it involves new construction. The proof is the hadith 'No castration in Islam nor church.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited act and ruling
Determine what exactly is prohibited. The text forbids 'ihdath' (establishing) of churches. Also moving a church from one place to another is included. The ruling is haram.
The prohibition applies to dhimmis; Muslims are not addressed here.
2
Examine the grammatical form
The phrase 'wa la tuhdath' is analyzed. It is in the passive feminine form, or active masculine. In either case, it functions as a prohibition (nahy). This is an indicative statement with the force of prohibition.
Understanding grammatical forms helps confirm the legal intent.
3
Trace the dalil (proof)
The hadith 'La khisa' fi al-Islam wa la kanisah' is cited. It explicitly negates the existence of churches in Islam, interpreted as a ban on new ones. The hadith is narrated by al-Bayhaqi and Abu Ubayd.
The hadith is ahad, so its thubut is zanni, but its meaning (dalala) is considered qati' in Hanafi usul.
4
Understand the qiyas extension
Moving a church to a new location is equated to establishing a new church because it requires construction in a new place. This is qiyas based on the common illa (effective cause) of 'new construction'.
This is a key point: relocation is not mere transfer but involves rebuilding, thus it falls under the prohibition.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Example: A Christian community in a Hanafi city wants to move their church to a different neighborhood. According to this ruling, they are prohibited from doing so because it constitutes ihdath (new establishment).
Repairing an existing church on the same site may be permissible, but moving it is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣] قوله : ولا تُحدث ؛ بصيغة المجهول المؤنّث، ونائب فاعلـه مـا بعده، أو هو بصيغة الغائب المعروف المذكر، وفاعله الضمير الراجع إلى الكافر، فما بعده مفعول، وعلى كل تقدير، فهو خبر بمعنى النّهي ، ويدخل فيه نقل كنيسة من موضع إلى موضع ؛ فإنه إحداث من وجه، فيمنعون عنه.

In any case, it is an indicative statement meaning prohibition. And included in it is moving a church from one place to another, for that is establishing in some respect, so they are prevented from it. And the basis for it is the hadith: 'No castration in Islam nor church,' narrated by al-Bayhaqi and Abu Ubayd. Thus in al-Binaya.

Commentary
This passage explains the matn's phrase 'wa la tuhdath' (and do not establish), which prohibits dhimmis from building new churches or places of worship. The author clarifies the grammatical form and notes that it functions as a prohibition. Importantly, moving a church from one location to another is included in the prohibition because it constitutes a form of new establishment. The proof is the hadith 'La khisa' fi al-Islam wa la kanisah' (No castration in Islam nor church), recorded by al-Bayhaqi and Abu Ubayd. This ruling is part of the regulations governing non-Muslims in Islamic territory, aimed at preserving the Islamic character of the land.

Key Terms
إحداث (ihdath): Establishing or bringing into existence something new; here, building a new church or moving one to a new location.
كنيسة (kanisah): Church or place of worship for Christians.

A) Yes, as long as the same materials are used.
B) No, because it is considered establishing a new church.
C) Yes, with the permission of the Muslim ruler.
D) No, because churches are not allowed at all in Islamic cities.

Key Takeaway
Under Hanafi law, dhimmis may not establish new churches or relocate existing ones, as relocation constitutes new establishment.

Jizyah is Not Repeated: Abu Hanifa's Position Contrary to the Two Companions
Explaining that jizyah does not repeat yearly and the intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa holds that jizyah does not repeat yearly and evaluate the objection of the Sahibayn using the principle of tadakhul al-uqubat.

Core Idea
Jizyah is a punishment, not a compensation — punishments overlap, so it is not repeated yearly.

The obligation of jizyah on dhimmis is settled, but does it recur every year? This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The answer hinges on the legal nature of jizyah: is it a punishment (uqubah) or a compensation (iwad)?

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling that jizyah is obligated once at the beginning of the year and does not repeat with each subsequent year.
2
Identify the opposing view
Recognize that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) contend jizyah repeats yearly, analogous to kharaj.
The opposing school here is within the Hanafi school itself.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Sahibayn's reasoning: jizyah is like kharaj, a compensation that accumulates and can be collected over successive years.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand Abu Hanifa's argument: jizyah is a punishment (uqubah) for persisting in kufr, and punishments overlap (tadakhul), so no repetition.
Evidence includes the dhimmi's obligation to bring jizyah standing, indicating humiliation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Note the practical consequence: if a dhimmi converts during the year, the Sahibayn would require jizyah for the elapsed period, but Abu Hanifa would not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالتكرار، ولا تُحدَث بالتكرا) ، هذا عند أبي حنيفة له خلافاً لهما".
( ولا تُحدث بخلاف الجزية فإنّها عقوبة ابتداء وبقاء، كذا في «العناية»).
[۱] قوله : بالتكرار ؛ اختلفت في أنّ التكرر يحصل بمجيء سنة أخرى أو بمضيها، قال في «الهداية ) : الأصح أنّ الوجوب عندنا في ابتداء الحول، وعند الشافعي له في آخره اعتبارا بالزكاة.
ولنا : إنّ ما وجب بدلاً عنه لا يتحقق إلا في المستقبل، فتعذر إيجابه بعد مضي الحول فأَوْجَبناها في أوله.
(۲) قوله : خلافا لهما ؛ بناءً على أنّ الخراج يجب عوضاً، والأعواض إذا اجتمعت وأمكن استيفاؤها تستوفى، وقد أمكن هاهنا استيفاؤها بعد توالي السنين، بخلاف ما أسلم فإنه يتعذر حينئذ استيفاؤه ؛ لعدم وجوب الجزية على مسلم. وله : أنّ الجزية وجبت عقوبة على الإصرار على الكفر، ولهذا لا تقبل منه لو بعث بالجزية على يد نائبه في أصح الروايات، بل يكلّف أن يأتي بها بنفسه، فيعطى قائماً، والقابض منه قاعد والعقوبات إذا اجتمعت تداخلت كالحدود، كذا في «الهداية»(۳).

(And no new church is established) contrary to jizyah, for it is a punishment initially and ongoing, as in al-‘Inayah. [1] His statement: 'by repetition' – there is disagreement about whether repetition occurs by the arrival of another year or by its passing. He said in al-Hidayah: The correct view is that the obligation according to us is at the beginning of the year, and according to al-Shafi‘i at its end, by analogy to zakat. Our proof: What is obligated as a substitute is not realized except in the future, so making it obligatory after the passing of the year is impossible; thus we make it obligatory at its beginning. (2) His statement: 'contrary to the two companions' – based on the fact that kharaj is obligatory as a compensation, and compensations when they accumulate and it is possible to collect them are collected, and it is possible here to collect them after successive years, unlike one who becomes a Muslim, for then collection is impossible due to no jizyah being obligatory on a Muslim. And his proof: Jizyah is obligated as a punishment for persisting in disbelief, and therefore it is not accepted from him if he sends it via a deputy according to the most authentic narrations; rather, he is required to bring it himself, giving it standing while the receiver sits. And when punishments accumulate, they overlap like fixed punishments, as in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the repetition (takarrur) of jizyah. The base text states that jizyah is not repeated, and a new church is not established by repetition. The commentator first clarifies that the ruling on jizyah not being repeated is Abu Hanifa's position, opposed by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). Then, the commentary explains the term 'by repetition': there is a difference of opinion on whether repetition occurs by the arrival of a new year or by its passing. Al-Hidayah states that the correct Hanafi view is that the obligation of jizyah is at the beginning of the year, contrary to al-Shafi‘i who places it at the end, analogizing to zakat. The Hanafi proof is that jizyah is a substitute for protection (badal ‘an al-dhimma), and a substitute can only be realized in the future, so making it obligatory after the year passes is impossible; thus, it is obligated at the beginning. Then the text explains the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn: the Sahibayn analogize jizyah to kharaj, which is a compensation (iwad) that accumulates and can be collected over successive years, while Abu Hanifa argues that jizyah is a punishment (uqubah) for persisting in kufr. Since punishments overlap (tadakhul) – like fixed punishments (hudud) – jizyah does not repeat. This is supported by the ruling that a dhimmi must personally bring jizyah standing, showing it is a humiliation and punishment. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's view that jizyah is not repeated, as it is the mufta bihi.

Key Terms
التكرار (al-takarrur): Repetition of the obligation of jizyah with each passing year.
الجزية (al-jizyah): A poll tax imposed on non-Muslim subjects (dhimmis) as a punishment for persisting in disbelief and in return for protection.
العقوبة (al-uqubah): A punitive payment that humiliates the payer, thus it overlaps like hudud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does jizyah not repeat with each year?
A) Because it is a punishment and punishments overlap.
B) Because it is a compensation and compensations do not accumulate.
C) Because it is only paid once in a lifetime.
D) Because the year is counted from the time of the contract.

Key Takeaway
Jizyah does not repeat yearly according to Abu Hanifa, and the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school follows him.

Prohibition of Building New Churches and Distinguishing Dhimmis in Dress and Ride
Ruling with proof on church establishment and dhimmi distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on establishing new non-Muslim places of worship in Dar al-Islam and the conditions for distinguishing dhimmis in dress and ride, citing the primary proof.

Core Idea
A dhimmi may not build a new church in Dar al-Islam; prohibition is absolute, but rebuilding a destroyed one is allowed under strict conditions.

This lesson examines two related rulings from the Hanafi school regarding dhimmis in an Islamic state: the prohibition of establishing new places of worship, and the obligation to distinguish them from Muslims in dress and mode of transport. The primary evidence is a hadith, and the commentary clarifies the scope of the prohibition, including the nuance of rebuilding vs. new construction, and the specific restrictions on attire and riding. Understanding these rules is essential for grasping the classical Hanafi framework of dhimma rights and public order.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the exact act being ruled upon (establishing a new church or synagogue) and classify its hukm (haram). Note that the text treats the statement as a prohibition.
The phrasing 'wa la tuhdath' is interpreted as a prohibition.
2
Locate the primary proof
Find the hadith cited as the basis: 'La khusa'a fi al-Islam wa la kanisah' (no castration in Islam and no church). Understand its transmission status (ahad) and how it proves the ruling.
The hadith is from Sunan al-Bayhaqi and Kitab al-Amwal; its general negation indicates prohibition.
3
Analyze the scope of the prohibition
Explore what is included under 'establishment' (moving a church, fire temples, idol houses) and where it applies (Dar al-Islam, major cities by consensus, non-Arab villages with ikhtilaf, mufta bihi is absolute).
The text mentions that prohibition applies in major cities and Arab villages by consensus; in non-Arab villages there is a minority view allowing it, but the chosen position is absolute prohibition.
4
Apply conditions for rebuilding destroyed places
Understand that rebuilding a destroyed church is allowed only if it is exactly as before (no expansion or improvement), and note the difference if destruction was by the Imam (not allowed) or by themselves (allowed).
The text permits rebuilding but not enhancing; if the Imam demolishes, they lose the right to rebuild.
5
Identify the distinguishing rules for dhimmis
Extract the rulings on dress (prevented from wearing turbans and scholar's garments) and ride (should not ride horses; if riding a donkey out of necessity, dismount in crowded places; also ride in a distinctive manner like sitting sideways).
The obligation to distinguish is derived from the passive verb 'muyyiz', indicating a required differentiation.

His statement: 'and no new church is established' - in the feminine passive voice, its agent is what follows, or it is in the masculine active voice with the pronoun referring back to the disbeliever, and what follows is the object. In any case, it is a statement meaning prohibition. Included in this is moving a church from one place to another, for it is a kind of establishment, so they are prevented from it. The basis for it is the hadith: 'There is no castration in Islam and no church' narrated by al-Bayhaqi and Abu ‘Ubayd, as in al-Binayah. Book of Jihad / Section on Jizyah. A church and a synagogue here, and for them to rebuild what has been destroyed, and the dhimmi is distinguished from us in his dress and his ride. [1] His statement: 'church' – said in al-Fath: al-bi‘ah (with kasr) and al-kanisah are places of worship of Jews and Christians, then kanisah became predominant for Jews and bi‘ah for Christians specifically. [2] His statement: 'and synagogue' – similarly, they are prevented from establishing a fire temple, an idol house, and the like. [3] His statement: 'here' – meaning in Dar al-Islam, and this is in the major cities by consensus, and likewise in Arab villages. As for villages of non-Arab lands, it is said that they are permitted to establish them there, because the major cities are where the rituals of Islam are established, so opposing appearances are not opposed. The chosen and mufta bihi view is absolute prohibition, as in Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir by al-Sarakhsi, al-Fath, and others. [4] His statement: 'rebuilding the destroyed' – he indicated by this that they may build the destroyed structure as it was without addition. If they intend to build a second one better than the first, they are prevented from that. And if the Imam demolishes it, they are not permitted to rebuild it. But if they themselves demolish it, they may rebuild it, as in Hawashi al-Bahr al-Ra’iq by al-Khayr al-Ramli. [5] His statement: 'and distinguished' – in the passive voice of tamyiz, and it is a statement indicating obligation. [6] His statement: 'in his dress' – al-ziy (with kasr of the zay and tashdid of the ya) means appearance, and what is meant here is clothing, so he is prevented from wearing the turban and the dress of scholars and honorable people. [7] His statement: 'and his ride' – said in al-Ashbah: 'The relied-upon view is that they should not ride at all, and should not wear turbans. If he rides a donkey out of necessity, he dismounts in crowded places.' End. And in al-Nahr: 'The violation of appearance in the ride is only when they ride from one side.' I think I heard this from al-Shaykh al-Akh as well. End. And by al-Shaykh al-Akh is meant the author of al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage is a ruling commentary on the prohibition of establishing new churches and synagogues in Dar al-Islam, and the obligation to distinguish dhimmis in dress and ride. The commentator first explains the grammar of 'wa la tuhdath', showing it is a prohibition. This includes moving a church, as it is a form of establishment. The primary proof is the hadith 'La khusa'a fi al-Islam wa la kanisah' (no castration in Islam and no church), reported by al-Bayhaqi and Abu ‘Ubayd. The commentary then defines 'bi‘ah' and 'kanisah': bi‘ah is specifically for Christians, kanisah for Jews. The prohibition extends to fire temples and idol houses. The phrase 'here' indicates Dar al-Islam. In major cities and Arab villages, the prohibition is by consensus. In non-Arab villages, there is a difference; the mufta bihi is absolute prohibition. Regarding rebuilding destroyed structures: dhimmis may rebuild exactly as before, but not better. If the Imam demolishes it, they cannot rebuild; if they demolish it themselves, they may. Finally, the text explains the ruling on distinguishing dhimmis: they must be distinguished in dress (prevented from wearing turbans and garments of scholars) and in ride (they should not ride horses, only donkeys if necessary, and dismount in crowded places). The relied-upon view is that they should not ride at all, and the violation in ride is when they ride with a distinctive style (e.g., riding from one side).

Key Terms
البيعة (al-bi‘ah): A place of worship specifically for Christians.
الكنيسة (al-kanisah): A place of worship for Jews, but also used generally for non-Muslim places of worship.
الإحداث (al-ihdath): Establishing a new structure; includes building and moving a church.
الزي (al-ziyy): Dress or appearance; here specifically the clothing that distinguishes a dhimmi from a Muslim.
المركب (al-markab): Ride or mount; dhimmis must not ride horses, only donkeys if necessary, and must dismount in crowded places.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a dhimmi in a major city wishes to build a new church, what is the ruling?
A) Permitted with the Imam's permission.
B) Permitted only if no mosque is nearby.
C) Prohibited absolutely.
D) Permitted only in non-Arab villages.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits establishing new non-Muslim places of worship in Dar al-Islam absolutely, and requires dhimmis to be distinguished from Muslims in dress and ride as an obligation.

Ruling on Building and Rebuilding Places of Worship for Dhimmis
Defines prohibition of new churches and rules for rebuilding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on building new churches for dhimmis, distinguish between new construction and rebuilding, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Dhimmis may not build new places of worship in Islamic lands; only exact rebuilding is permitted, and only if not demolished by the Imam.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqāya explains the rules governing non-Muslim places of worship in Dār al-Islam. The matn states a general prohibition on building new churches and synagogues, but allows rebuilding of demolished structures. The commentary expands on the scope of the prohibition, the conditions for rebuilding, and the relied-upon position within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is being ruled upon? Building new places of worship (bīʿa, kanīsa, etc.) for dhimmis. Ruling: prohibited (haram).
The prohibition covers churches, synagogues, fire temples, idol houses.
2
Locate the proof
The commentary cites consensus (ittifāq) in the cities (amsār) and Arab villages as the basis. The proof is ijmaʿ of the Companions and scholars.
The text says 'by consensus in the major cities and Arab villages'.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
A minority view allowed construction in non-Arab villages (qurā bilād ghayr al-ʿarab) because Islamic symbols are less prominent. Identify the opposing view and its reasoning.
This is a legitimate difference within the school.
4
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon (muftā bihī) position is absolute prohibition (manʿ muṭlaqan), as stated in authoritative works like Sharḥ al-Siyar al-Kabīr and Fatḥ al-Qadīr.
The tarjih is based on preserving Islamic symbols and the terms of the dhimma pact.
5
Apply the condition for rebuilding
Rebuilding is allowed only if the structure is restored exactly as it was (no additions or improvements) and if the demolition was not carried out by the Muslim imam.
If the imam ordered the demolition, the right to rebuild is lost. If the dhimmis themselves demolish, they may rebuild.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجهاد / فصل الجزية

بيعة وكنيسة هنا، ولهم إعادة المنهدمة، وميز الذمي منا في زيه ومركبه.

[قوله: بيعة؛ قال في «الفتح»: البيعة بالكسر، والكنيسة متعبد اليهود والنصارى، ثمّ غلب الكنيسة لمتعبّد اليهود، والبيعة لمتعبد النّصارى خاصة. قوله: وكنيسة؛ وكذا يمنعون من إحداث بيت نار وبيت صنم، ونحو ذلك. قوله: هنا؛ أي في دار الإسلام وهذا في الأمصار بالاتفاق، وكذا في قرى العرب، وأما في قرى بلاد غير العرب فقيل: يجوز لهم فيها إحداثها؛ لأنّ الأمصار هي التي تقام شعائر الإسلام فيها، فلا تعارض بإظهار ما يخالفها، والمختار المفتى به هو المنع مطلقا، كما في «شرح السير الكبير للسرخسي له، و«الفتح»، وغيرهما. قوله: إعادة المنهدمة؛ أشار بهذا إلى أن لهم بناء المنهدم كما كان من غير زيادة عليه، بل لو قصدوا أن يبنوا ثانياً أحسن من البناء الأول منعوا من ذلك، وإلى أنه لو هدمها الإمام لم تجز لهم إعادتها، وأما إذا هدموها بأنفسهم فلهم الإعادة، كذا في «حواشي البحر الرائق» للخير الرملي.]
Book of Jihad, Section on Jizyah: 'A church and synagogue here, and they may rebuild what is demolished, and the dhimmi is distinguished from us in his dress and mount.' [Commentary: 'Church' (bīʿa) – in al-Fatḥ: bīʿa is for Christians, kanīsa is for Jews, but later kanīsa became common for Jews and bīʿa for Christians. 'And synagogue' – likewise they are prevented from building fire temples, idol houses, etc. 'Here' – i.e., in Dār al-Islam, and this is by consensus in the major cities and Arab villages. As for non-Arab villages, it was said that they may build there because Islamic rituals are not prominent, but the chosen and fatwa-worthy position is absolute prohibition, as in Sharḥ al-Siyar al-Kabīr, al-Fatḥ, etc. 'Rebuilding what is demolished' – this indicates they may rebuild it as it was without addition, and if they intend to build it better, they are prevented. If the imam demolishes it, they may not rebuild; if they demolish it themselves, they may rebuild, as in Ḥawāshī al-Baḥr al-Rā’iq by al-Khayr al-Ramlī.]
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqāya explains the rules regarding dhimmis’ places of worship in Islamic lands. The matn states the general rule: dhimmis are not permitted to build new churches, synagogues, fire temples, or idol houses—this is the position by consensus in the cities and Arab villages. However, a minority view within the Hanafi school allowed construction in non-Arab villages on the grounds that Islamic symbols are less prominent there, but the relied-upon (muftā bihī) opinion is absolute prohibition, as recorded in authoritative works like Sharḥ al-Siyar al-Kabīr and Fatḥ al-Qadīr. As for a building that falls into disrepair or is demolished: dhimmis may rebuild it exactly as it was, with no additions or improvements. If they try to make it nicer, they are stopped. A key distinction: if the Muslim imam orders its demolition (e.g., for public interest or punishment), they lose the right to rebuild. But if they themselves demolish it (e.g., to renovate), they may rebuild. This is from the commentary of al-Khayr al-Ramlī on al-Baḥr al-Rā’iq.

Key Terms
بيعة (bīʿa): A church for Christians; specifically the place of worship for Christians.
كنيسة (kanīsa): A synagogue for Jews; originally a general term for places of worship for Jews and Christians, but later specified for Jews.
إعادة المنهدمة (iʿādat al-munhadima): Rebuilding a place of worship that has fallen into ruin or been demolished.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only in the major cities (amsar)
B) In both cities and villages, including non-Arab villages
C) Only in Arab villages
D) Everywhere except non-Arab villages

Key Takeaway
New non-Muslim places of worship are absolutely prohibited in Islamic lands; rebuilding is only allowed if the original structure is restored exactly, and only if the imam did not order its demolition.

Ruling on Distinguishing Dhimmis in Dress, Mount, and Weapons
Specifies required distinctions in clothing, riding, weapons, and women's conduct for dhimmis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four areas of distinction required for dhimmis and apply the relied-upon Hanafi positions to identify permissible exceptions.

Core Idea
Dhimmis must be distinctly humble in dress, ride, weapon, and women's conduct to preserve Islamic honor.

This lesson examines the Hanafi obligation to distinguish non-Muslim citizens (dhimmis) from Muslims in external appearance and behavior. The rulings derive from the pact of dhimma, which requires humility and prevents any resemblance that might compromise Islamic honor. We will explore the specific requirements for dress, riding, weapons, and women's conduct, noting differences among early and later Hanafi authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal basis for distinction
The phrase 'muyyiza' (he is distinguished) is treated as an imperative (amr) indicating obligation (wujūb). Understand that this obligation falls upon the Muslim ruler to enforce, and upon dhimmis to comply.
The matn uses a passive form to denote obligation, a common Hanafi usul method.
2
List the four areas of distinction
From the text, extract: (1) dress (zī) – no turban or noble garments; (2) mount (markab) – no horses, saddle must be like an ikkāf; (3) weapons (silāḥ) – no carrying or using; (4) women's conduct – walk on side of road, not middle.
Each area aims to prevent dhimmis from resembling Muslims in honor or status.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on riding
Earlier scholars (mutaqaddimūn) allowed riding mules or donkeys, but later scholars (mutaʾakhkhirūn) prohibited all riding except for necessity. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) is the stricter view.
This shift reflects a broader trend in later Hanafi fatwa toward greater precaution.
4
Apply the mufta bihi to a scenario
Given a dhimmi who needs to travel a long distance, the later position allows riding a donkey only if necessary, and he must dismount in crowded places. He may not ride a horse under any circumstance.
Necessity is narrowly defined; convenience is not sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وميز؛ بصيغة المجهول من التمييز، وهو خبر مفيد لمعنى الوجوب. قوله: في زيه؛ الزي بكسر الزاي المعجمة، وتشديد الياء المثناة التحتية الهيئة، والمراد به اللباس، فيمنع من لبس العمامة، ولباس أهل العلم والشرف. قوله: ومركبه؛ قال «الأشباه»: «المعتمد أن لا يركبوا مطلقاً، ولا يلبسوا العمائم، وإن ركب الحمار لضرورة نزل في المجامع». انتهى وفي «النهر) : مخالفة الهيئة في المركوب إنّما تكون إذا ركبوا من جانب واحد ، وغالب ظني أني سمعته من الشيخ الأخ كذلك. انتهى. والمراد بالشيخ الأخ : هو صاحب «البحر».

وسرجه، وسلاحه، فلا يركب خيلاً، ولا يعمل بسلاح، ويُظهِرُ الكستيج، ويَرْكَبُ على سرج كإكاف، وميزت نساؤهم في الطريق، والحمام.

قوله: وسرجه؛ فلا يكون سرجه على المركب، كسرج المسلمين. قوله: وسلاحه؛ تمییزه بسلاح بأن لا يعمل بسلاح كما ذكره المصنف له، فإن استعان بهم الإمام جعل سلاحهم مميزاً من سلاح المسلمين. قوله: خيلاً؛ قال في «الذخيرة» : إلا إذا استعان بهم الإمام لمحاربة وذب عنا. وفي «التاتارخانية»: جاز بغل كحمار. وفي «الفتح» : هذا عند المتقدمين، واختار المتأخرون أن لا يركب أصلاً إلا لضرورة. قوله: ولا يعمل؛ أي لا يستعمل السلاح، ولا يحمله؛ لأنه من باب العزّة، وكل ما كان من هذا الباب يمنعون عنه، هذا هو الأصل. قوله: الكُستيج؛ هو - بضم الكاف، وسكون السين المهملة، وكسر التاء المثناة الفوقية، بعدها ياء مثناة تحتية ساكنة، بعدها جيم - ، وقد فسره الشارح بما فسّر. وذكر في «النهر»: إنّه فارسي معرب معناه العجز والذل. وفي «البحر»: كستيجات النّصارى قلنسوة سوداء، مضربة وزُنّار من الصوف. قوله: وميزت... الخ؛ يعني تمييز نساء الكفّار بما يحصل الامتياز به بينهن وبين نساء المسلمين، بأن تمشي في ناحية الطريق لا في وسطه.
His saying: 'And is distinguished' (muyyiza) is in the passive form, meaning obligation. His saying: 'in his dress' (zīhī) – dress is appearance, meaning clothing, so he is prevented from wearing the turban and the clothing of scholars and nobles. His saying: 'and his mount' (markabihī) – al-Ashbāh says: 'The relied-upon is that they should not ride at all, nor wear turbans. If he rides a donkey out of necessity, he must dismount in crowded places.' And in al-Nahr: 'The difference in the mount is only when they ride on one side.' And I think I heard that from al-Shaykh al-Akh. And the meaning of al-Shaykh al-Akh is the author of al-Bahr.

'And his saddle, and his weapon, so he may not ride horses, nor work with weapons, and he must display the kustīj, and ride on a saddle like an ikkāf, and their women are distinguished in the road and the bath.'

His saying: 'and his saddle' – his saddle should not be like the saddle of Muslims on the mount. His saying: 'and his weapon' – his distinction by weapon is that he does not work with weapons, as the author mentioned. If the imam seeks their help, their weapons are made distinct from Muslim weapons. His saying: 'horses' – al-Dhakhīra says: 'Except if the imam seeks their help for warfare and defense.' In al-Tātārkhāniyya: 'A mule is permissible like a donkey.' In al-Fatḥ: 'This is from the earlier scholars; the later scholars chose that he should not ride at all except for necessity.' His saying: 'and does not work' – i.e., he does not use weapons, nor carry them, because it is a matter of honor, and everything of that sort they are prevented from, this is the principle. His saying: 'al-kustīj' – with damm on the kāf, sukūn on the sīn, kasr on the tā, then yā sākina, then jīm; the commentator explained it as such. In al-Nahr: it is a Persian arabicized word meaning weakness and humility. In al-Bahr: the kustīj of Christians is a black cap and a woolen zunnār. His saying: 'and their women are distinguished' – meaning distinction of the women of the disbelievers so that there is distinction between them and Muslim women, by walking on the side of the road, not in the middle.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage details the obligation (wujūb) of distinguishing dhimmis from Muslims in external appearance, as a mark of humility and preservation of Islamic honor. The commentary explains the matn's phrase 'muyyiza' (is distinguished) as an imperative requiring enforcement. 

1. Dress (zī): Dhimmis are forbidden from wearing the turban (ʿimāma) or any garment associated with scholars or nobility. They must wear coarse, simple clothing—typically a rough cotton shirt with a chest opening like women's attire, as noted in al-Durr al-Muntaqā.

2. Mount (markab): The relied-upon position (from al-Ashbāh) is that dhimmis should not ride at all. If riding a donkey is necessary, they must dismount in crowded places. The saddle (sarj) must differ from Muslim saddles; they may only use a packsaddle-like saddle (ikkāf). Additionally, they are prohibited from riding horses. Some earlier scholars allowed riding mules or donkeys, but later scholars restricted riding to dire necessity only, as in Fatḥ al-Qadīr.

3. Weapons (silāḥ): Dhimmis may not use or carry weapons, as this is a sign of honor and empowerment. If the imam recruits them for military assistance, their weapons must be distinctly marked to avoid confusion with Muslim weapons.

4. The kustīj: A distinctive mark—a thick woolen cord tied around the waist (or a black cap and woolen belt according to some reports). This symbolizes humility and subordination. It is distinct from the zunnār (usually a silk belt worn by non-Muslims in some regions).

5. Women of dhimmis: They must also be distinguished. The text says they should walk on the side of the road, not in the middle, and presumably wear distinctive clothing (though not detailed here).

Key Terms
زي (zī): Appearance, specifically clothing; dhimmis are required to wear distinct, humble attire.
كستيج (kustīj): A thick woolen cord worn around the waist by dhimmis as a mark of distinction and humility.
إكاف (ikkāf): A packsaddle used for beasts of burden, distinct from the raised saddles of Muslims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the rule regarding dhimmis riding horses?
A) They may ride horses but not with a saddle.
B) They may ride horses only for necessity.
C) They are absolutely forbidden from riding horses.
D) They may ride horses if they wear the kustīj.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school obligates clear distinction of dhimmis in dress, mount, weapons, and women's conduct, with the later relied-upon view prohibiting riding entirely except for desperate need.

Signs of Humiliation for Dhimmis (Ahl al-Dhimmah)
Lists required markers of dhimmi humiliation in public

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five signs of dhimmi humiliation according to the Hanafi school and explain their legal status as wajib conditions of the dhimma contract.

Core Idea
Dhimmis must visibly distinguish themselves—kustij, pack-saddle, no weapons, women distinct, houses marked—as a contractual obligation.

This passage enumerates the practical signs of humiliation (dhillah) that the Hanafi school requires of dhimmis (ahl al-dhimmah) in public life. These rules are part of the covenant of protection (ʿahd al-dhimmah), which grants dhimmis security and religious freedom in exchange for jizya and acceptance of a subordinate status. Understanding these signs is essential for grasping the classical Hanafi framework of interfaith relations under Islamic rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify each of the five signs from the passage
Read the Arabic carefully: 'وَيُظهِرُ الكستيج، ويَرْكَبُ على سرج كإكاف، ولا يركب خيلاً، ولا يعمل بسلاح، ومُيُزَتْ نساؤهم، ويُعلم على دورهم'. Extract each distinct requirement.
The signs are: (1) display the kustij, (2) ride on a pack-saddle, (3) do not ride horses (with Muslim saddles), (4) do not bear arms, (5) women are distinguished, (6) houses are marked.
2
Understand the legal classification
These are wajib conditions of the dhimma contract. They are binding on dhimmis; the state enforces them to maintain social distinction. Violation can lead to nullification of the covenant.
Contrast with sunnah or mubah; this is a contractual obligation.
3
Connect each sign to its purpose
Each rule prevents tashabbuh (resemblance) with Muslims. For example, the saddle must be like a pack-saddle, not a Muslim saddle, to avoid equality in riding. The kustij distinguishes attire.
The mark on houses serves to prevent Muslims from seeking forgiveness for the dead dhimmi.
4
Memorize the key Arabic terms
Learn: الكستيج (kustij), سرج كإكاف (pack-saddle), مُيُزَت (distinguished). These technical terms appear in Hanafi fiqh texts on jizya.
5
Situate in the dhimma framework
These signs are part of the 'dhillah' clause of the dhimma contract. They are not punishments but symbols of the agreed subordinate status. Recognize them as conditions, not arbitrary humiliations.
Refer to the chapter on jizya in al-Mabsut or al-Hidayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسرجه، وسلاحه، فلا يركب خيلاً، ولا يعمل بسلاح، ويُظهِرُ الكستيج، ويَرْكَبُ على سرج كإكاف، ومُيُزَتْ نساؤهم في الطريق، والحمام، ويُعلم على دورهم؛ لئلا يستغفر لهم.
And his saddle, and his weapon: so he does not ride a horse, and does not use a weapon, and he displays the kustij, and he rides on a saddle like a pack-saddle, and their women are distinguished in the road and the bathhouse, and a mark is placed on their houses so that no one seeks forgiveness for them.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement enumerating the signs of humiliation (dhillah) that must be observed by dhimmis (non-Muslims living under Islamic protection) to distinguish them from Muslims. These rules are part of the covenant of dhimma (ʿahd al-dhimmah). The text lists: (1) They must not ride horses with Muslim-style saddles; instead, if they ride, they should use a pack-saddle (like an ikaaf). (2) They must not bear arms or use weapons. (3) They must display a kustij—a thick woolen cord tied around the waist, as explained in the commentary, distinguishing it from the silk zunnar. (4) Their women must be distinguished in public roads and bathhouses, presumably by modest dress or walking on the side. (5) Their houses must be marked so that passersby do not pray for forgiveness for them, indicating their non-Muslim status. These measures are meant to uphold the contract of dhimma, which grants protection in exchange for humility and payment of jizya.

Key Terms
الكستيج (al-kustij): A thick woolen cord, about a finger's breadth, tied around the waist, worn by dhimmis as a mark of humiliation.
سرج كإكاف (sarij ka-ikaf): A saddle like a pack-saddle, without the high pommel of a Muslim saddle, to avoid resembling Muslims in riding.
ميزت (muyyizat): They are distinguished: meaning the women of dhimmis must be recognizable from Muslim women in public by dress or behavior.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Wear a black veil
B) Be distinguished in the road and bathhouse
C) Walk only at night
D) Carry a weapon

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires dhimmis to publicly display signs of dhillah as binding conditions of the dhimma contract, distinguishing them from Muslims in dress, riding, arms, and housing.
